Tums           : Twenty years, including five years of grace, at the Bank's standard variable

Pl?e sioda0 GOI recognizes the need to expand groundwater development, in ooth Java and
the Outer Islands, in ameas with no surface water potential using facilities that are
sustainable under beneficiary management. The project would be targeted at
selected areas in: Aceh, Nordt and West Sumatra, and Lampung, East and West
Java, North, Central and South Sulawesi, Lombok and Sumbawa in Nusa
Tena Barat (NTB), West Timor in Nusa Tenggara Timor (NTI). About
25,000 ha would be developed including al activities to bring tubewell irrigation
systems into full opetion. In additon, the project would support agricultural
development activities. Components would include: survey, investigation and
design; construcdon of about 240 deep tubewell systems serving some 6,000 ha;
about 1,900 intermediat technology tubewell systems serving about 18,650 ha;
construction of domesic water supply and houseyard irniption faciities; technical
assistance; and institutional support for detailed design and implementation
supervision. The project would also strengthen the groundwater monitoring
networks and provide tining in groundwater resources evaluation. In addition, a
pilot program would develop some 500 ha using shallow tubewells through the
agnculturl development component for poor farmers unable to afford the
investments themselves.
B fits and Risks:  The proect would gene  substantial incremental benefits from low cost works
to develop areas with known reliable groundwater resources. Direct benefits
would include: increased crop production on at least 25,000 ha presently under
rainfed agriculture; improved performance and sustainability of existing
groundwater irrigation schemes; improved management and administrative
capability by institutions involved; and turnover of groundwater infrastructure to
farmer groups. The project would benefit directly about 50,000 families; promote
furtr development in less developed regions impacting on poverty alleviation;
and contribute to GOrs policy objectives of rice-sufficiency and agricultural
divesification Insdtutional stengthening would contribute to decentralizadon of
technical authorities by increasing the role of provincial agencies in long term
O&M support and groundwater monitoring activities. There would also be
indirect benefits by tacling subsector issues related to: project preparation,
standardizaion of  planning and design; quality control; and environmental, agro-
socil, institutonal issues relatd to small scale irrigation including sustainability,
beneficiay participn, effective O&M and cost recovery. The main project risks
rlate to improing quality control in planning, design and implementation and
lether farmers will utilize fully and sustain groundwater facilities. Other
uncerainties rlate to condnuing management constraints, particularly through the
inital O&M period. To address these risks, the project would cover a six year
pesiodto include mpport of the initial O&M phase of completed tubewell systems.
and design criteria and implementaton processes and procedures would
be standardized for better quality control and easier supervision. The work
program would be subject to annual reviews of performance with regad to: local
funding; institutional coordination; quality control; implementation criteria and
procedures; training; provision for project supervision; and regular monitoring and
evaluation of project perormanc.

I. SECT0- AND PERJECT BACKGROUND
Gener BackoUnd
1.01 The large expansion of irigation infrastructure in Indonesia over the last two decades has
made a major contribution to agricultural production in the country, particularly to the achievement of
self-suffliciency of rice. The total irigated area is now about 5.5 million ha, primarily under surface
water inigation, with groundwater irrigation representing less than 1.0 percent of the command area
served. Most development is concentrated on Java which has a long tradition of irnigated agriculture.
The annual growth rate in irrigated area nationwide was about 1.6 percent per annum during the
period 1969-85. However, coordinated efforts to develop full potential benefits fron government
irrigation schemes have not, until recently, been afforded the importance given to the construction
program.
1.02 The Government of Indonesia (GOI) has been involved in groundwater development for
irrigation since the late 1960s. This catalyzed private sector development of groundwater irrigation
facilities, particularly in Java. Despite considerable proven potential, actual groundwater development
has been slow and this water resource has contributed little to the total irrigated surface (para 1.01).
Existing groundwater irrigation is concentrated mainly on Java, thougn efforts have been made to
expand development to the Outer Islands, under the Irrigation Subsector Project (Loan 2880-IND),
through USAID and ODA activities and, more recently, with Japanese and EEC aid. GOI has
recognized that in many areas, groundwater facilities are too technically complicated and expensive
for construction by individual small scale farmers, or farmer groups. However, such facilities are,
nevertheless, suitable for handover for farner maintenance and management. In addition, GOI has
recognized that groundwater development can be specifilcally targeted at poor farmers in some areas
w3here surface water irrigation development is not feasible.
A2iultural S&=
1.03 Agriculture remains a key sector of the Indonesian economy, contributing about 21 percent of
GDP (iri constant terms) and providing employment to some 55 percent of the labor force. Foreign
exchange earnings from agriculture account for about 26 percent of non-oil exports. Of some 24
million ha under cultivation, about 16 million ha are under annual crops, of which some 5.5 million
ha are irrigated. Except for estate crops, nearly all agricultural production is undertaken by the 18
million smallholders, most of whom farm less than 1.0 ha. Farm incomes are increasingly being
supplemented by off-farm employment, which contributes up to 30 percent of net farm family
incomes. In areas near the larger cities, where there are expanding opportunities, women are playing
an increasingly important role in farming.
1.04 The agricultural sector grew at 3.8 percent per annum between 1969-89, compared with a
population growth of 2.1 percent. Food crops, 1/which account for about 60 percent of agricultural
GDP, grew at 4.2 percent per annum during the same period. Rice, the single largest component of
the food crops subsector, grew at an even faster rate, 4.8 percent per annum, particularly during the
1979-89 period. These growth rates are substantial considering that, in the 1970's, not only was rice
the principal import, but Indonesia was also the world's largest importer of rice. The policy of
achieving self-sufficiency in rice was not only efficient, given Indonesia's comparative advantage in
labor-intensive production, but was equally a policy for alleviating poverty, because rice production
was the main economic activity and income of the rural poor. In areas where soils are a constraint to
rice and other food crop production, tree crops contributed to employment and income generation for
I/ Comprising rice and palawija (secondary) crops such as corn, soybean, casava, groundnuts and sweet potte



-2-
smallholder families. Rural poverty, however, condnues to exist in pockets in Java, and more
rampandy in some of the Eastern Islands.
1.05 The overall impressive performance of the sector is attributed to Indonesia's appropriate
response to the oil boom through a combination of me-coeconomic management and sectoral policies.
These policies included public investments in irrigation, research and extension, widespread
promotion of high-yielding vareties, and subsidized input packages, compensating for adverse terms
of trade for tradeable goods, mainly agricultural. Administered price programs in conjunction with
the trade policy regime, helped achieve food price stability.
1.06 Some shortfalls associated with these policy instruments, which the Government is beginning
to address, are: (a) high budgetary costs (Rp 700 billion for fertilizer subsidy in 1990/91) posing a
fiscal problem; (b) inefficiencies in resource allocadion, e.g., high subsidies esultng in excessive use
of fertilizer and underpricing of resources leading to speculative demand for land and an inefficient
use of water; and (c) a regulatory environmernt leading to some rent seeking and cost inefficiency in
maketing.
kigatio Subseo
1.07  SuJraxjQn. O£the total surface water irigated area of about 5.5 million ha, about 1.1
milion ha are privately managed and some 4.4 - "lion ha are under public sector management.
Public sector irrigation comprises some 2.3 millio.a ala of technical irrigated area, 1.3 million ha of
semi-technical area, and 0.8 million ha of simple irrigated area 1. Most iigation schemes are based
on run-of-the-river supplies, and thus the actual area cultivated in any year may vary considerably,
particulaly in the dry seasons.
1.08  Groundwate Irgao. Proven potential for groundwater irrigadon is presendy estimated at
about 175,000 ha. Most of this potential is located on Java, reflecting the fact that exploration and
development activities have been concentrated on that island. Additional groundwater potential will
doubtlessly be discovered as exploration of groundwater resources proceeds on the Outer Islands.
1.09  Public sector irrigation development using g. )undwater was inidated in East Java in the late
1960s, but proceeded slowly through prolonged periods of exploration and csting in pilot schemes.
The total area irrigated by groundwater under government schemes in Java is now estimated at about
27,000 ha. Public sector groundwater development for irrigation in the Outer Islands has occurred
only in the last seven years. The total groundwater irrigated area in the Outer Islands is now about
3,000 ha, with the largest concentration of some 1,700 ha on Lombok.
1.10 Up to a decade ago, there was little groundwater irrigation development in the private sector in
Indonesia, except through manually operated hand-dug wells and some very minor development
using tubewells on sugar estates. In the early 1980s, the private sector on Java developed a low-cost
technology for constructing shallow tubewells (STW) suitable for installation of centrifugal pumps.
Subsequently, private sector development of groundwater has expanded relatively rapidly on that
island.
1.11  1MgFWn l1=Dditus and nr Support Over the last two decades, Gors expenditures
on irrigation have totalled about Rp 13,000 billion, or more than US$ 7.0 bilion (1988 prices).
About half the funding has been provided through external assistance from multilateral and bilateral
sources. The Bank has provided loans totalling about US$ 2.0 billion (para 2.06). Other important
donor agencies have included ADB, USAID, CIDA, EEC, Japan, Australia, Netherlands, the United
Kingdom and Ily. The coordinaton between the donor agencies has generally been good.

with Govmem control to main canal lee; and   k irriated schemes have few pmanent struc   and ae

1.12  Primary Government funcdons for irrigation development, including goundwater irrigation,
are with sever government agencies (Annex 10). Development planmng and budgeting ae done by
the National Development Planning Agency (BAPPENAS) with provincial planning inputs by the
BAPPEDA in each province. The Ministiy of Public Works (MPW), through its DiMrectorate General
of Water Resources Development (DGWRD), has general responsibility for planning and
development of publicly funded physical infrastructure for irrigation and drainage. Ile Dirctate of
rrigation II (D)OI-Il) within DGWRD is directly responsible for construction of new works for large
ace water schemes and groundwater inigadon development. Within D)OI--, a subdirectorate for
groundwater development, Pengembangan Air Tanah (PAT), manages and provides coordination and
technical backstopping to subproject offices in the provinces, known as P2AT. The long tenn
objective is to integrate these centrally funded subproject offices into the Provincial Water Resources
Development Services (PRIS) of the Provincial Public Works agencies. At present, the P2AT offices
operate primarily under direct technical guidance of DOI-I through PAT, and are responsible for
implementing groundwater irrigation projects through the construction stage. The P2ATs are also
responsible for supporting and supervisi"% groundwater scheme operadons after construction, a role
similar to that exercised by PRIS m publiv surface water irrigation systems.
1.13  The primary responsibility for basic groundwater data collection and evaluation (Annex 7) is
assigned to the Directorate of Environmental Geology (DEG) with i the Ministry of Mines and
Energy (ME). DEG also has responsibility in law to provide technical advice to provincial
Governors on issuing licenses for groundwater development and use, and for technical monitoring of
the enironmental impact of groundwater abstactons. In practice, DEG has insufficient staff and
funds to carry out this task and has, de facto, delegated much of its oversight powers to the
provinces. Authority to issue licenses for groundwater development rests with each provincial
G}overnor. Licensing of groundwvater abstraction and metered monitoing of abstractons has focused
imarily in urban areas, partularly for commercial and industrial uses, as these attact a user fee.
Frquenty, temporadr licenses are issued when resource evaluations are inadequate prior to issuance
Of irm permits. Groundwater abstracton for irrigation and other agricultural uses, and for domestic
use, is free of user fees. Consequendy, groundwater is not closely monitored or regulated by the
licensing a.gency. The P2ATs are usually given freedom to explore for and develop groundwater for
rrigation afterconsultaton with the relevant provincial authorities. Development licenses me issued
tO P2ATs on a blankcet basis. In some provinces, the P2AT office functions as the technical advice
agency to the Governor's office regarding issuance of groundwater use licenses. However, a new
initiative in East Java has vested responsibility for groundwater monitoring and evaluation to PRIS
(para 5.08).
1.14 Agricultural extension is provided through a tiered hierarchy of the Ministry of Agriculture's
(MOA) Extension Service which extends from the center to provincial, subprovincial (Wiliyah),
district (Kabupaen) and subdistrict (Kecamatan) levels. The extension agents (PPLs) work out of the
last office level to serve areas of about 300-1,000 ha. Extension services are coordinated in a
province by the Provincial Agricultal Senrice (PRAS). The agricultural extension service's role in
groundwater irrigation schemes has, in the past, been limited for a variety of reasons including, lack
of interagency coordination, competing extension schedules, and budget and personnel constraints
(para 3.12).
1.15 Water User Associations (WUAs) of beneficiaries are established on government financed
goundwater facilities to take responsibility for post construction management. The legal provisions
regarding the status of WUAs reflect both wntml government and provincial government legislatve
actions. The 1984 Presidential Instru ton No. 2 on Establishing Water User Farner Associations
prescribes responsibilities for establishing the associations to the Ministries of Public Works, Home
Affairs and Agriculture and serves as a model for the establishment of WUAs under individual
provincial decrees. In general, provincial decrees grant WUAs the rights to: privately develop and
munage water sources for irrigation; manage govemment-owned surface water irrigaton systems at
te teray level; and manage govemment-constructed groundwater irrigation facilites. The WUAs
lso have the rights to: elect their own officials; establish locally adapted bylaws as adjuncts to model



-4-
WUA constitutions; owr. prowerty; and raise their own revenues, including collection of inrigadon
fees from the beneficiaries of facilities that they nianage.
1.16 The Ministiy of Home Affairs (MOHA) under its Regulation No. 12, 1992, has enhanced the
status of WUAs as 'legal entities' with contractual rights under national law. This change in the law
will enable WUAs to: open bank accounts; receive Government assets; make contracts; enforce
agreements in court; have access to commercial bank crrdit; and have legitimate control over their
members. This recent action has been encouraged by the Bank through the Irigation Subsector
(O&M) II Project (ISSP II, Ln. 3392-IND). Although the new MOHA Regulation strengthening the
legal status of WUAs is now effective, reflecting this in provincial decrees will take longer.
1.17 Local goverments at provincial, district and subdistrict levels are responsible, in their areas
of jurisdiction, for coordinating support agencies involved in irrigation development through
Interagency Irrigation Committees (I1Cs). Tne most strategic level is that of the district committee
under the aegis of the District Officer (Bupati). In practice, lICs vary widely in their degree of
activity and performance. The agencies which are supposed to be coordinated, in fact, exercise
considerable independence. Moreover, the RC structure is essentially oriented to planning seasonal
operations of government surface water irrigation systems. This is not important in the case of
groundwater irrigation facilities which are completely managed by their beneficiaries.
Ch]alenges Facing Groupdwater Inigamo Development
1.18  Government has rec.gnized that although laws and regulations exist in Indonesia for
integrated water resources management and planning, their application and adequacy to meet current
requirements and future challenges need to be substantially strengthened. Moreover, the strategy for
multi-sectoral water use and management is not fully defined, and the institutional arrangements for
supporting such a strategy have not been developed. The operating functions of different
m-anagement units (e.g., provincial services, river basin authorities, municipalities, and pollution
control and other regulatory agencies) need to be clearly defined and mandated. In some areas of
Indonesia, in particular on Java, competition for scarce water resources (including groundwater) and
degradation of their quality are developing such that an action plan for water management and
regulation needs to be urgently fonmuiated and implemented. An immediate requirement would be
collection of sufficient data to enable effective monitoring and evaluation of quantity and quality of
water resources on a river basin basis. Aspects of this challenge would be specifically addressed
under the proposed project with respect to groundwater resources in the project provinces.
1.19 Despite Indonesia's success in developing its food production (para 1.04), the situation
regarding nce self-sufficiency remains questionable when measured against the rapidly growing
population. The easy opportunities for expanding surface water irrigated agriculture have been
exploited. Potential for further new development is essentially exhausted on Java, Bali and Lombok,
and remains mainly in the Outer Islands. By contrast, there are significant proven groundwater
resources remaining for development, mainly on Java, and investigations will certainly expand the
proven potential on the Outer Islands. However, in developing groundwater resources for irigation,
t will be necessary to take account of growing demands for other uses of water, some of which must
be afforded higher priority than irrigation.
1.20 Notwithstanding Indonesia's satisfactory economic performance overall, there is extremely
wide diversity of incomes. A significant proportion of the population is at the lower end of the
income range and remains below the poverty level. Part of the poverty in rural areas may be related to
small farm size and, to some degree, with lack of irrigation. However, the small commands of
groundwater irigation facilites make it possible to target poverty groups as beneficiaries with far
greater accuracy than is possible with larger surface water irrigation systems. Such targeting
demands a 'hands-on' approach by Government for selecting development areas, particularly at the
planning and site selection stages. Reliance on market forces to provide a purely 'demand driven'
development, entirely financed from private funds, would tend to exclude the poorer population.
Thus, the project would promote the fullest possible measure of beneficiary participation in
development decision making (para 5.10). In this respect, a specific objective is for beneficiaries to



-5-
develop the concept that it is their 'common property', despite the fact that capital costs have been
borne by Government.
1.21  With respect to groundwater development, the above challenges would require changes and
improvetn .-s to project implementation procedures, particularly with regard to subproject planning
and desig- ;nteragency coordination and monitoring performance. It would also be necessary to
maintain . -,ad construction quality so that commissioned facilities can funcdon and be sustained as
intended. Full conmnunity participation is essential at all stages of the development process, since all
project works would be handed ever for farmers' management. Thus, a project objective would be to
minimize the public sector role once facilities have been constructed and commissioned for handover
to WUAs. In this respect, special care will be necessary to ensure that selected subprojects are
technically appropriate and financially viable so that facilities can be sustained under beneficiary
management.
H. BANK INVOLVEMENT AND LESSONS LEARNED
Bank Involvement in ETigation Development
2.Oi General. Bank experience with irrigation development in Indonesia and in many other
countries, demonstrates that the subsector is facing a number of important technical, financial,
institutional and human resource challenges. The OED's Annual Review of Evaluation Results for
1989 1/ suggests that successful irrigation projects have produced important long-term benefits,
particularly in increasing national food supplies and raising incomes in the rural communities. The
review also found that overall operational performance of irigation systems has been poorer than was
anticipated at project appraisal and completion. It notes that physical infrastructure has often
deteriorated, leading in some cases to long-term decline in production and low efficiency of water
u=se. Inadquate O&M funding and institutional inadequacies have contributed to this shortfall in
performance. The review suggests that Borrowers for irrigation development have often disengaged
oo early from projects, leaving irrigation and drainage systems incomplete and land development
unfinished. Moreover, it notes that complementary services--extension, research and marketing--
which are essential to obtaining full productivity from irrigation development, have been inadequate
or seriously delayed. The report also states that beneficiary participation has been much less than
required. Such shortfalls can be attributed to much of Indonesia's irrigation development.
2.02 Dmundwater Irrigation. The Bank has supported substantial programs for groundwater
irrigation development in India, Bangladesh and Pakistan 2/ and smaller scale programs in Philippines
and Myanmar, in addition to its support for developments in Indonesia. In all cases, initial
developments were based primarily on relatively high discharge tubewells which were constructed
and managed by the public sector (Myanmar has yet to develop beyond this stage). While this type of
development may be necessary to catalyze private sector interest in tubewell facilities for irnigation,
expenences have shown that: (a) public sector operations have had only modest success in assunng
equity of access to groundwater (one of the key objectives); (b) public sector management of the
facilities has generally been inefficient and has resulted in lower than expected agricultural benefits;
(c) operating costs have been substantial; and (d) cost recovery has been poor. Thus, the overall
social, financial and economic viabilides of the schemes have been substantially less than predicted.
2.03 In all the above countries, except for Myanmar, introduction of public tubewells for irrigation
was followed by private sector development of shallow tubewells (STW) constructed at relatively low
cost to serve small command areas. However, the private sector wells, fitted with centrifugal
pumping equipment, can be used where pumping water levels are appropriate and other
1/ Annual Review of Evaluation Results for 1989: Agriculture and Rural Development Sector Working Paper, OED,
August 29, 1990.
2/ Bank supported public groundwater development in Padistan was mainly for the Salinity Control and Reclamation
Projects (SCARP) which ostensibly had groundwater drainage as their prime purpose, in most cases involving
vercal dranage wells, but such projects were conceptualized to also provide irrigation benefits.



-6-
hydrogeological conditions are suitable for the well construction technique. Governments have
recognized the advantages of this type of development which is driven by private sector initiative with
facilities purchased, managed and maintained by the owners. They have supported the private
development initiative by provision of: (a) easy access to medium-term credit on attractive terms; (b)
subsidies to costs of tubewell facility equipment; and (c) subsidized electrical power connections and
energy.
2.04 It is generally considered that private sector groundwater irrigation facilities have
demonstrated better performance on most scores than publicly financed and managed facilities. While
this is basically true, private sector development has not been without some important drawbacks.
When governments have supported private development by provision of easy access to medium-term
credit, loan recovery has been typically poor and has often become politicized. Construction of rurl
power networks in India and Pakistan to give highly subsidized connections to pump sets has led to
deteriorating engineering standards, because the programs were underfunded. This in tum has led to
poor quality rral energy supplies. The burden of providing subsidized rural power networks and
electrical energy, without appropriate recompense from the state budgets, has often undermined the
financial viabiliies of the power supply utilities. Moreover, in most cases, the policy decision to
provide electrical energy for agricultural pumping has deprived more cost-effective users from access
to public energy supply sources.
2.05 Large scale private sector development of groundwater for irrigation becomes uncontrollable
by government without imposition of an effective licensing system. Such systems tend to become
bureaucratized and abitrary, and are often corrupted. Furthermore, private sector development tends
to benefit the rich. Moreover, typical private sector facilities can only be installed where
hydrogeological conditions are appropriate. In some countries, including Indonesia, these social and
physical restrictions become an mportant development constaint. More technically complex and
expensive facilities, not appropriate for construction by the private sector, must then be used if the
objective is to make maximum use of groundwater resources for inigation and reach the poor.
2.06 Bank Lendinj for Ifrigaion. Irrigation has absorbed a major part of Bank lending for
Indonesian agricultural development over the past two decades with loans/credits totalling some US$
2.0 billion for 26 opertons (Table 2.1 below). Most irrigation development has been on Java, and
has almost entirely been based on surface water resources. During the 1970's, the Bank supported:
(a) rehabilitadon and modernization of irrigation and drainage systems, construction of new systems
and a multipurpose dam; and (b) institutional strengthening and manpower development, (although
with a limited impact). More recently, the focus of Bank lending has been on improved O&M of
existing systems, support for policies entailing turnover of small public schemes to farmer groups,
and introduction of an irrigation service fee. These policies are aimed at increasing efficiency and
sustainability of investments, reducing dependence on central government budgets for O&M funding,
improving resource allocation, and increasing beneficiary participation. The ongoing Provincial
Irrigated Agricultural Development Project (PIADP, Loan 3302-IND) aims to complete and fully
develop ongoing small and medium schemes in the Outer Islands and to develop institutional capacity
to improve coordinadon of inigetion with agriculttual and other related services.

2.07 Of the 20 Bank loans for inrigation and related agricultural development closed to date, some
15 have been evaluated by the Bank s Operation Evaluation Department (OED), of which, the most
recent and relevant audit reports are Nos. 6864 and 7956 1/. In addition, two Project Completion
Reports (PCRs) 2/for projects that have not yet been audited by OED, have relevance for recent
irfgation development. The Intemational Irrigation Management Institute (IIMI) has also produced a
report on Indonesian irrigation development M/ and USAID and ODA have produced evaluation
reports on pump irrigation in Indonesia and the Madura groundwater development project respectively

Lssues and Lessns Ied in ki"£onm Devlodamet in Indontesia
2.08 There can be no doubt that provision of irrigation facilities has had a major positive pact on
food grain production in Indonesia (para 1.05). Moreover, it is evident that, in general, farmers
resond well when provided with reliable and timely irigation service. Nevertheless, there is room
for considerable improvement in all aspects of planning, design, construction and O&M of irrigauon
systems. Lessons learned, which could lead to improvements, fall into four broad categories -
P/ PAR, Idgao Projec  VI, VLII, IX and XII, (Ln. NoS. 1100, 1434, 1435, 1579 and 1691-IND), June 30, 1987,
Report No. 6864, and PPAR, Ipigaton Projects X, XrV and XV (Ln. NoS. 1578,1811 and Cr. No. 995-IND);
June 30, 1989, Report No. 7956.

4/Policy Altmatives for Pumping Irigton in Idnesia, USAID Mission of Indonesi, June 1992, and (Daft)
Evaluation of Madma I uan Project, ODA (UK), August 1992.



-8-
technical, institutional, financial and environmental. The main shortcomings include: (a) inadequate
quality control for all development phases; (b) Government's lack of priority for nonconstruction and
O&M activities; (c) insdtutional and human resource constraints; (d) lack of coordination of irrigation
and agricultural programs; (e) poor monitoring and evaluation of irrigation facility performance and
the impact of irrigation on agricultural production; (f) local funding constraints; (g) inappropriate
priority in use of available funds; (h) inadequate cost recovery mechanisms; (i) inadequate beneficiary
participation through formalized water user organizations; and (j) insufficient consideration of
environmental impact.
2.09 Technical Constraints. Inadequate physical resource data (hydrology and soils) and agro-
economic data at the preparation stage of many irrigation projects has resulted in flaws in planning
and design. These, with deficiencies in construction, have caused large reconstruction expenditures
that are often ureated as maintenance. Priorities are not geared to improving operations, but are
predicated on the degree of physical disrepair. Maintenance activities are generally uncoordinated and
often do not relate to efficiency, or operational objectives, but rather to the availability of funds,
which are generally unpredictable. There is litle reliable information on the technical Derformance of
irrigation systems, and thus no confinnation of the O&M principles and operational citeria built into
designs. An underlying problem is that many schemes, particularly in the Outer Islands, were never
fuflly completed and thus, the irrigation systems are generally inadequate to deliver the full benefits of
irrigation. Government and the Bank (under the PIADP, Ln. 3302-IND) are addressing these issues
to ensure that schemes can operate as designed before handing over to provincial O&M authorities.
In addition, action is being taken to institutionalize processes whereby plans and operational
procedures are in place, and -sufficient funding and other resources are available for efficient O&M.
2.10 Institutional and Human Resource Constraints. The developmental impact of the irrigation
subsector depends on effectve coordination and cooperation between the Ministries of Public Works,
Agriculture, Home Affairs and Provincial Authorities. In particular, such coordination is not effective,
on the Outer Islands. Each agency follows its own budget provision (which, for developmuent
activities, is directed by the central agency in Jakarta), program, and priorities, none of which are
effectively coordinated with other agencies. The agencies function as if each project activity is self-
contained. In addition, no agency involved in irrigated agricultural development undertakes a regular
evaluation of either its own perfonmance, or its contribution to the overall benefits from irrigated
agriculture.
2.11 The centrally controlled approach followed by most agencies involved in irrigation
development tends to impose a 'top-down' approach to project planning. Inability of Government to
provide adequate staff incentives, limited career prospects and inefficient use of human resources
contribute to the staffing problems. These are compounded by unclear accountability. In addition,
Govemment heavily depends on consultant services which substitute for shortages of technical skills
within the public services. In many cases, the quality of consultant's work is less than satisfactory,
primarily because there is no accountability for performance. The impact of training, supported by
various donors, is also unclear. In these respects, the Bank is now encouraging improved monitoring
and coordination of consultancies employed by GOI, and the assignment of suitably qualified
Govemment staff to work with consultants to facilitate transfer of knowledge.
2.12 It is not generally possible to statistically evaluate the effectiveness of operational projects
because of lack of reliable data. Reporting records are concerned maiiily with implementation
progress of ongoing projects, and individual contracts and their related budget allocations and
expenditures. Little useful data is collected on the quality of performance of completed schemes. As
a consequence, there is a general lack of diagnostic analysis on performance of both the irrigation
service and the related agricultural support service.
2.13 There has been inadequate participation of beneficiaries and local communities in the design
and management concepts of irrigation schemes. While water user groups are found in many
irrigated areas in all their provinces, their impact often has been less than satisfactory. More
formalized organizations are required. Thus, the proposed project would give special attention to the
processes and procedures for community participation and to monitoring the effectiveness of these



-9-
arrangements (para 5.10). In addition, the Bank is supporting under ISSP II (Ln. 3302-IND) and
PIADP (Ln. 3302-IND) the establishment and strengthiemng of Water Users Associations (WUJAs) so
thlat they can take over and manage small schemes previously under public sector management. In
support of this concept, the Government is putting in place the legal and administrative procedures
needed to facilitate the turnover of small public schemes to farmer groups (para 1. 16).
2.14 Financial Constraints Insufficient local funding has been an important development
constraint, particularly for activities that require predictable and regular budgetary provisions, such as
hydrological data collection and monitoring and evaluation. Priority is given to funding works, to the
detriment of other key development activities such as land acquisition for infrastructure and settlement
and agricultural development, which often are not supported by donors under projects. Generic
funding problems have two features, namely unavailability of funds and the budg= procss. In
general, schemes do not have secure financing commitments when they are started and projects are
often delayed by lack of funding for advanced action prior to Loan effectiveness.
2.15  Envionmentl Constraints. There has been only limited consideration of the environmental
impact of irrigated agricultural development. This reflects the fact that irrigation projects generally
have been planned, designed and implemented as civil works rather than as development operations.
Nominal attention has been given to the adverse effects of irrigation development on the surrounding
area and the long-term environmental impacts. Thus, the needs are becoming acute (particularly in
Java) to protect and conserve water and other natural resources, and to provide better management for
catchment areas. The growing competition for water between various users has also revealed the
need for proper management of all water resources at the river basin level. This must include
appropriate measures for alocation of surface water and groundwater and to ensure that their
chemical/biological quality is maintained at sustainable levels. Fnally, particularly on the Outer
Islands, involuntary resetdement is not always planned and implemented with a thorough analysis and
understanding of its sustainability, and with sensitivity to the needs of the population affected.
2.16                                                         001 activities and Bank-
supported operations for groundwater irrigation development in Indonesia have been relatively limited
and mainly concentrated on Java. From 1968, systematic groundwater explorations were initiated in
Kederi-Nganjuk, Madiun and Madura with bilateral support from the United Kingdom (UK) and
others. During 1976-79, groundwater exploration was extended to cover other areas in East Java
province, again with UK assistance. Some exploratory activities progressed to include small pilot
groundwater irrigation developments in East Java. In 1982, the East Java Groundwater Development
Project was launched as a component of the Bank-supported Irigation XVII Project (Loan 2119-
IND) aiming to develop some 6,300 ha of groundwater irrigation. In 1983, the Madiun-Solo
Groundwater Pilot Schemes were initiated to provide irgation to about 6,703 ha. By 1985, all
groundwater development projects in East Java, except for Madura, were merged into the Java Timur
Groundwater Development Project which continued into FY 1988/89. Meanwhile, the Madura
initiatives progressed slowly with continuing UK and (lattely) with EEC assistance.
2.17 The East Java developments were conceived initillly to provide supplementary irrigation to
parts of surface water systems which had marked deficit supplies in the dry season. The belief was
that wells should be designed to maximize discharges to obtain lowest costs per unit volume of water.
It then became apparent that such conjunctive use systems were very difficult to manage, particularly
with the very large command areas implicit with high discharge wells. Subsequently, in the mid
1980's, wells were located where they would provide the sole source of irrigation supply. This
period also saw recognition that lined conveyance systems were required to deliver the expensive
pumped water supplies efficiently to the fields. Aftrexpe imentation with various forms of channel
lining, most of the systems were completed with factory-wade, steam-cured, precast concrete units,
which have proved to be physically efficient. However, experments with buried PVC pipe systems
revealed that these had cost, construction and opaional advantages over any type of open channel
lining. An important institutional development of this period was the establishment of WUAs in
accordance with the Presidential Instruction No. 2 of 1984 (para 1.15). Subsequently, tubewell
irrigation facilities in East Java were handed over to WUAs for management, albeit with initial
Government support (two years) for costs of fuel supply, pump operators' salary and maintenance.



- 10-
Responsibility for major facility replacements still remains with Government, though large scale
replacement of pump units and wells have yet to be required (para 6.15).
2.18  The ISSP (Ln. 2880-IND, 1988) included a component for groundwater development, which
included some development on the Outer Islands, in addition to East Java. A review revealed that,
apart from an investigation then approaching compledon on Lombok (which was receiving Italian
assistance), the status of knowledge of groundwater conditions in the Outer Islands was nominal 11/
A groundwater reconnaisace in Central and South Sulawesi indicated that, although there was some
groundwater potential for irrigadon, this was likely to occur in small pockets that could not justify
detailed exploration inputs before development was initiated. Moreover, the previous practice of
having a prolonged exploration phase (as in East Java) had not been particularly cost-effective. A
process was therefore proposed to implement small progmams of exploratory wells in selected areas
with likely groundwaterpotential. These well points then would be converted to production wells if
they prove successful, and the development program progressively expanded. This 'expansion'
approach has been implemented with fair success in Central and South Sulawesi.
2.19 Other successful features of the ISSP groundwater component were: introduction of the
intermediate technology tubewell (ITTW); use of buried PVC pipe for conveyance systems; and
introduction of PVC pipes and screens for well construction. The ITIW concept (para 3.07) was the
most important design advance, since this recogzed that groundwater conditions in many areas were
suitable for relatively shallow tubewells equipped with centifugl pumps to serve command areas of
about 10 ha through buried PVC pipe distribution systems. the lTTW facilities have important
advantages over the deep tubewell (DTW) facilities previously installed by Goverrunent, including:
(a) lower capital COStS per unit area; (b) relatively easy maintenance and replacement of equipment;
and (c) smaller numbers of beneficiaries per well and thus more manageable WUAs.
2.20 Lessons learned from the ISSP groundwatercomponent (to be addressed under the project),
include: (a) combining exploration and pilot development, leading rapidly to full development, must
be applied with care; (b) supervision of construction must be properly organized and quaity control
improved; (c) procurement should be timely with equipment properly specified; (d) more emphasis
should be given to the post construction phase through closely coordinated government support
agencies; (e) community participation and W UA traimning should be strengthened; and (f) monitoring
and evaluation of development impact needs substantial strengthening.
2.21 In Java, there has been a considerable expansion of private sector fmanced development of
groundwater for iigation using shallow tubewell (STW) technology since the early 1980's (para
1.10). This development has taken place with very limited support from Govenmuent An attempt to
improve the construction technology of STWs under ISSP (through DGWRD) was abandoned
because well performance was generally very poor. It is now considered that the farmer-developed
well construction technology is cost-effective and can not easily be improved. A Japanese aid-
assisted project, initiated in 1991, has a component intended to extend the STW technology to a
selectd area of West Timor. This project provides 100 percnt subsidy for capital cost, all inputs for
the first year of operation and very heavy extension support through consultancy services. The
overall effectiveness and economic viability of this approach is yet to be demonstated.
2.22 The Central Java Groundwater Irrigadon Project, financed by ADB in 1991, was originally
conceived to have a significant farmer contribution to capital costs through credit. However,
Indonesia's recent deregulation of the financial sector, and particularly phasing out of liquidity credit
to rural institutions, has increased the cost of borrowing (annual interest of 25-30 percent) beyond
most farmers' capacity to pay. The ADB project will therefore now construct ITrW and DTW
groundwater irrigation facilities (very similar to those installed under ISSP and as intended under the
proposed project) with all initial capital costs met by Govenment. Beneficiaries will be responsible
1/ Sumbawa and Went Tmor Sand, sect of gromdwae elration with CIDA adsi in 197841, were not
induded under tie ISSP program, since tr deveopment was akeady beig suppoed by USAID.

for O&M costs and the replacement costs of pumps and power units. The proposed project intends to
require similar support from beneficiaries (para 6.15).
Pxposed Strategy and Rationale fcw Bank Inolvement
2.23  Govemment St=         . Government's objectives for rice-based farming systms in surface
water irrigated lowlands are to: maintain food security; facilitate crop diversification during the
second crop season; alleviate poverty; and support regional development. Goverment's 1987 Polic
Statementfor the Ei&an Subsector defined these inigation policy objectives and laid a bss for the
subsector priorities. These are: (a) introduce sound O&M practices so that irrigation systems can be
sustained, (b) increase local revenue generadon and cost recovery from beneficiaries; (c) turn over
(privatize) smaller government irrigation schemes to farmer groups; and (d) complete viable high-
priority projects, particularly those with large sunk investments. In carrying out the last objective,
Government has recendy expressed its intention of focusing increasingly on the integradon of
agricultjral development activities along with development of physical infrastucu  to fuly and more
quickly realize potential agicultural benefits. Groundwater development has imwpotance in the overall
strategy because it can support irrigated agriculture in some areas where surface irrigation is not
feasible. Moreover, it can be directed to support poverty alleviadon, regional development and crop
diversification. Thus, the overall strategy is to concentrate groundwater development in the rainfed
areas where poverty is prevalent, particularly in the Outer Islands.
2.24 Bank Sttegy. The Bank fully supports the Government's objectives of sustained growth in
the agricultural sector, focus on reductuon of rural poverty, increased producdve employment
opportunities in rural areas, and a more balanced regional distribution of growth. The Bank strategy
is to reflect the lessons learned from PCRs and OED audits in respect to both the portfolio
management and lending strategy, and incorporate these into a continuing sector dialogue with
Government. The Bank strongly supports Governments focuses on ensuring the efficiency and
sustainability of past irrigation investments, on improved irrigation water resource management, and
on water allocation given competing demands. This strategy is needed to sustain the growth in
production of food grains required to meet the growing consumption demand. The Bank support
would focus most in areas of institutional structure, organizational strengthening and human resource
development, particularly in the context of multipurpose management of water.
2.25 The Bank strategy would be pursued through a coordinated lending program with each
operaion focused on inportant and complementary objectives. In this respect
(a) The Provincial Irrigated Agriculture Development Project (PIADP, Ln. 3302-IND) supports
integrated development and completion of schemes, and develops institutional mechanisms
and procedures for irngated agriculture in the Outer Islands, with emphasis on planning and
design o irrgation works, land development and related agricultural aspect
(b) The Second Irigation Subsector Project (ISSP 11, Ln. 3392-IND) focuses on strengthening
of O&M-related insdtutions, cost recovery for inigation services, tumover of small schemes
to farmer groups, and on improved management and allocation of water resources both at
the scheme level and in a river basin context, primarily in Java.
(c)  The proposed Groundwater Development Project and the Itgrted Swamps Development
Project (under preparation) would exploit water resources in less developed regions and
specificaly promote regional equity and support poverty alleviation.
(d) Looking to the future, the Bank would support the overall strategy through the proposed
Java Water Resource Management Project and the proposed Dam Safety PzojecL



- 12-
2.26 A recent Bank agricultural sector review for Indonesia 1/ analyses the potential and rationale
for expansion of the irrigated area to meet the needs for food production, particularly to maintain self-
sufficiency in rice production to year 2010. The report indicates that there are 2.7 million ha
identified as 'fully suitable' for new irrigation development, and, in addition, there are 0.5 million ha
of land located within irrigation command areas which are not served with water. Since only some 26
percent of this land is in Java and Bali, most of the potential for irrigation expansion is in the Outer
Islands. The report envisages that new irrigated area will be developed at a rate of about 35,000 ha
per year to replace area lost to urban expansion and rural settlement, and for regional development
policy reasons, primarily to combat poverty. Groundwater development would have a significant role
in achieving this increase in new irrigated land.
2.27 Rationale for Bank Involvement. The main rationale for Bank involvement is that the
proposed project would: (a) consolidate the groundwater development in Indonesia; (b) address the
main lessons leared to date; (c) give increased attention to community participation and achieving full
potential benefits; (d) support GOs efforts to develop groundwater as a multipurpose resource; and
(e) give focused support to strengthening institutional arrangements for groundwater resource
management and regulation. Continued Bank support of groundwater development would be
justified because it accords with GOrs objectives for regional development, equity and poverty
alleviadon, in areas without surface irrigation potential. Moreover, the project would provide
important opportunities for community participation in irrigation system development, O&M, cost
recovery, and water use management

3.01 EProect Concept. The proposed project would standardize the approach to groundwater
development covering most areas of Indonesia 2/ with known groundwater potential. This would
provide an opportunity to systematically address many generic subsector lessons leamed for previous
groundwater projects including: (a) strengthening institudonal arrangements, interagency
coordination, and community participation activities; and (b) standardization of criteria, procedures
and processes related to the planning, design, implementation and O&M of groundwater facilities.
Justification for the geographical spread of the project and the number of components is detailed in
Annex 5. The project supervision requirements by the Bank are estimated in Annex 16.
3.02  ProjecQOjecve. A major project objecdve would be to develop groundwater irrigation in
less developed regions to help alleviate hard core poverty. Technical objectives would be to promote
multipurpose use of groundwater resources and to develop least cost, appropriate technology that can
be sustained by user groups with minimal public sector support. Thus the project would enable full
beneficiary participation in development and O&M of the irrigation systems, and would assist GOI in
strengthening its planning and implementation capacity for groundwater development In addition,
the project would give specific support to: (a) agricultural development activities; (b) interagency
coordination at national and provincial levels; (c) quality control procedures; (d) encouraging the role
of women as part of the communuity participation activities; (e) monitoring and evaluation of irrigation
and agricultural production performance; and (f) strengthening the monitoring, evaluation and
regulaton of groundwater resources.
3.03 EPrject Stratgy. The project would be structured as a six-year operation. The first year
would cover mainly detailed preparation of work programs and procurement. In the following years,
the project would implement annual work programs, with activities to be defined and agreed through
an annual review process scheduled to fit in with the Government's budgetary process (para 5.26).
This approach would provide the flexibility needed to respond to inevitable unknowns in groundwater

development, particularly the limited hydrogeological data in some proposed locations in the Outer

3.04 .roect Scoge. Development areas have been identified in eleven provinces for the
construction of: (a) some 1,900 intermediate technology tubewells (1T`Ws) to serve about 18,650
ha, (b) some 240 deep tubeweUs (DTWs) to serve about 6,000 ha. In addition, a pilot program using
shallow tubewells (STWs) would develop about 500 ha (para 3.12). The proposed schemes would
be screened to confirm that they provide the least cost facility for any paricular development area.
The schemes have been categorized under two programs - a 'go-ahead' program with limited
associated risks, and an 'expansion' program which would be implemented only after nsks have been
reduced to acceptable levels throught monitoring performance of existing tubewell developments and
confirmation of available groundwater resources (para 3.20). About 15,300 ha of groundwater
development have been included under the 'go-ahead' program with some 9,350 ha under the
'expansion' cateaory. A detailed breakdown of the proposed development by scheme area, facility
type and under go-ahead' and 'expansion' programs is shown in Annex 1, Table 1, and proposed
annuai implementation programs are shown in Annex 1, Table 6.
B. Projiect Components
3.05 The project would finance all activities until tubewells schemes can be demonstrated to be in
full operation after two years of O&M support. Major components would include: (a) survey,
investigation and design; (b) construction of tubewell systems serving about 25,000 ha; (c) initial
O&M support for two years after commissioning of tubewell systems; (d) provision of domestic
water supply and house garden irrigation facilities; (e) agricultural development activities, including
strengthening of extension services; (f) community support, including training and strengthening of
WUAs and activities related to the role of women; and (g) institutional support for design,
implementation, supervision and monitoring and evaluation of project performance. Technical
assistance through consultancy services would be provided to support the above activities, as
necessary. The project would also strengthen groundwater monitoring networks by construction and
equippig of observation wells, provide water quality monitoring equipment, and provide training for
PRIS staff in the project provinces on groundwater resources evaluation.
C.   ailEn=  of the Pro
3.06  .Suny. Investigon. I2eig} (SD). The SID activities would relate to the site and facility
selection screening process, planning and design of the irrigation facilities and, where applicable, the
domestic water supply works (Annex 5). A major objective of the SI) activities would be to evaluate
the technical, social and economic viability of each proposed development area. The process would
decide the appropriate technology and assess the need for domestic water supply, and with full
participation of potential beneficiaries, sequentially undertake the steps in the screening, planning and
design process. The SID process would be applied to all tubewell schemes to assess: (a) adequacy
of physical resources (groundwater availability and quality, soils, topography, rainfall); (b) land
ownership and related issues; (c) beneficiary commitment to managing and sustaining the works; and
(d) foreseen agricultural impact. The SID work would be undertaken by P2AT staff with the
assistance of consultant sevics. Esdmated costs are given in Annex 3, Tables 1 to 11.
3.07  Innwdiame Technology Tubewell aITI=V1 Facilides. The 1TIW facilities are planned in all
provinces. The standard facility is based on tubewells designed for discharges of about 10 liters per
second (V/sec) to serve command areas of about 10 ha, with proportionately larger command areas
served when higher well discharges can be obtained in practice. The wells would be equipped with
centrifugal pumps powered by diesel motors restricting the technology to areas where groundwater
conditions are suitable for sucdon mode pumping. The wells would be dri!led to depths of about 40-
70 m, depending on local hydrogeological conditions and, in most cases, fully lined with casing and
screen and gravel packing as required. Where aquifer formations would stand in open hole
(limestone or sandstone), screens would be omitted and only an upper well PVC casing would be
insalled. Block or brick lined sumps up to 3.0 m deep would be provided on the well head, when
necessaiy, to extend the range of suction mode pumping. A simple block or brick-built pump house



- 14-
would be provided. Water distribution would be through buried PVC pipe to tumout boxes with
alfalfa valves commanding about 1.0 ha, and then through earth channels to the fields. Typical
designs and selection of sites are discussed in Annex 5. Detailed cost estimates are given in Annex 5,
Tables 2, 4 and 5 for the wells, distribution systems, and pump sets respectively.
3.08       vnibewell MTM) Facil. The DTW facilities are planned only in East and West Java,
Lampung and Lombok in areas where the groundwater conditions are not suitable for rITWs. They
would be based on tubeweLls designed for discharges of about 20 to 30 I/sec to serve command areas
of about 20-30 ha. The wells would be equipped with vertcal shaft turbine pump units The wells
would be drilled to depths of 70110 m. Most wells would be fully lined and screened with PVC
tubular materials and would be gravel packed. Where aquifer formatons would stand in open hole
(as for ITTWs), only an upper well PVC casing would be installed A block or brick-built pump
house would be provided. The vertical shaft turbine pumps would be powered through belt drive by
diesel motors eqWpped with inerta startng mechanisms. Distribution would be through buried PVC
pipe to turnout boxes wih alfalfa valves coanding about 1.0 ha, and then rough ear channels
to the fields. Typical designs are discussed in Annex 5. Detailed costimates are given in Annex 5,
Tables 3,4 and 5 for the wells, distribution systems, and pump sets respectively.
3.09  Initial Suot for O&M of Facilides. A basic project objective is to develop tubewell
irigation facilities that can be transfered to user groups (WUAs) for management with minima public
sector suporT Tubewell facilities will be sited in areas where farmers are poor and generally have no
previous experence of irrigation or management of a group facility (Annex 4). Thus, once tubewell
iigation facilities are certified by P2AT as complete for commissioning, an essential project activity
would be initial support of WUAs for the O&M of facilities. The project would therefore support full
O&M costs for two years after the date of comissioning, including, inter alia: supply of fuel and
lubricants; routine maintenance; the tubewell operator's salary; and the incremental staffing costs to
provide the WUAs with technical advice on all aspects of O&M and the management of the facilites.
HIowever, during the O&M support period, beneficiaries would be expected to pay an irgation
service fee to the WUA so that the associate could accumulate funds for subsequent O&M of
facilities, including routine maintenance and repairs (para 6.14). The scope and estimated costs of
this component for each province are given in Annex 3, Tables I to I l.
3.10  Domesi Wt Suolv (DWS) Facilities. Project tubewells would be located primarily for
agricultural objectives but, once sited, multipurpose use of the water source would be evaluated. In
this process, the potential impact of a project tubewell on existing water supply wells would be
assessed and the use of the irrigation tubewell water source for new DWS facilities would be
examined. The assessments would be undertaken with full participation of the potential beneficiaries
(Annex 4). Wher demand is recognizd, the project would provide DWS facilities appropriate to the
site conditions and the number of beneficiaries to be served. The simplest facility would be a low
level strage tank with tap outlets connected to an itrigation tubewell facility when this is located close
to a settlements. It would be managed by a typical WUA (primarily established to manage the
irrigation facility). More complex viage water supply facilities, as adjuncts to tubewell irrigation
systems, could reticulate wel water supply to the public water points, or into individual house
compounds 11. Planning and design criteria for DWS 2/, and the process and procedures for
community participation are discussed in Annexes 4 and 5.
3.11  Hueard rigiO Facilities, A challenge with most irrigation schemes in Indonesia is to
effectively reach rral families which are either landless, or have holdings that are too small to
produce an income from agricultural production. For these poor households, it is generally assumed
that the increased laborrequirement for irrigated agriculture would provide employment opportunities
to be supplemented with oter non-farm work. However, the project would also test and demonstrate

3.12  Ag   e veIn aL   lo~mcnL Though Government agencies exist to provide the entire range of
agricultural support senrices, past history of agricultural support to groundwater irrigation
development has been less than satisfactoiy (Annex 9). This reflects the fact that tubewell command
areas are small and scattered and their extension requirements do not fit well within the existing
organizadonal arrangements of tne PRAS. The project would therefore support agricultural
development activities to ensure achievement of predicted irrigated agricultural benefits through
coordinated actions with MOA at the center and the PRAS in the roject provinces. Such activities
would be location specific and include: support of additional sta mg, facilities and equipment for
agricultural extension services; seeds for high value crop trials and demonstation; training for farmers
groups for both tubewell command areas and house garden enterprises (para 3.10); and development
of marketing arrangements. In addition, the project would support a pilot program of shallow
tubewells (STWs) to develop about 500 ha using some 250 'taxi' pump sets. The scope and
esdmated costs of agricultural development activities are given in Annex 3, Table 13. The project
would also provide for technical assistce under the consultant sevices to assist MOA and the PRAS
to assist in the preparation of agricultural action plans focused on groundwater development. More
general support for agricultural research and extension would be forthcoming from projects and
programs available in Indonesia under other finmcing sources (ADB, IBRD and other donors).
3.13  _mmuniI Emipadn&tivides. Govemnment's stategy is to minimize the public sector
role in groundwater development by introducing appropriate technology that can be managed and
sustained by the beneficiaries. Ful community participaton is essential to support this strategy
because the beneficiaries must fully accept their responsibilites and condidtons under which facilities
will be provided. Thus the project would support additional staffing, services, transportation,
facilities and equipment required for support of commurity involvement in the development process,
and for establishment and training of WUAs. The project would also support community
participation activities specifically focused to encourage and enhance the role of women in the project
impact areas (para 3.23). The community partcipation activities are descrbed in Annex 4.
3.14  Insitutional S u  Most project activities are simple and straight forward (i.e., routine
planning, design, and implementation of facilities, and initial O&M tasks). In this respect, the project
continues ongoing programs which are being implemented by the existing technical agencies.
However, the insttutional performance to date needs to be strengthened, primarily for non-
construction tasks such as inter-agency coordination, agricultural development, and monitoring and
evaluation of the performance of completed works. The project would therefore finance institutional
support activities for additional project staff and for new institutional initiatives intended specifically
to implement the project and continue future groundwater development, including: office facilities;
services; transportation and office equipment; selected project-specific staff training; and support for
non-irrigation activities, particulatiy with regard to the role of women.
3.15 Office equipment would be provided for centers in each province irn which project activities
are planned and in the projects central offices in Jakarta. The equipment would include computers
and associated hardware, mainly for data processing and for reporting purposes. Transportation
would be for survey and investigation, construction supervision and to provide mobility for WUA
formation units and agricultura support staff. Iaining would be project specific and directed
priwarily to improve the quality of lower level staff such as trainers of tubewell operators, staff
responsible for establishment of WUAs and the agricultural support staff.
3.16  Groundwater ResouMangent. The project would finance activities to support the
PRIS in project provinces to take responsibility for monitoring and evaluation of groundwater
resource data (para 5.19). In this respect, the project would provide for: installation of water level
monitoring networks equipped with water level recorders; water quality monitoring equipment;
computers for data storage, retrieval and processing, incremental staff for data collection and
processing; raining for PRIS staff in groundwater resource evaluation; and special studies of the
organizatonal requirements in East Java for water resource monitoring and evaluation. The support

3.17 Consultancy Services. The consultancy services would be set up to reflect that the the
proposed project covers eleven provinces of Indonesia. For rndwater dOMelmt activities, the
consultant services would be some 164 foreign and 528 local person months. The main tasks would
be to assist in: (a) the review of subproject preparation; (b) standardization of technical criteria and
procedures; (c) supervision and overall coordination of project works implementation; (d) the
monitoring, evaluation and general reporting of project inigation performance; and (e) strengthening
the PRIS in East Java and other provinces with respect to groundwater resources monitoring and
evaluation. The foreign consultants would be based in Jakarta under the PAT subdirectorate of DOI-
II in DGWRD, with individual consultants visiting subprojects in all project provinces as required.
The consultancy input would include some 22 person months for item (e) above. In addidon, there
would be two regional teams of local consultants to provide direct support to P2AT offices in the
provinces. One would be located in Surabaya to support activities in East Java, North, Central and
South Sulawesi, and NTB and NTT, and the second in Jakarta for West Java and the project
provinces in Sumatra. For agicultuml developet and npae community pation activities,
the consultant services under MOA would be some 130 foreign and 396 local person months. The
main task of the consultants would be to assist in: (a) agricultural aspects of the preparation and
analysis of the subprojects; (b) supervision and overall coordinaton of provision of agricultural
support services; and (c) monitoring and evaluation and general reportng of the project's agricultural
perfonmance. The local consultant teams would include a WID specialist. Draft terms of reference
for both consultancy services are given in Annex 13. The estimated costs of the consultant services
are given in Annex 3, Table 14. An assurance was obtained that Government, by September 30,
1993, would employ consultant services to assist with project implementation and supervision under
terms of reference satisfactory to the Bank.
D. Status gf enaraton gnd  lmenato Schedule
3.18  Status of eprtio. The project has been prepared by the line agencies at national level in
consultation with the provinces with the assistance of consultants employed by DGWRD. The
preparation report was submitted in July 1991, and following discussions between the Bank and
GOI, a major addendum was submitted early in 1992. This addendum reformulated the project to
better reflect the present performance of existing groundwater development and resulted in changes to
the project assumptions including: agricultural objectives; consultancy services inputs; revisions to
the project analysis; and new initiatives and procedures related to community participation and the role
of women. Additional consultancy inputs, financed by the Italian Trust Fund for Special Studies,
were provided from March through August 1992 to pursue further aspects of the preparation study
and to assist Government in finalizing the first year's work progrdm.
3.19  The project concept envisages that all groundwater development would be ba&  ~n planning
and design criteria, implementation processes and procedures, and institutional arrangt-.aents which
are standardized and coordinated. The first year of project implementation would include start-up
work progreums and procurement of equipment and materials for all project locations that are presendy
well defined on technical, social and economic grounds, and can be undertaken with minimum risk.
In the remaining five years, the project would support similar programs (or continuation of the initial
work programs) to be defined and agreed during an annual review process with the Bank (para 5.25).
3.20  The work programs would be divided into 'go-ahead' and 'expansion' programs. The 'go
ahed part of a program can be implemented immediately and the 'expansion' part would proceed
when: (a) tubewell perfomwance has been sufficiendy monitored to confirm the availability of reliable
goundwater resources for the additional development proposed; and (b) existing tubewells have
satisfied agreed performance ctiteria. This approach of 'go-ahead' and 'expansion' work programs
would provide the flexibility needed to reflect the inevitable uncertainties in groundwater
development. At the present stage, 15,300 ha of development are categorized as 'go-ahead', while
9,350 ha are in the 'expansion' category (para 3.04). Assurances were obtained that Government
would cause the project executing agencies to: (a) locate, screen, select and commission project



- 17-
schemes in accordance with criteria and procedures agreed with the Bank; and (b) itmplement the
project in accordance to technical, financial, administradve, agricultural and environmental practices,
standards and criteria satisfactory to the Bank. An assurance was also obtained from Government
that, prior to implementation of schemes under 'expansion' areas, it would cause DGWRD to
undertake studies in the 'expansion' areas, satisfactory to the Bank, to confir that: (a) existing
tubewells in the area perform to agred performance criteria; (b) groundwater resources are sufficient
in quantity and quality for the proposed development; and (c) proposed schemes are technically and
socially viable.
3.21  Implemntation Schedule. The implementation schedule is shown by province, subproject
and type of irrigation facility in Annex 1, Table 6 and by province and component, expressed as
annual expenditures, in Annex 3, Tables 1 to 14. Subprojects in 'expansion' areas have been
provisionally scheduled to reflect insdtutional implementation capabUlity 1/. The implementation
schedule would be updated annually to reflect progress in the previous year and confirmed by about
May/June each year during an annual review process so that budget provisions and other resources
can be planned for the following fiscal year. The review process would decide what 'expansion'
schemes can be taken into the 'go-ahead' program (para 3.20). In this respect, assurances were
obtained that Government would submit to the Bank for its review and comments, for each province
under the project: (a) not later than September 30 each year, a coordinated work program and cost
estimates for the next fiscal year, and (b), by December 31 each year, coordinated budget proposals
for the agreed provincial work programs.
E. Ennm    An
3.22 Groundwater development in the designated project areas would not involve clearance of
natural vegetation, and any negative impact on the groundwater resources would be signalled through
the monitoring system (para 3.16) and forestalled by appropriate adjustments to the development
program in any affected area. The social impact would be positive through poverty alleviation and
improved incomes in agricultural areas which have limited alternative sources of income. The social
objectives would be achieved through full community participation in the planning process.
Environmental aspects to be given special attention in the planning process, detailed in Annex 15,
would include: (a) groundwater availability, particularly in coastal areas with risks of saline intrusion;
(b) competing uses of groundwater, and in particular, possible impact of project wells on domestic
water wells; and (c) biological and chemical water quality when facilities would povide village water
supply in addition to inigation. Social equity considerations would be given attention, in particular,
the position of tenant farmers, those with very smal farm holdings, and the landless. The intention to
develop irrigated house gardens would enable such disadvantaged families to be reached (para 3.10).
To ensure environmental considerations are fully taken into account, studies would be undertaken in
project areas, satisfactory to the Bank, to ensure that both water quantity and quality and social equity
considerations are taken into account in the project development process. This test would be a
specific criteria in the project planning and design process for each subproject area (para 3.20).
F. ole of Women
3.23 The project includes support to specifically enhance and encourage the role of women in
project activities as described in Annex 12. Studies in Indonesia have shown that there is an
important potential for a greater involvement of women in initiating and managing income generating
activities relating to irrigation, such as small scale cultivation and marketing of produce. Women also
have the main responsibility for management of domestic water use, family hygiene and maintenance
of clean households and compounds. When a group activity is managed by a mixed gender group,
such as a domestic water supply (DWS) system, it is often the women who are responsible for user
fee collection, bookkeeping, and day-to-day supervision of the systems. Many women have been
1/If the planned target to be developed in one province cannot be achieved, the pogm can be transferred to another
province, sice the overall groundwater development potential in the prqject area substantialy exceeds the project
objecfive of developing about 25,000 ha over six years.



18-
trained in the maintenance of DWS systems and are often mome responsive to the opeational problems
of such schemes as they are the first to suffer from disruptions in supplies. The project would give
special attendon to this potendal by involving women in the development process and encouraging
their participation in facilty management.
3.24  The projects success depends on full community pardcipation in all stages of the development
process. In many project locations, the facilities provided would be the first significant public
investment affecting the community and would thus provide an opportunity to reach the women.
Seveal strategies would be used to fully involve women in the context of the community participation
including: (a) an institutional arrangement to enable project funding to reach the women's activities;
(b) specific criteria and procedures to ensure their full participatdon in the planning, design and
implementation stages of the project; (c) support of acdvitif s with a special interest to women,
including the I)WS and house garden iIngation components; and (d) use of community based
women's organizations to encourage women's participation. In addidon, the project would ensure
that the projecfs objectives and development opportnites are known to other government agencies
and to NGOs with programas focustd on women m the project locations.

4.01 Total project cost, including physical contingencies and expected price increases, is estimated

quantity and design variations have been assessed at up to a maximum of 10 percent of individual
item costs. Price contingencies assume increases in local currency costs of 5.5 percent per annum

1993 and thcreafter. Project cost estimates are summarized in Table 4.1 below and detailed by

4.03 Waos. The procurement under the project would be undertaken by the implementing
