whichever is lower. Small amounts of materials (US$ 0.9 million) which can be fabricated locally
(outlet valves, PVC storage tanks for DWS works, etc.) would be procured by LCB tenders let by
provincial project offices. Most ohrm  iAmnt (US$ 0.7 million), including office and survey
equipment and computers, would be required by project offices in each of the project provinces.
However, procurement of these goods would be centralized using quantities and specifications drawn
up by the project coordination unit after consultation with provincial prtect offices. The procurement
would be by LCB procedures acceptable to the Bank (US$ 0.4 miUlion) or by local shopping tUS$
0.3 million). Foreign firms would not be precluded from bidding, but would not generally be
interested in the goods to be procured under LCB procedures. Government regulations prohibit
importation of vehicles. Thus, vehicles (US$ 0.7 million) would be classified as reserved
procurement and not be financed under the Loan. An assurance was obtained from Government that
vehicles required for the project would be acquired under a schedule agreed with the Bank.

4.07 Review of Procurement. Ihe Bank would review only the equipment and materials contracts
estimated to cost US$ 200,000 equivalent or over before they are tendered and awarded. Thus prior
review is expected to cover about 16 contracts and about 95 percent of the goods by value. As works
will be procured following LOB procedures and their size would be small (less than US$ 1.0
million), these contacts would be subject to random post-award review. Contracts or works and
goods would be based on model bid documents approved by the Government and Bank. Invitation
documents, proposed awards and contracts for consultant services would be reviewed by the Bank.
Disbursements
4.08   JImlementation Period. Disbursements under the Loan are expected to be completed by
September 30, 1999, about six years after Board approval. The Loan closing date is December 31,
1999. The disbursement profile for past investment projects in the irrigadon subsector in Indonesia
has stretched over about eight years. However, the proposed project constitutes a six-year time slice
of an investment program, and groundwater development is not generally susceptible to the delays
experienced in compledng surface water irrigation schemes. The schedule of disbursements is given
in Annex 1, Table 3, and the detailed implementation schedules of the project components indicate



-21 -
that this disbursement schedule is feasible. The target areas for development in each of the provinces
are feasible in relation to the institutional implementation capacity in the provinces.
4.09  Lo  AlloQa on. The proposed allocation of the Loan and percentage of expenditures to be
financed are given in Annex 1, Table 4. Disbursements would be as follows: (a) 40 percent of
expenditures on civil works, including irrigation, domestic water supply and observation wells; (b)
100 percent of foreign expenditures (CIF) for directly imported equipment, 100 percent of local
expenditures (ex-factory) for locally manufactured equipment, and 65 percent of locally procured
items; (c) 91 percent of expenditures for surveys, investigation and design, agricultural support
services and training; (d) 50 percent of expenditures for institutional support services and initial
O&M; and (e) 100 percent of expenditures for consultant services and special studies.
4.10 An assurance was obtained from Government that, as conditions of continuing disbursement,
for the works and irrigation equipment and materials components, it would make annual budgetary
allocations sufficient to implement the agreed annual work programs in each of the project provinces
for the fiscal year 1994/95 and each subsequent fiscal year thereafter until project completion. In this
respect, Government stated at negotiations that budgetary provisions for FY 1993/94 would be
sufficient for the agreed provincial work programs in that fiscal year.
4.11  Retroactive financi;ng up to the amount of US$ 2.0 million equivalent would be available for
expenditures after February 14, 1993 to facilitate advance action under the project, including the
procurement of irrigation materials, survey and design and consultant services (Categories 2.1, 3.1
and 4.1 of Table 4, Annex 1). Expenditures on contracts for works valued at less than US$ 1.0
million and equipment valued at less than US$ 200,000, and works executed on a force account basis
would be disbursed against Statements of Expenditures (SOEs). Contracts disbursed under SOEs
would be based on standard bid documents approved by the Bank and disbursement for all other
expenditures would be on the basis of full documentation. Documentation for SOE expenditures
would not be submitted to the Bank for review, but would be retained by the project implementation
units and made available for inspection by Bank supervision missions. Disbursements against SOEs
are expected to amount to about US$ 20.7 million, or about 40 percent of the Loan amount.
4.12  Sgcial Accoun. In order to facilitate disbursements, a Special Account would be established
in Bank Indonesia to be maintained by the Director General Budget in the Ministry of Finance (4MOF).
It would be used for all eligible payments made against all disbursement categories. The Bank would
make an initial deposit equivalent to US$ 4.0 million into the Special Account (equivalent to an
estimated four months of project expenditures). A monthly statement on the Special Account would
be submitted to the Bank by the Director General Budget.
Accounts and Audits
4.13 For all Government agencies, each accounting section is headed by a qualified accountant who
reports monthly on budget and financial status. Internal audit sections visit project areas quarterly and
examine local accounting records. Accounting procedures now in place are adequate to support SOE
applications. Final accounts, including consolidated receipts and expenditures statements, are
prepared at year end and meet Bank requirements. Project accounts are subject to two levels of
audits: (a) by the Inspector Generals of the Ministries of Public Works (MPW) and Agriculture
(MOA); and (b) by the Fiancial and Development Supervisory Board (BPKP). Work of both
auditors has been found to be generally satisfactory on Bank-assisted irrigation and agricultural
projects.
4.14  Asswances were obtained that Govemment would cause each project executing agency at both
national and provincial levels to: (a) establish and maintain adequate and separate accounts from the
beginning of project implementation; and (b) maintain such accounts in accordance with sound and
generally recogniized accounting principles and practices satisfactory to the Bank, so as to enable these
agencies to provide annual financial statements that reflect the financial perfonnance and status of
project comonents. In addition, assurances were obtained that Government would:



-22-
(a)   keep all project related contracts, documents related to the SOEs, and orders and
receipts for at least one year after the Bank has received the audit report for the fiscal
year m which the last withdrawal from the loan account was made;
(b)   maintain the Special Account, including records and documentation of receipts and
withdrawals, as deemed satisfactory by the Bank;
(c)   complete consolidated annual financial statements of the project for independent audit
within six months of the close of each Government fiscal year, and
(d)   undertake independent audits of each year's project consolidated accounts, including
the audits of SOEs and the Special Account, and submiit such audit reports to the Bank
within nine months of the close of each Government fiscal year.
V. ORGANIZAT(IN AND MANAGEMENT
A. Background
5.01 Groundwater irrigadon development in the public sector has concentrated mainly on
construction of facilities. More attention needs to be paid to community participation in planning,
d esign and implementation of the works and to agricultural support services. Governmentpolicy is to
provide initial O&M support for completed tubewell facilities (para 3.09), but this actvity has
generaly been underfunded. Although efforts have been made to establish WUAs to manage
completed works, long term technical support to achieve potendal benefits needs to be substantially
strengthened. In addition, more formal arrangements are needed to sustain the tubewell systems in
the long-term, in particular for the replacement of pumping equipment. Moreover, activities related to
agricultural development generally have been less than satisfactory and performance could be
increased if the initatives of the beneficiaries were supplemented by stronger agricultural support In
some groundwater irrigation areas, such as Madura and Lombok, attempts have been made to counter
this failing by MOA/PRAS through provision of large consultancy services under the DGWRD. This
proach has been project specific and is likely to have limited impact since it is under DGWRD rather
tan the repctive PRAS.
5.02 To addrss these challenges, the project's institutional objectives are to: (a) concentrate public
sector support for O&M to the first two years after facilides are commissioned; (b) encourage and
enable effective community participation; (c) support agricultural development activities necessary to
achieve potential benefits; and (d) support multipurpose use of groundwater resources. To achieve
these objectives, the project would strengthen the agencies involved and their coordination at national
and provincial levels, and also make provisions for full community participation in all phases of the
development process.
5.03  The basic organizaonal structure to achieve project objectives is already in place (Chart 1).
The main technical agencies involved (described in para 1.12) are: (a) MPW for planning, design and
construction and support for the initial O&M of irrigation works and DWS facilities undertaken by
WUAs; (b) PRIS for continuing technical support to WUAs of irrigation systems; and (c) MOA and
PRAS for agricultr suppo services. Coordination of project activities related to women would be
through the Directote of Extension in MOA at national level and through PRAS at the provincial
leveL The project would use these existing institutional arrangements with appropriate strengthening
and support to implement the various project components, as required.
5.04 The coordination activities, including planning and integration of budget poposals, would be
undertaken at national and provincial levels by BAPPENAS (the national planning agency) and the
BAPPEDA (the provincial planning bodies) respectively. The Interagency Irrigation Committees
(HICs) at provincial, district and subdistrict levels would be oriented more towards groundwater
development than in the past, and used as the local coordination mechanisn. In this respect, tie
district level committee chaired by the district officer (Bupati, or his nominee) would have the most
important role. An assurance was obtained that Government would cause BAPPENAS and the



-23 -
BAPPEDA in each project province to undertake project coordinadon at national and provincial levels
respectively, in a manner satisfactory to the Bank.
B. PrjetC) r&Wnzation
5.05  Irrigation and DWS Works. The main implementing agency at national level would be the
Pengembangan Air Tanah (PAT), a subdirectorate of the DOI-II directorate of DGWRD within MPW,
(see Chart 1). PAT maintains subproject (P2AT) offices in all of the project provinces. The PAT and
its P2AT offices would be responsible for planning, design and construction of tubewell irrigation
systems, setting up of the WUAs and the initial O&M of the completed works. The P2AT offices
would also be responsible for construction of the domestic water supply (DWS) and house garden
irrigation facilities, as required (para 3.10). The PRIS would be responsible for supervision and
technlical support for the OM of completed tubewell systems after the initial O&M support by P2AT
units has terminated. The consultants would provide on-the-job training for the PAT and P2AT staff
in their specialist fields of activity (para 3.17).
5.06 In a recent reorganization, the P2ATs are now placed under PRIS direcdon. The objective is
to move towards treating groundwater irrigation development as a provincial activity rather then as a
subproject of a national project. In some provinces, the PRIS has seconded from its staff the P2AT
manager and other senior technical staff (irrigation enincm). The arrangement is a first stage in the
process of decentralization of groundwater irrigation development to the provinces by fully involving
P'RIS in any provincial program. However, at present, the administrative, technical and financial
control still remains with PAT at national level. The proposed project is thus timely, since an
essential prerequisite to strengthening the provincial role is to formulate standardized criteria,
processes and procedures to be used as the basis for groundwater development throughout Indonesia.
5.07 In this espect, an important step has been taken in East Java (which includes about 30 percent
of the proposed project area) to strengthen the provincial role in groundwater irrigation development
through the Governors Decree No. 700 (August 1992) and the related PRIS Decree on Technical
Guidance for the Groundwater (Irrigation) Management. These Decrees effectively divide
responsibility for groundwater (inigation) development into project and non-project activities. P2AT
would be responsible for groundwater development from the planning stage through to completion of
the initial support of O&M (two years after commissioning of a tubewell system). PRIS would be
responsible for long term support of WUAs after P2AT completes its initial O&M support
5.08 In addition, the East Java PRIS (Chart 3) has been given responsibility for monitoring and
evaluation of groundwater resources and for providing technical advice to the Governor with respect
to groundwater regulation (pam 5.19). To effectively implement the Decrees, the PRIS would have
to set up new units, or strengthen existing units, and make sufficient budgetary provisions to take on
the mandated tasks. PRIS has recognized that these are new responsibilities which would require
additional qualified staff, facilides, equipment and institutdonal support. PRIS would draw on P2AT
experience and resources during the transition period. However, technical assistance to PRIS would
be required to help develop new capability, particularly with regards to groundwater monitoring and
evaluation. The initiatives taken in East Java could be applied in all project provinces. They would
address the unresolved issues relating to sustaining groundwater development after WUAs take over
completely from P2AT, and provide a practical arrangement for the monitoring and management of
groundwater resources. G0I intends to use the East Java Governores Decree and the related PRIS
technical decree be used as models for similar decrees in the other project provinces. The project
would thus provide support for the East Java PRIS to enable it to undertake its newly mandated tasks
(para 3.16). A related project objective would be to test and demonstrate this new organizational
arrangement and enable any lessons learned to be utilized in the other project provinces.
5.09  Agulu=   Devetopmnt. The MOA, through the Directorate of Land Rehabilitation and
Development (DLRD), would be responsible at national level for the agricultural development
acdvities (Chart 2). The PRAS would be responsible for such activities at the provincial level, and
would be represented on the IIC at the provincial, district and subdistrict levels concemed with
proposed project areas. The main project acdvides would be to assist in: (a) setting up of WUAs;



- 24 -
(b) helping beneficiaries make the transition from rainfed to irrigated agriculture; (c) developing
market opportunities for specialized crops; and (d) setting up micro-enterprises to develop secondary
agricultural benefits such as house-gardens. These extension tasks would be primarily implemented
by the extension services which are organized through a tiered hierarchy extending from the center to
provincial, subprovincial, district and subdistrict levels. Existing extension arrangements would be
strengthened with special village level extension staff, who would serve only groundwater
development areas. The field staff would be supported by the existing specialist services within the
PRAS in each province. The consultants to MOA would provide general assistance, on-the-job
training and special courses, as required, for the MOA and PRAS staff in their specialist fields of
activity (para 3.17). An assurance was obtained that Government would cause MOA to provide
staffng, facilities, services and the other resources required to carry out agricultural extension and
support satisfactory to the Bank throughout aL the proposed project impact areas
5.10  Commnunity lPaticipation. To achieve the project objectives, a basic requirement would be full
and effective community participation in all phases of the development process (Annex 4). An
essential activity is to set up, train and technically support WUAs such that they are able to effectively
manage and sustaln the tubewell systems. The legal provisions (para 1.17), structure, responsibilities
and management arrangements of WUAs are detailed in Annex 11. Government groundwater
facilities are handed over to established WUAs for management immediately after completion of
construction, though legal ownership remains with Government. Although existing tubewell systems
are under WUA management, the performance of these associations has been mixed. Efforts would
be made under the project to strengthen new and existing WUAs including: (a) more attention to their
establishment, (b) provision of clearly defined initial O&M support from government for two years;
and (c) carefully supervision and monitoring of WUA performance.
5.11 The WUAs would be used as the nucleus body for other activities that can be organized to
achieve full socio-economic benefits (i.e., farmer groups for cooperative implementation of
agricultural development and activities related to the role of women). Community level activities
would be coordinated at the subdistrict level, with representation from existing community based
organizations and the agencies involved in the project. Consultant services, resource groups and
universities would be utilized, when appropriate, to assist in the implementation of community
development activities related to the project.
C. Proect Implementation
5.12 General. Improvements in the project management which would be given special attention
under the project include: (a) quality of planning and design of the development process; (b) facility
completion and the handover to WUAs; (c) the management and sustainability of facilities by WUAs;
(d) groundwater resource management and regulation; (e) project monitoring and evaluation; and (f)
annual implementation reviews and project reporting.
5.13  Scheme eparon Analyses and Documentation. Major technical objectives of the project
are to: (a) provide the lowest cost type of groundwater irrigation facility that is technically appropriate
to the hydrogeological conditions in any area; and (b) construct facilities that are suitable for handover
for management by the beneficiaries through properly established WUAs. In this respect, areas
suitable for development by privately financed SIW facilities would be screened out of the program,
thus avoiding untoward Government expenditures. These objectives would be achieved under the
project by initial screening of all proposed development areas to decide the most appropriate type of
facility before detailed design is undertaken, and by following procedures based on standardization of
designs, including materials and equipment used (Annex 5). The technical decision on the type of
groundwater irrigation facility most appropriate for any subproject area would be made by the
specialist staff of the concerned P2AT office with technical advice from the provincial consultancy
support team
5.14  Arrangements for Multipurpose Use of Groundwater. A feature of the project would be to
provide formal anrangements for multipurpose use of the groundwater facilities developed primarily
for irrigation. The main other uses would be domestic water supply (DWS) and irrigation service for



-25 -
house gardens. Such developments would be possible wherever the location of the well makes this
viable and when multipurpose developments are requested by the community to be served. The
development process would be to: (a) evaluate the potential demand for multipurpose use when siting
a well; and (b) if viable, incorporate multipurpose use into the detailed planning and design of an
irrigation facility. If a DWS facility is required, the planning and design criteria (including water
quality standards) and procedures would be compatible with standards set under the proposed Water
Supply and Sanitation Project for Low Income Families (WSSPLIC). The standards and designs for
DWS facilities would also be agreed with Cipta Karya within MPW at the center and with the
provincial authority responsible for provision of rural water supplies.
5.15  ProJect Development Process. The project consists of a large number of discrete tubewell
systems each with similar technical characteristics and management requirements. To date, technical
support and direction from PAT at national level has been less than satisfactory, primarily because
development has been tackled on an individual project basis with no uniform arrangements to
standardize the development process. To address this issue, the development process for tubewell
systems would be formalized and the staffing at field level would be strengthening to ensure that the
process can be effectively implemented (Annex 4). The four stages of the development process
would be: (a) pr -rnaration to decide where each tubewell (subproject) program would be sited
after satisfying technical groundwater and agricultural determinants and taking into account general
provincial and local development priorities; (b) pm  iQn including the detailed planning and design
of tubewell clusters and individual tubewell systems; (c) impementadon when the tubewell systems
are constructed; and (d) O&M (post construction) from commissioning of tubewells to deliver an
irrigation service.
5.16 Important steps in the implementation stage would be: (a) production testing after completion
of the well to confm that the water supply is adequate and to select the suitable pump units; and (b)
pressure testing of buried pipe conveyance systems for leaks before back filling of trenches. An
essential requirement of the concerned P2AT would be to formally test the facilities and to certify that
they are fully operational before they are handed over to their WUAs. Thus, an important part of the
commissioning process, DGWRD/PRIS would verify and certify that all groundwater development
schemes are complete and can function as designed before handing over to the WUAs for initial O&M
(para 3.09). This certification process would substantially reduce the risks during the initial O&M
support period, since it would technically confilrm that tubewell facilities are efficient and effective
before such facilities are handed over to the WUAs.
5.17  Establishment and Suport of WUAs. Under the development process, a critical step would
be to identify the command areas that individual facilities co"'1d serve and thus identify the potential
beneficiary groups. Only after wells have been successfully .ested and actual tubewell command
areas is decided, can the formal establishment of WUAs for the facility be undertaken (Annex 11).
Once beneficiaries are selected, the WUA would be formally set up with the assistance of staff from
the WUA support unit of P2AT. This would include establishment of the constitution, election of
WUA officers and definition of operational bylaws. The establishment of a WUA would include full
beneficiary participation so that potential beneficiaries understand the responsibilities and benefits
from their mutual decision. Thus, the sequence of activities relating to establishment of WUAs would
be, in effect, a training activity to be undertaken by the WUA support unit within each P2AT.
5.18 Training. Elected officers of WUAs would need training in groundwater irrigation facility
management, including routine technical supervision, bookkeeping, and other record keeping relating
to the O&M of the facilities. Specialized training would be required for tubewell operators in general
operation of the pumping equipment, routine maintenance and executing simple repairs, and also in
operational record keeping. All beneficiaries on a facility would have to understand the principles of
rotational water supply if the service is to be shared equitably and delivered in a timely manner.
Training would also be required to initiate newly recruited subprofessional WUA field staff in the
techniques of their craft. Seminars would be conducted at regular intervals (with the IIC) at
provincial, district and subdistrict levels to keep members updated on development strategies and
experiences under the project. This training for WUA officials and on-the-job training of project staff
would be provided with the assistance of the project consultants.



-26-
5.19  Manaymet of Groundwatnr Resources, Idonesia's groundwater resource management and
regulation requirements, aimed at safe exploitation of the resources and avoidance of their polludon,
are described in Annex 7. The Government Regulation No. 22 on Water Management, (1982) gives
provinces the implicit right to allocate groundwater within their Jurisdiction through the requirement
that all groundwater use, except for domestic purposes, shall be licensed by the provincial authorities.
Under the law, technical oversight and approval of groundwater development is vested with the
Ministry of Mines and Energy (MME) which has delegated this power to its Directorate of
Environmental Geology (DEG). However, DEG is not staffed or provided with budget to carry out
this mandate. It must rely on information provided by the provinces for its technical evaluadons, but
the provinces also lack technical expertise in groundwater evaluation.
5.20 At present, the most serious problems relating to management of groundwater resources, in
terms of both water quality and quantity, are in the urban areas, notably in Jakarta and some cities in
West Java. However, the rate of development of groundwater for irrigation, particularly in East Java,
gives cause for concern because it is not being regulated on the basis of technical information. These
aspects of groundwater management have not been treated adequately by the PATIP2AT organizations
which were established mainly to construct projects. The necessary data base can be provided only
by establishing groundwater monitoring networks together with data evaluation units so that any
adverse effects of development can be identified (and responded to) in a timely manner.
5.21 Provincial licensing is almost entirely directed at industrial and conmmercial uses which attract
a user fee based on metered abstractions. These activities are located mainly in urban centers and
there is strong evidence that licensing provisions are being circumvented by many of these users.
Block licenses are provided by the provinces for public financed groundwater development for
irrigation and an abstracdon charge is not levied. Essentially all private groundwater development for
irrgation (located almost endrely on Java) is unlicensed. This will be extremely difficult to control
since the only fixed asset is a hand drilled (or dug) hole in the ground which can be installed at very
low cost. Pumps are mobile and are moved between wells, often under rental arrpngements.
5.22 The problems of regulation of abstraction and pollution control are now mainly in the cities,
paricularly in Jakarta where overdraft and/or pollution of aquifers is a major concern. Involvement in
this aspect of regulation is beyond the scope of the proposed project. There is as yet no evidence for
over-development of groundwater due to irrigation use. Neither are adverse effects on water levels in
village water supply wells due to groundwater irrigation pumping commonly reported. Nevertheless,
overdraft could occur in some areas in the future if groundwater development for irrigation is not
properly regulated. Thus, as a first step, the project would make provision to monitor groundwater
resources in subproject areas to ensure that existing hand pumps and open wells are not adversely
affected by project tubewells and overdevelopment is avoided.
5.23  Monitoring and Evaluti-on. Monitoring and evaluation (M&E) of the development impact
generally has been poor on previous groundwater irrigation development projects. Exceptions are the
Bank/Italian cofinanced development on Lombok under ISSP and the ODA/EEC financed projects on
Madura, both of which were afforded very large consultancy inputs. Most monitoring has
concentrated on physical and financial progress of project implementation. However, reliable
information on irrigation and agricultural performances of facilities must also be collected to
effectively evaluate the developmental impact. Procedures for keeping records by WUAs (para 5.18),
if properly implemented, would provide useful facility utilization data, but collection of adequate and
reliable data on cropped areas, inputs and crop yields has always been beyond the capability of
WUAs. This information must therefore be collected by the PRAS through seasonal prog. -  is on a
sample basis. The survey programs would be devised with the assistance of the project consultants
(para 3.17) who would also assist with the collation, evaluation and reporting on the data collected.
An understanding was reached with Govemment that it would cause all project agencies to monitor
and evaluate prject activites and perfonmance according to standards satsfactory to the Banlc
5.24  Piogrogs Review and  oring. Semi-annual progress reports would be prepared for each
project province, collated at national level by DGWRJ and MOA, and subnmtted to the Bank for



- 27 -
review and commrient. Each report would contain a brief description of the main events during the
reporting period including: (a) construction progress; (b) expenditures incurred; (c) project staffing
status; (d) tender and award of contracts; (e) functional irrigation area developed; (f) agricultural
performance, as applicable, and (g) other items of note. Thus, the reports would summarize key
performance indicators for all aspects of the development process. In addition, a Project Coompletion
Report (in standard Bank format) would be submitted by Government to the Bank no later than six
months after project completion.
5.23 Annual Implementation Review. An assurance was obtained that Government would
undertake an annual implementation review of the project not later than June 30 each year,
commencing June 30 1994, so that the findings can be reflected in the provincial work programs for
the following fiscal year. The annual review arrangement would give flexibility to, inter alia: (a)
amend individual provincial programs to reflect performance during the previous year, (b) evaluate
'expansion' schemes which can be promoted to 'go-ahead' schemes, based on performance data from
pilot development areas; (c) consider any new scheme areas not ncluded in the original program; and
(d) incorporate any lessons learned in the course of the previous year. The proposed work program
through FY 93/94 was reviewed and would be agreed at negotiations. The first annual review would
take place in 1993 to define the FY 94/95 work program. The project consultants (para 3.17) would
assist in the preparation and collation of information for the review, and the responsible provincial
agencies would ensure that necessary performance data are made available.
5.26 Budget Procedures. Present procedures for funding groundwater irrigation development,
including agricultural support services, need to be strengthened. Uncertainties in budget allocations
and delays in release of budgets make planning and implementation of work programs difficult,
particularly activites pertaining to tendering and awarding works contracts. Procurement of materials
and equipment, of prime importance in groundwater development programs, can also be delayed by
untimely release of budget allocations. Government budgetary procedures cannot be changed for a
specific Bank-financed project. However, the project would review and agree on annual work
programs and budget proposals well in advance of the fiscal year. This would allow BAPPENAS
and BAPPEDA to plan appropriate project funding to be r,leased in a timely manner.
VI. PRODUCrION. MARKEIG. FARM INCOMES AND COST RECOVERY
Agricultural Production and Markleting
6.01 The project consists of groups of individual groundwater irrigation facilities scattered in a
number of scheme areas in each of the eleven provinces targeted for development. They therefore
present a wide range of climatic and soil conditions, cropping patterns and farmer capability. The
common feature is the present lack of irrigation in the areas to be developed with crop production
being entirely rainfed under the 'without' project situation 1/ Other features are the small land
holdings (particularly on Java) and the related financial risks that small farmers must take in the
transition from rainfed to irrigated agriculture. These will include raising funds for crop production
inputs and the O&M costs for the irrigation service. Successfully making this transition is the basic
challenge of the poverty alleviation objective of the project.
6.02 Cropping Intensities. Present cropping intensities are generally in the range of 140-180
percent. Exceptions are some very low rainfall areas on Lombok and in Central Sulawesi with
present cropping intensities of about 70 percent. Dry season cropping occurs mainly during the first
diy season using residual moisture and some rain. Where rainfall and soil conditions are suitable, the
wet season crop of choice is paddy. In some areas with low rainfall and light soils, wet season
cropping consists of palawija crops, typically maize, groundnut, mungbean and soybean, sometimes
with less then 100 percent of the area cultivated. The first dry season crop under present conditions is
1/ Excepuiomn a    mareas wifin proposed schemes which produce vegetables with hand irgation fomn dug weUls, for
example in the Sape and Keli subproject areas on Sumbawa Island. In addition, some schemes may be located in the
tai ends of surface water irrigation systems where water supplies are available. but very insecure.



-28 -
aiainly palawija, except in some parts of Java and Sumatra where rainfall is sufficient to attempt a
second rice crop. Provision of reliable groundwater irrigation would have the following effects: (a)
increase in cropping intensity during the first dry season; (b) a shift to paddy cultivation in the wet
season L some areas where paddy is not now practiced; (c) possible introduction or expansion of
paddy cultivation during the first dry season; (d) extension of cropping into the second dry season;
and (e) higher crop yields due to reliable irrigation supplies, together with increased use of other
production inputs. Reliable irrigation would also encourage farmers to cultivate higher value crops,
including vegetables for home consumption and sale whenever markets are available. Estimates of
the potential project impact on agricultural production have been derived from field observations in
proposed new areas for groundwater development and by surveys of existing groundwater schemes.
6.03 The impact of the project on crop production is estimated by province in Annex 2, Table 2.
Under the future 'without' project scenario, little or no change in cropping is foreseen. In the 'future
with' project situation, cropping intensities are expected to increase up to about 280 percent in most
areas. There would be 100 percent cover of paddy in the wet season with irrigation used to
supplement rainfall. Where feasible, most farmers would prefer to grow a second paddy crop on at
least part of their land during the first dry season. It is anticipated that the paddy cropping would be
almost entirely of improved varieties. Some farmers are expected to introduce or expand the
cultivation of high value crops during both dry seasons, (e.g., shallots, chillies, watermelons,
tobacco, etc.). The crop choice would be decided by marketing opportunities. Other farmers would
use the dry season irrigation supplies to grow more of the traditional palawija crops (maize,
groundnut, mungiean, and soybean). The estimated total crop areas 'with' and 'without' the project
are summarized below:

6.04 Water Requirements. The irrigation requirements of the assumed 'future with' project
cropping patterns for each of the subproject areas have been derived by extrapolation from existing
studies and projects, using appropriate records for estimating effective rainfall and other climatic
determinants and taking account of local soil conditions (Annex 8). For estimating peak irrigation
demands, the 80 percent probable rainfall has been used with appropriate adjustment to effective
rainfall. Average pumping requirements to serve the assumed cropping patterns have been estimated
on the basis of the 50 percent probable rainfall. They indicate that project cropping patterns can be
served under the water duties adopted for the design of the irrigation supplies with the assumed
maximum daily pumping hours and system irrigation efficiencies. However, there are clear
indications from already completed and well established schemes, that actual pumping hours are
considerably lower than the calculated requirements with apparendy no significant loss of crop
production. In addition, project tubewells are only assumed to run for about 18 hours per day at
maximum demand periods, whereas pump units can safely be operated for up to 22 hours per day for
short periods. It may therefore be feasible to decrease the irrigation design duty and/or increase
maximum running hours per day. Both these changes in design assumptions would effectively
increase the command area which could be served by a typical tubewell syste . The implications of
these potential design changes are discussed in the sensitivity analyses (para 7.11 and Annex 2).
Thus, one important objective of project monitoring and evaluation would be to confirm whether the
project planning and design assunptions are too conservative with respect to design water duties.
6.05  C    Yields. Use of improved varieties of rice and of chemical fertilizers are well established
throughout Indonesia. Fann surveys indicate that improved technologies for rice production have



- 29 -
been adopted in mo¢e than 90 percent of the irigated area. This pattern can be expected to be repeated
for rice production when groundwater irrigation is available. Present average yields of wet season
rice under rainfed conditions range from 2.5 to 3.5 tons/ha (dry, unhusked paddy) between
subproject areas and provinces, reflecting variations in wet season rainfall conditions. Full
development paddy yields under the project are expected to increase to 4.5 to 5.0 tons/ha, depending
on the locality. Palawija field crop yields now range from 1.5 to 1.8 tons/ha for maize, about 0.8
tons/ha for soybean and some 0.7 tons/ha for groundnuts, wlhen cultivated in the dry season without
irigation. Ful project development yields are expected to average about 3.0 tons/ha for maize, 1.3
tons/ha for soybean and 1.2 tons/ha for groundnuts. High value crops and their expected 'with'
project yields include: 7 tons/ha for shallots, 4.5 tons/ha for chili and some 0.9 tons/ha for tobacco
(dry leaves).
6.06 Maketing. Annual incremental production under the project at full development is estimated
at about 83,000 tons of paddy, 33,000 tons of palawiya crops (maize, soybeans, groundnuts) and a
substantial volume of high value crops. This is not expected to cause marketing problems. A
significant proportion of the increased rice production would be consumed by the producers or in the
immediate vicinity of production area, thus improving dietary and nutritional standards. In addition,
about 10 percent of all marketed rice in Indonesia is acquired by Government, creating a dependable
marketing opportunity. Any marketable surplus is expected to be sold by the producers as paddy to
private traders or millers. Numerous private traders operate in the viUages, while village cooperative-
owned rice mills provide competition and offer services such as drying and husking.
6.08 Marketing of bulk palawija crops such as maize, soybean and groundnuts is handled almost
entirely by private traders and weil established marketing channels exist. Shallot and chilli (which can
be dried for storage) are not particularly perishable vegetable crops and can be transported over long
distances. Significant amounts of these crops usually can be marketed locally from roadside stalls
and in village markets by the producers, or through middle men into markets in nearby towns.
Experience indicates that, if significant marketable surpluses of shallot and chilli are produced, larger
scale middle men are atuacted and are willing to transport the produce in quantity over long distances
(even inter-island, as evidenced by the shallot and chili markets developed on Sumbawa). Perishable
vegetables, such as tomatoes,would be marketed through outlets in towns and villages in close
vicinity to the production area. The project would support the development of marketing of high
value crops under the community participation and agriculturl development components, with special
attention to the role of women. One advantage of groundwater irrigation development is that the
facilities provide a flexible 'on demand' water source. Thus, if opportunites change because of crop
prices, or other factors, farmers under the project would be able to respond quickly to the varying
market pressures.
6.09  F1ncialr Ed. Rice prices in Indonesia are largely determined by local supply and demand.
Although there are price fluctuations between locations, seasons and years, prices in the open market
are generally higher than Government's floor prices. The major instruments influencing rice
production in the past have been irrigation investments by the public sector, subsidies on production
chemicals, and price policy through trade regulations. However, fertilizer subsidies are now being
phased out and pesticide subsidies have been eliminated. Prices for palawija field crops (maize,
soybean and groundnuts) also are largely determined by local markets and are not subject to
Government intervention. Vegetable crops are subject to large price fluctuations on a seasonal and
annual basis, reflecting conditions ranging trom scarcity to glut in the local markets. Financial prices
for all commodities used in the project analysis have taken these variables into account and are based
on farm surveys conducted in the proposed development areas during project preparation and on
information provided by MOA and the M&E unit within DGWRD. Prices have been adjusted, as
necessary, to reflect farmgate prices observed during appraisal. Financial prices assumed for the
most importnt farm-related commodities are given in Annex 2, Table 1.
6.10 Farrn Budgeta. Assessment of project impact on farm incomes was made in a sample of
twenty situaions to reflect a range of proposed development areas to test 'without' and 'with' project



-30-
conditions. The determinants taken into consideration include: (a) agricultural production costs and
incomes; (b) costs related to the type of irrigation facility; and (c) pumping requirements to support
the assumed 'with' project cropping patterns. Farm budgets for typical holdings of 0.5 ha were
prepared for each of the subproject areas on the basis of assumed average cropping patterns and crop
yields. The incremental farm income tested is that at full development, which on average, can be
reached in about four years after commissioning a facility. Estimates of farm income are calculated
after allowing for the value of the farmers' own labor, thus indicating incremental incomes after the
farmers have been rewarded for their additional efforts. The results of the analyses, detailed in Annex
2, Table 4, indicate that net incomes from 0.5 ha farms are projected to rise from about Rp 40,000 to
Rp 300,000 in the 'without' project situation to about Rp 370,000 to Rp 700,000 at full development.
In all cases tested, farmers should have the cash flow to make the transition from rainfed to irrigated
agriculture.
6.11  The above estimates refer only to the farm activities in areas served by new irrigation facilities.
However, most farm families are involved in other economic activides because of the small average
farm sizes. Such 'off-farm' activities can include: (a) cultivating land outside the command area of
the tubewell; (b) working as laborers on other farmer's land; and (c) by engaging in non-agrcultual
employment. A benchmark survey conducted in 1988-90 in Lombok revealed that about 80 percent
of the farm families earned income from activities outside the command area and that the 'pre-project'
income from land under command of the wells was only about 12 percent of total family income. It
also showed that some 25 percent of the command area was farmed by tenant farmers.
6.12 A village study in Java showed that about 75 percent of the households meet some or all of
their subsistence needs from non-farming activities. Of about 480 households, 6 percent owned more
than half the rice cropped area, 40 percent had holdings too small to produce their basic rice
requirements, and 37 percent had no land under paddy cultivation. This study showed that some 20
percent of household income and some 40 percent of calorific requirements came from house garden
land (pekarangan). Thus, the project makes specific provision to reach the less advantaged
households in or near the tubewell command areas through support of house garden irrigation. The
financial impact of these initiatives cannot be calculated at present, but they are expected to be
substantial for pooar househo'ds and, in particular, the landless.
O&A FuanLand CostRecg
6.13   -&,M Funding. At present, Government is faced with funding of O&M on some 4.4 M ha of
surface water irrigated lands and about 0.6 M ha of reclaimed swamp lands. The policy is to
gradually reduce this burden by turning over of all surface water schemes of less than 500 ha for
management by WUAs, and having WUAs on large schemes take responsibility for O&M of tertiary
canal systems. The policy for the last decade has been to hand over Government financed tubewell
facilides to their WUAs for management as they are completed, albeit with government support for
O&M for the first two years of fuel supply and tubewell operators' salary. These condidtions would
continue with regard to all groundwater irrigation facilities developed under the project. Thus, the
WUAs woud finance the full O&M costs of facilities from the third year after conumissioning
6.14  Cost Reey. Government would finance all initial capital costs of any facility apart from
land acquired, which would be procured by a levy on the beneficiaries and become a common
property of the WUA. The project specifically targets hard core poverty areas and thus in order to
enhance the financial viability of the new facilites, capital costs would not be recovered from the
be->~ -aries. In addition, Government's initial support to O&M would be regarded as a subsidy
(j,a 6.13) which would not be recovered from the beneficiaries 1/. This initial O&M support would
substantially reduce the risks associated with the transition from rainfed to irrigated agriculture by
providing a full and guaranteed irrigation service. However, during this initial two year period,
I/ Govemment policy of phasing out of Bank Indonesia's liquidity credit to rural institutions has raised the cost of
boffowing to 25-30 percent per annum and essentially eliminated the possibility of using medium term credit as a
means for beneficiary contnbution to capital costs of facilities (see para 2.23).



-31-
farmers would be expected to pay their WUA for water, generally after crop harvest, with accrued
income placed in a reserve fund for any long term major repair costs. The WUAs would be
encouraged to use these savings for short term as an agricultural credit source for the group
beneficiaries.
6.15 WUAs would be paying all O&M vosts from the third year after a facility is commissioned.
These costs would be recovered from the WUA members in the form of a water charge relating to the
irrigation service rendered. The WUA generally charge farmers by the hour (equivalent to a
volumetric basis) since the well discharge is constant and each farmer taking the full well supply in
turn. The water charges set by WUAs at a level sufficient to cover routine O&M expenditures,
including any repairs to facilities, plus some savings so that funds are accumulated in WUA accounts
for the eventual replacement of at least the pumping equipment. With respect to replacement
requirements, the life span of wells are expected to average at least 15 years, pumps should not need
replacing for at least 10 years, and pump houses and distbution systems can be maintained in
perpetuity. An assurance was obtained that Government would cause WUAs to bear full cost of
O&M in project financed groundwater schemes from the third year after a facility is comnissioned.
6.16 Other users of tubewell water (domestic water supply and house garden irnigation) would pay
at rates set by WUAs to reflect incremental O&M costs of the service provided. In addition, there
would be indirect cost recovery through increase in land tax (PBB) once the land status changed from
rainfed to irrigated.
VII. PROJECT JUSTIFCATION AND RISKS
PDjec ZBenfit and Bnfcae
7.01  This project complements previous Bank support to irrigation development on Java and the
Outer Islands. The development of about 25,000 ha would be almost entirely for new irrigation,
generally located in areas where poverty is prevalent. The screening process into fimn 'go-ahead'
schemes (about 62 percent of planned area) and 'expansion' schemes (38 percent of planned area),
together with the annual implemer. ation review, would initmize risks of failure.
7.02 The project consists of many small irigation facilities which have short construction periods,
generally less than one year from start of construction to commissioning. Thus, the generation of
benefits is relatively rapid as compared to surface irrigation schemes. In addition, groundwater
irrigation facilities can offer an essentially 'on demand' iigation service which gives considerably
flexibility to change cropping patterns as necessary. This will enable farmers to quickly response to
changes in crop prices or market opportunities.
7.03 Primary benefits of the project would be increased production of rice, palawija field crops
(maize, soybean, mungbean, groundnut and tobacco) and vegetables of various kinds. The main
beneficiares would be farmers with land within the command areas of the irrigation facilities.
However, the rural community in the immediate vicinity of any facility would have the potential
benefit of potable domestic water supply and irrigation for house gardens.
7.04 Major benefits at full development would be would be annual incremental production of about
83,000 tons of paddy, about 16,000 tons of maize, about 9,000 tons of soybean, about 8,000 tons of
groundnut, and an important increase in production of various vegetable crops (including onion,
chilli tomato, longbean, eggplant) and tobacco. About 50,000 farm families (some 300,000
persons) would benefit directly from the new areas brought under irrigation. The equivalent of about
20,000 full-time farm jobs are also expected to be created, mainly as a result of cropping
intensificaton. Since the project schemes would be spread through eleven provinces (of which nine
are in the Outer Islands), the development should generate significant indirect benefits, particularly in
the more isolated areas. The project would alleviate poverty by generating additional labor demand,
mainly benefiting the landless, and by increasing agricultural incomes of small farners who are now
dependent on rainfed production.



- 32 -
Economic Justification
7.05 Economic evaluations have been carried out using internal economic rate of return (ERR)
calculations and net present value (NPV) estimations. The assumptions and calculations used in the
economic evaluations are summarized in Annex 2. The ERR calculations have been made for all
subproject areas and types of groundwater irrigation facilities proposed under the project, on the
assumption that all groundwater potential in the 'expansion' areas would eventually be developed, or
an equivalent area developed elsewhere under the project.
7.06 Cost streams include all capital and operating costs. They include physical contingencies, but
exclude price contingencies and transfer payments such as taxes and subsidies. They are based on
detailed cost estimates which are summarized in Annex 3. The costs of consultancy services have
been attributed to the different subproject areas pro rata to the development areas. An allowance has
been made in the physical contingencies to account for unsuccessful wells. Well construction costs
also include an allowance for depreciation and rehabilitation of well drilling and testing equipment and
assumes that wells would generally be constructed using P2AT equipment under contract
arrangements. Administrative overheads are estimated at a flat rate of 5 percent of all investment
costs. The costs of institutional support activities (M&E, formation of WUAs, etc.) and agricultural
support services have been defined and included pro rata to developed area in each subproject.
7.07 Agricultural benefits have been evaluated for all subproject areas, taking account of local
physical determinants (rainfall, soils, etc.) in the subproject areas. Benefits are expressed in terms of
the value of incremental crop production. A buildup to full benefits over four years is assumed in all
schemes. Benefit steams do not take into account benefits from house garden irrigation, or social
benefits from domestic water supply facilities. Additional benefits from improved coordination of
irrigated agricultural development, technical assistance and other institutional support activities cannot
be quantfied directly to individual schemes, but would contribute to the project impact. In particular,
-echnical assistance and institutional strengthening activities are expected to generate incremental
benefits from existing groundwater development in the vicinity of the project facilities.
7.08 The ERR for the overall project is estimated at about 14.5 percent, though this indicator has
relatively limited significance as all schemes have their own particular characteristics of costs and
benefits. The ERRs range from 11.4 percent (DTW facilities in Madura, East Java) to 17.1 percent
(lTTW facilities in Besuki, East Tava). As can be expected, IlTW facilities generally have higher
ERRs (with a range of 12.0 per.... ; to 17.7 percent) than DTW facilities (with a range of 11.4 percent
to 13.8 percent). However, a p...,ning criteria is that DTW would only be proposed in areas where
ITTWs are not technically viable. Annex 2, Table 5 gives the base ERRs for all subproject areas,
together with present net value (NPV). This table also shows that the investment costs vanes from
some Rp 4.0 million per ha to Rp 6.4 million per ha and that net annual benefits range from about Rp
0.9 million per ha to Rp 1.2 million per ha.
7.09 Sensitivitv Ar-ses. Analyses were carried out for all schemes to test the sensitivity of their
ERRs to variations in investment costs and direct benefits as described in Annex 2. The tests reflects
the variations in costs and benefits which can be expected from groundwater irrigation development
with a large number of separate tubewell systems spread over areas with different physical and social
determinants. Cost changes of +10 percent and +20 percent were tested, as were the impacts of
benefit reductions of the same magnitude (Annex 2, Table 6). Reduction in expected benefits (-10
percent and -20 percent) reflects what could occur due to changes in cropping patterns, or cropping
intensities as farmers respond to crop pricing and marketing opportunities. In addition, a delay of one
year in achieving benefits has been tested to reflect: (a) possible delays in the farmers making the
transition from rainfed to irrigated agriculture; and (b) the specific targeting of poor farmers who may
be more risk averse than assumed. The test results emphasize the attention needed during the post-
construction phase of the development. Sensitivity to delays in implementation were not tested
because each facility is an operational entity and there is litdle nisk that delays in the total construction
period would exceed one year.



- 33 -
7.10  'fhe premise that project costs and benefits streams are based on conservative planning and
design assumptions was also tested. For example, an analysis tests that pumping hours may be based
on conservative estimates of the irrigation requirements (para 6.04) and that fanners may tend to more
efficiently irrigate when they must pay for water. This test shows that if pump running hours are
decreased by some 20 percent (and benefits remain the same), the ERR increases by about one
percentage point. Another analysis tests project design assumptions which may be conservative,
namely that: (a) maximum pump running hours are assumed at only 18 hours per day; and (b) that
average well discharges could be higher than assumed if wells were designed more efficiently. Both
these design changes, if adopted, would have the effect of decreasing the capital costs per unit area of
a project facility and/or reducing the running hours per annum for typical wells (thus reducing O&M
costs). For example, with respect to item (a) above, if running hours are increased to a maximum of
22 hours a day for short periods, the command area served (and thus benefits) could be increased by
about 20 percent. This would increase the estimated ERRs for all tubewell systems by up to some
four percentage points. With respect to item (b) above, there is evidence that, on average, discharges
are not fully optimized when the well point is constructed and tubewell command areas are not
designed to reflect actual well point discharge (after the well is developed and tested and before the
buried pipe distribution system is constructed). No data exists to quantify the effect of these
optimizations of tubewell design, but both changes would improve the average ERRs of subproject
schemes.
7.11 The above tests have shown that ERRs are fairly stable against the tested levels of cost
increases, while ERRs are more sensitive to the tested range of benefit reductions. Test results also
indicate that estimated ERRs may fall below 10 percent in a few isolated subproject areas if benefits
are reduced by 20 percent, or cost is increased by 20 percent. These low values relate in most cases
to the unlikely event that the capital costs per unit area of DTW facilities would be higher than
anticipated. However, the risks of these events are small since project is phased into 'go-ahead' and
'expansion' programs which ensure that acceptable performance on existing tubewell systems be
obtained before marginal impact areas are developed. In addition, the analysis indicates that the
project ERRs can be substantially improved if more attention is given to the optimizing the planning
and design of groundwater development. For example, if through improvements in design, the
command area served (and thus benefits) were increased by 30 percent, the ERRs would increase by
some eight percentage points. To test these opportunities, a priority task of the project consultants
would be to advise DGWRD on technical viability of possible changes in design assumptions.
Appropriate improvements in design can be expected as the project proceeds.
Risks and Uncertainties
7.12 The main project risks are generally known from groundwater components under previous
projects. They relate to selection of viable subproject areas, quality control of all stages of the
development process, and delayed or incomplete agriculturl support The project has been conceived
and designed to address and minimize specific risks as described below.
7.13 The institutional risks include inability to keep the organizational arrangements envisaged in
place and inadequate staffing of agencies at provincial level, particularly in the Outer Islands.
Previously there has been over-reliance on technical assistance, unpredictable and inadequate funding,
and ineffective coordination of government support agencies, particularly for the post-construction
activides related to irrigated agricultural development. Specific actions proposed to address these
risks include: (a) an extended project period; (b) a phased and flexible work programs subject to
annual reviews (c) improved institutional coordination; (d) focused technical assistance, institutional
support and training; (e) strengthening of monitoring and evaluation; and (f) improvements to
implementation and procurement procedures. With respect to item (b), the proje_t solution would be
to contain the risks by control of funding through disbursement conditionalities.
7.14 The technical risks are relatively small since technologies used have been tested and proven.
Although proposed technologies are generally accepted, they have not been applied systematically. In
those provinces where groundwater development is still limited, risks would be contained by the
flexiplan approach of categorizing the project area to reflect the extent of the hydrogeological



- 34-
unknowns, as described in para 3.04. This planning approach would reduce the risk of undisbursed
loan amounts since work programs can be transferred between provinces if expansion areas have to
be deleted. The proect would also contain technical risks by: (a) careful screening of the planned
irrigation facilites; (b) standardizadon of desig criteria and implementation procedures, (c) specific
attention to quality control and monitoring and evaluation of the technical performance of completed
works; and (d) fuHl community participation throughout the development process. An allowance for
failed wells in the cost estimates covers the risk of individual well fai: rc at construction.
7.15  Project economic viability mainly depends on containing costs and achieving potental benefits
in a timely manner. Thus, the socio-econmic risks would reflect that, in some areas on the Outer
Islands, the project technology has not yet been tested and demonstrated as acceptable to the fanning
population. In addition, some WUAs may not take up tubewell management as planned, and
individual farmers may not fully utilize the irrigation service provided. To contain these risks, the
project would: (a) support full community participation at all stages of the development process; (b)
monitor performance of newly developed areas to confirm farmers commitment and response before
expanding programs; (c) provide detailed assistance with establishment of WUAs followed by
support and guidance, particularly during the initial O&M period; and (d) provide agricultural services
with specific support for the transitional stage from rainfed to irigated agriculture. The project also
supports activities to generate social and secondary benefits (domestic water supply and house
gardens, etc.) which should encourage strong community commitment and promote self help.
7.16 The enironmental risks associated with the project are small. The schemes would all be
located on land already developed for agriculture and, therefore, no land clearance would be involved.
Unlike much surface water irrigadon development, there is no risk with groundwater irrigation
development of inducing water logging and associated secondary soil salinization. In fact,
groundwater developments should improve overall drainage conditions in their impact areas. The
project would support groundwater resource monitoring and evaluation and strengthen institutions
inVolved in groundwater regulation and licensing. Screening and planning criteria would ensure
sitng of irrigation we}ls would avoid negative effects on existing village water supplies areas, where
the groundwater balances are in jeopardy, or where salt water intrusion could be induced. In this
respect, most project schemes in the Outer Islands are located in areas where groundwater is
undeveloped apart from small abstractions for conumunity water supplies. Positive environmental
impacts would include the provision of potable water supplies to communities in the vicinities of the
groundwater irrigation facilities (by formal or informal anrangements) and general improvements to
the social well-being of populations in areas where poverty is common. The overall environmental
impact of the project would be positive.

the audits of SOEs and the Special Account, and submit such audit reports to the Bank within
nine months of the close of each Government fiscal year (para 4.14);
(h) cause BAPPENAS and the BAPPEDA in each project province to undertake project
coordination at national and provincial levels respectively, in a manner satisfactory to the Bank
(para 5.04);
(1) cause MOA/DGFCA to provide staffing, facilides, services and the other resources required to
carry out agricultural extension and support satisfactory to the Bank throughout all the
proposed project impact areas (para 5.09);
j) undertake an annual implementation review of the project not later than June 30 of each year,
commencing June 30, 1994, so that the findings can be reflected in the provincial work
programs for the following fiscal year (para 5.25); and
(k) cause the WUAs to bear full cost of O&M in project financed groundwater schemes from the
third year after a facility is commissioned (pam 6.15).
8.02  An understanding was reached that Government would cause all project agencies to monitor
and evaluate project activities and performance according to standards satisfactoy to the Bank
(pars 5.23).
8.03 An assurance was obtained that, as a condition of disbursement for the works and irrigation
equipment and materials components, Government would make annual budgetary allocations
sufficient to implement the agreed annual work programs in each of the project provinces for the fiscal
year 1994/95; and each subsequent fiscal year thereafter until project completion (para 4.10)
8.04 With the above assurances and understanding, the project would be suitable for a Bank loan
of US$ 54.0 million equivalent with a 20 year maturity at the Bank's standard variable rate, including
a grace period of 5 years. The Borrower would be the Republic of Indonesia.
