-43-                              Annex2
ROLNDWTERDELOPNESNA
AsupMptns for Proec Analysis
InSWucton
1.   The subproject areas are spread over eleven provinces and have substantial differences in
climatic conditions, soils and farmers' capability. Actual cropping pattems will evolve in any
subproject area and individual facility after taldng account of soil conditions. The patterns will be
decided by farmers themselves and depend on such factors as farmers' preferences and skills, and
possible marketing constraints with regard to vegetable crops. The project analysis has been
restncted to one analysis for each subproject area using simplified cropping patterns appropriate to
each subproject area. Benefits accruing from provision of community water supply (either formal
Or informal supply systems) and houseyard irigation have not been included in the analysis since
they cannot be quantified.
2.   Conversio          A standard conversion factor of 1.0, except for labor, has been used
for all costs and benefits in the economic analysis.
3.   Dim of Produce. Product pnices used in the analyses are shown by Table 1 of this Annex.
The economic prices for traded commodities such as rice, maize, soybean and groundnuts were
derived by using border prices in US dollars a,cording to IBRD commodity projections and were
converted to the local currency values at the exchange rate assumed under the project (Annex 2,
Table 3). In the analysis, it has been assumed that Indonesia will be self-sufficient in rice, maize
and groundnuts, while the project production for soybeans would be to substitute for imports.
Prices for vegetables were taken as medial farm gate price with appropriately adjusted handling and
marketing charges.
4.    Prcs of AgdguM  Inuts Prices of agricultural inputs used in the analyses are presented
in Table 1 of this Annex. Fertilizers have been valued at projected world market prices. Indonesia
exports substantial quantities of urea. Phosphate and potash fertilizers are either imported or
manufactured from imported raw materials. Lime is generally widely available and is usually
obtained locally. Economic prices for pesticides have been obtained from list prices of major local
suppliers. The economic price of seeds has been assumed to exceed the economic price of the
concmed commodities by about 50 percent.
5.   QPPWIity CQost OfLJb[. Farm labor wage rates vary throughout Indonesia, depending
on the opportunities for work, and are notably related to the extent of irrigation development in any
particular area. Also taken into consideration is that many project schemes are located in remote
areas. In the economic analysis, unskilled labor is priced at 90 percent of the market rate of
unskilled labor. A market rate of Rp. 2,500 per day (average for men and women), has been
assumed in Sulawesi, Aceh, North and West Sumatra, NTB and N1T. The market rates in Java
and Lampung are taken at about Rp. 2,200 per day, reflecting fewer alternative job opportnities
than in other parts of the country. The Lampung rate is affected by its proximity and access to Java.
poectm Cost
6.   Investment Costs. The capital costs of all works were based on 1992 contract rates.
Individual facilities are assumed to be completed within one year of work being initiated on them.
The costs of civil and mechanical works include additions for supervision of construction or
intallation. The physical contingencies have been assessed separately and would range from 0.0 to
10.0 percent. The cost of administration and overheads is included at an estimated rate of 5 perent
for all investment costs for all new tubewells and aU support components (except for technical



-44 -                                 Annex 2
assistance). Taxes and duties have been calculated following Government regulations. A VAT of
10 percent is due only on the parts of the civil works contracts not financed by the Bank.
7.    Operation and Maintenance Costs. Major items of O&M costs include: (a) fuel and
lubricants costs for running the pump engine; (b) pump operators salary plus payments made for
any assistance during high demand periods; (c) stipends paid to elected management of WUAs
(e.g., president, secretary and treasurer); and (d) maintenance of the facility, mainly minor spare
parts for pump units. Indonesia is an oil exporter and has domestic refining capacity at least
sufficient for its own needs. The prevailing financial prices of diesel fuel and lubricants are based
on world parity prices with adjustments to reflect internal handling, transport and marketing to the
location of use. Operation and management manpower inputs are treated as skilled labor, as is the
labor input for repairs. An allowance is made for government supervision inputs after facilities are
handed over to WUAs for management.
8.    Tubewell Rglacement Costs. The pumps and diesel engines have an assumed life of 15
years reflecting average annual running hours. O)ther components of the facilities are maintained
through the project life and do not require replacement.
9.   C    Yields. The 'future with' project yields are estimated on the basis of average yields on
existing tubewell inrigation systems and, where applicable, take into account soil fertility and other
factors. The justification of higher yields is the interaction of improved inputs (improved seeds,
fertilizers, crop protection) and a sufficient and reliable irrigation water supply to encourage their
use. The latter is also the reason for introduction of dry season palawija crops which could not be
grown with rainfall alone.
Ei m3cial A yW 
10.  Farm Budge. To simplify the analysis of a relatively large number of subprojects
distributed in eleven provinces, typical crop budgets (and thus benefit streams) were worked out for
typical subproject areas which could then be taken as representative of groups of subprojects. Farm
budgets were prepared with the assumed cropping pattern for the typical subproject areas. The
budgets cover only a representative range of conditons, using relatively conservative assumptions
for cropping patterns. Gross margins and costs of production were estimated using 1992 prices, as
presented in Table 4 of this Annex.
11.                      ---a . The payment capacity of irrigation beneficiaries would vary
considerably between subproject areas and within any specific area depending on the size of farm
holding. The situation has been tested by preparation of farm models for holdings of 0.5 ha for the
typical subproject areas selected for calculations of crop and farm budgets (para 10).
12.  £t Recovr. The support of initial O&M costs during the first two years is to reduce the
risks associated with transition from rainfed to irrigated agriculture by providing a full and
guaranteed irrigation service during this critical period. However, during this transition period,
farmers would be expected to pay their WUA for water, with accrued income placed in a reserve
fund against any long term major repair costs. WUAs would also be encouraged to use these
savings for short term as an agricultuml credit source for the group beneficiaries. In addition, after
the initial O&M support, farmers will have no option but to meet full O&M costs if they want an
irrigation service. Another factor taken into consideration is that O&M costs of groundwater
development are high compared to surface irrigation schemes, while capital costs are relatively low
per unit area served Thus, total cost recovery under the project will be high compared to typical
surface irrigation schemes. In addition, other users of tubewell water (domestic water supply and
house garden irgation) would pay at rates set by WUAs to reflect incremental O&M costs of the



.45-                                 Annex2
Page
service provided, and there would be indiect cost recovery through increase in land tax (PBB) once
the land status changed from rainfed to irrigated.
Bonawi Analys,a
13.   Backgrond. Economic evaluations have been carried out using Economic Rate of Return
(ERR) calculations for 20 subprojects in 11 provinces. Cast sams include all capital costs and
physical contingencies, but exclude transfer payments such as taxes and duties. In addition, the full
cost of consulting services are accounted for in the economic analyses and attributed pro rta to the
development area. The cost of unsuccessful wells is also included with an assumed failure rate of
10 percent for £)TWs and 5 percent for ITI Ws. An allowance has also been made for depreciation
of drilling rigs as components of well construction cost. It is assumed that the wells will be drilled
using P2AT equipment under contract arrangements. Benefit streams used to assess economic
benefits are based on estimating the difference between the agricultural production in the 'future
with' and 'future without' project situatons. The analyses have been carried out are based on the
typical subproject areas selected for calculations of crop budgets (para 10). Buildup of benefits is
assumed over four years after facilities are commissioned, and individual facilities are assumed to
become operational within one year of the physical works being initiated. An agricultural
development component has been defined and costed (Annex 9), and is included in the economic
analysis on a pro rata area basis.
14.   The base ERRs for all subproject areas, together with present net value (NPV) are given in
Table 5 of this Annex and esimate the ERR for the overall project at about 14.5 percent. However,
this project indicator has relatively limited significance since all schemes have their own particular
characteristics of costs and benefits which are location specific. Thus, the ERRs are calculated to
range from 11.4 percent (DTW facilities in Madura, East Java) to 17.7 percent (lITW facilities in
Besuki, East Java). lITW facilities generally have higher ERRs (with a range of 12.0 percent to
17.7 percent) than DTW facilities (with a range of 11.4 percent to 13.8 percent). However, a
dssign criteria is that DTW would only be proposed in areas where ITrWs are not technically
viable. Table 5 also shows that investment costs of the tubewell facilAties range from Rp 4.0 million
to Rp 6.4 million per ha and net benefits range fiom about Rp 0.9 mllion to Rp 1.2 million per ha.
15.   Sensit Analvses. Analyses were carried out for all schemes to test the sensitivity of
their ERRs to variations in investment costs and direct benefits as detailed in Table 6 of this Annex.
The tests reflects the variables in costs and benefits which can be expected from groundwater
irrigation development with a large number of separate tubewell systems spread over areas with
different physical and social deminants.
16.   To estimate any negative impact on the economic viability, cost changes of +10 percent and
+20 percent were tested, primarily to assess the effect of deeper wells than anticipated and
vaiations in the costs of equipment and materials. Reduction in the expected benefits of -10 percent
and -20 percent were also tested to reflect what could occur due to changes in cropping patterns or
cropping intensities as farmers respond to crop pricing and marketing opportunities. In this respect,
the main risk to benefits would be lower than expected area put to high value crops in the dry
seasons . This test would also take into account that the project specifically targets poor farmers
who may be more risk averse than assumed. In addition, a delay of one year in achieving benefits
was tested to reflect possible delays in the farmers making the transition from rainfed to irrigated
agriculture. Sensitivity to delays in implementation were not tested because each facility is a
separate operational entity and there is little risk that delays in the total construction period would
exceed one year. Tests show that ERRs are fairly stable against the tested levels of cost increases,
but more sensitive to the tested range of benefit reductions. This last indicator justifies the attention
given under the project to planning and design criteria (para 19 below) and the post-construction
phase of the development.



-46-                                 Annex 2
Pagg4
17.  If the design assumptions are not varied (see para 18 below), the tests results in Table 6 of
this Annex indicate that the estimated ERRs could fall below 10 percent in a number of subproject
areas if benefits are reduced by 20 percent, or cost increased by 20 percent. These low values
would relate to the unlikely event that the capital costs per unit area of DTW facilities would be
higher than anticipated. However, the phasing of the project into 'go-ahead' and 'expansion'
programs woulld require that the project economic viability be assessed before marginal impact areas
are fully developed, since a criteria for reinstating 'expansion' areas is the acceptable performance
of£existing tubewell systems in the area.
18.   Also tested are factors that may have a positive impact on the economic analysis, in
particular, the premise that project costs and benefits are based on conservative assumptions. This
was tested by assuming that (a) the command area served by the standard facilities be increased by
10, 20 and 30 percent, thus reducing capital costs per unit area 1/; and (b) running hours per annum
for typical wells be decreased by some 20 percent, thus reducing the O&M costs. The tests under
item (a) for both 11TW and DTW facilities have assumed that the distribution system costs would
not change, since the increase in distance from any oudet valve via farm channels to a field would
increase, on average, by only 20 to 50 meters with the irrigadon supply spread over a larger area.
19.   Specific design criteria considered to be conservative are that: (a) maximum pump running
hours are assumed at only about 18 hours per day; (b) the irrigation requirements are conservatively
calculated; and (c) average well discharges could be higher than assumed if the wells are more
efficiendy designed. These design assumptions can be varies as the project proceeds and changes
during the detailed planning and design process for each project tubewell site should enable
tubeweli systems to become more cost-effectve.
20. With respect to items (a) above, if the maximum pumping of about 18 hours per day were
made less restrictive, the additional pumping hours could also be converted into an increase in
command area served. For example, the estimated ERRs would (on average) increase by about
four percentage points if maximum pumping hours were increased to 22 hours a day for short
periods allowing a command area increase of some 20 percent.
21.  With respect to item (b) of pam 18, there is strong evidence that farmers, on average, are
using less water per crop season than the calculated inigation requirements without a measurable
decrase in crop production. This would imply that the assumed design water duty and pumping
hours (which defines a tubewell command area) are based on conservative estimates of irigation
requirements (Annex 8). Furthermore, a separate test shows that a 20 percent decrease in pump
running hours (with benefiting remain the same), would only increase the ERRs by about one
percentage point.
22.   With respect to item (c) of para 18, there is evidence that, on average, well discearges are
not maximized when the bore hole is constructed. Many bore hole logs of existing wells indicate
that all the productive aquifer strata have not been screened. This shortcoming would be addressed
by better quality contol during the construction of the well points. In addition, command areas are
not planned to reflect the actual well point discharge (known once the well point is developed and
tested and before the buried pipe distribution system is constructed). The conmnand area should be
designed to a duty of 1.0 I/sec/ha 2 / based on the actual measured discharge of the well point, and
not assumed discharges for DTWs (30 V/sec) and IrTWs (10 /sec). Review of planning and design
procedures under the project would specifically address issues related to over design of facilities.
I/ An incr in command area is assumed to result in an equivalent linear irease in benefits.
21  Mm design duty (1.0 Usca) may be reduced if it is confrmed tt this stndd inigation duty is too
conswtve (see paras 20 and 21).

TINDNFSL&
ORQMNZW/&= DEVTPIONT PROJERC
C;;MMlanit Pawdciado9 in Groundwater DelopmentO
1.   This Annex describe procedures and criteria related to planning, design, implementation,
and management of groundwat irrigation facilities by the public sectodwith the participation of the
intendod beneficiaries. It assumes that Government intervendon in groundwater development
would continue to catalyze private sector initiatives. The project objectives to be taken into account
in the development process are to: (a) standardize and formalize the development process; (b)
undertake the development cycle following a logical and sequential process; and (c) ensure full
community pardcipation.
2.   Government financed groundwater irriation development was originally conceived as a
public sector operation with all capital funding and construction being the responsibility of
Government. he O&M of facilities remained with a government agency with beneficianes paying
a water charge for the irrigation service. The PAT organization (through P2AT subproject units)
was established within DOWRD to perform these tasks. Water users associations (WUA) were
originally established on the facilites to enhance water management. However, by the early 1980s,
it was recognized that well established WUAs were capable of taking over full management of
facilities, including responsibility for (and funding of) routine O&M Thus, a process was initiated
for 'privatizing! management of the facilities after construction by Government. The present
practie is for Govemnment to provide O&M support for facilities (mainly for fuel and pump
operators salay) for the first two years after a facility is commissioned. This support period has
recently been reduced to one year for ITTW facilides in East Java. However, it is intended that
WUAs should charge their members an irrigation service fee equivalent to at least the O&M costs of
facilites during the support period. This would enable WUAs to build up working capital to
support continued operations after the initial O&M support is withdrawn.
3.   Government sponsored groundwater development has gradually been transformed from a
public sector, supply-driven operation to a demand-driven program to create facilities for private
sector management through WUAs, albeit that all initial capital expenditures are financed by
government. In essence, when groundwater conditions are known to be favorable for irrigation
development, requests for public financed facilities are made by local government on behalf of the
farmers, and P2AT organizations in the provinces respond by installing facilities within the
limitations imposed by their budgets.
'Mev rels
4.   BlackMgund. Various planning criteria have been apPlied under past projects for selection
of groundwater development areas, selection of sites for individual facilities, and selection of the
type of facilities to be installed. In recent years, development has been restricted mainly to rainfed
areas of sawah and tegal lands, with the proviso that facilides could also be installed in the ta areas
of surfce water inigation systms only when it could be demonstrated that the surface water supply
was totally inadequate and insecure. However, a standardized method of planning groundwater
development for irrigion has yet to be devised and applied which would reflect the demand-driven
concept while ensurng that the most appropriate and cost effective facility is provided.
5.   To address this shortcoming, methodologies for identification of proposed development
areas and for detailed planning of such development need to be standardized. They should be
simple to appl and must be based on adequate water resource data, and on other information which
should be readily available at local government levels, or easily obtainable by rapid field checkng.



- 72 -                                Anex4
In particular, the development need should be clearly defined and beneficiary participation should
be, as far as possible, ensured. For new development areas where hydrogeological conditions are
inadequately known, the process of a combined exploration and pilot development would be applied
to confirm water resource information before proceeding to large scale development.
6.    The development process specifically addresses lessons learned from previous groundwater
operations. These include the need to strengthen involvenient of the farmers, community based
organizations, residents, local governments, and other agencies in all phases of the project cycle.
To give emphasis to this community based approach, and to link irrigation service more closely to
agricultural development, each subproject (individual wells and clusters of tubewells) would follow
a development process of about four years. The development cycle breaks down into four phases:
(a) the pre-preparation phase of 3-6 months; (b) the preparation phase of 9-12 months; (c) the
implementation phase of 9-12 months; and (d) the operation and maintenance support phase of two
years after a facility is commissioned. The development process is shown systematically on Chart
5.
PrearaionPhase
7.    difiatiWoll of Poteal     . The first task would be for the P2AT office to identify
potential groundwater development areas through a desk study of existing hydrogeological
information, maps showing the distribution of surface water schemes and all available soil
information. The study would define general areas in which: (a) physical resources (groundwater
and land) are available; and (b) there is an apparent need for groundwater irrigation (i.e., the area is
presently rainfed, or is located in the tail end of a surface water irrigation system where the water
supply is likely to be inadequate and insecure). The study may also indicate whether ITIW
facilities could be installed, or whether DTWs would be necessary. In East Java, known areas of
intensive private sector STW development would be screened out at this stage. The study would
define areas of potential development by desas (villages) to facilitate the community partcipation
activities.
3. _   Dissen-Anation of Information to Local GMSMM= and Communif. Once the desk study
indicates that an area is potentially viable, the socio-economic viability of the proposed development
would be tested. P2AT would liaise with local government (kabupaten, kecamatan, desa) to
establish that there is a demand for irrigation. The objective would be to ascertain likely conmnunity
response and participation. The desa authorities and P2AT staff would explain the proposed
development to potential beneficiaries (farmers likely to be within tubewell command areas). This
would include explaining that Govemment would met the capital costs of groundwater development
and provide initial O&M support for two years with the farmers willing to accept the responsibilities
and costs of long term management of the tubewell facility. Having established the social/financial
viability of a potential impact area, a tentative program by desa area would be developed.
PEgaradon Phase
9.    General Planning of Tubewell Clusters. Once the pre-preparation process has been
completed, and before proceeding to the detailed planning and design stage, a general program
definng tubewell clusters by desa areas would be developed. The program would indicate: (a) the
locations of areas proposed to be developed in each desa; (b) the wpe(s) and numbers of facilities to
be provided; and (c) the priorities under which the progam would be implemented. The program
would be developed in two steps:
(a)  Conrmation ofDesa Progrms A sketch map would be prepared for each desa, or
group of desas, showing: (i) locadons of tegal and sawah lands; (ii) disr4hution of
rainfed lands; (iii) commands of existing public groundwater facilides; (iv) locations of
areas proposed by local govemment for development; and (v) major population centers
and roads. Other information required include the hydrogeological characteristics of



- 73 -                               Annex 4
Page 3
existing public tubewells and a general description of the agricultural development
opportunities. Compilation of this infonnation together with a field check of the local
government's proposals would permit confirmation or modifichtion of a desa program
and definition of the type(s) of groundwater irrigation facility to be provided. For
developing a confirmed program, special attention should be given to: (i) security of
the surface water supply if wells are proposed in surface water irrigated areas; (ii)
presence or absence of private STW development in the proposed area; and (iii) the
possibility of using the irrigation tubewells for domestic water supply and/or
houseyard irrigation facilities.
(b)  I)event of lProvinalPogs: Once the desa programs are confirmed, they
would be assembled to form a number of subproject areas which together formn the
provincial program. It would then be necessary to decide the priority of developing
subproject areas, taking into account: (i) regional priorities for groundwater
development within the province; (ii) P2AT institutional capability to implement the
proposed work programs; (iii) budgetary considerations; (iv) contracting constraints;
and (v) and coordination with the provincial offices of Cipta Karya, if multipurpose
water facilities are envisaged. This program prioritization would involve discussions
with BAPPEDA and other provincial agencies. The resulting annual provincial work
program would then be subject to some modification during the detailed planning and
design process of the selected subproject areas.
10. Planning of Desa Tubewell Clusters. Although groups of desa programs may be aggregated
into subproject areas for provincial programming, the desa boundary is important in planning of the
command area boundaries of individual tubewell systems. Experience has shown that the
management problems of WUAs can be exacerbated if tubewell command area cross desa
boundaries. Therefore, a general design principle would be that any tubewell command area would
be contained within a single desa.
11.  The planning process for tubewell clusters would be to provide an indicative program for
each desa indicating the type(s) of facility, the number of facilities and the locations of command
area that can be served. The potential development areas would be shown on simple maps. In
practice, a high level of beneficiary participation should be involved in defining the boundaries of
individual tubewell command areas as this, in effect, defines the composition of WUA. Technical
considerations must however be given due weight in the definition of command areas and may
sometimes override social and cultural considerations. However, the use of buried PVC pipe
distribution systems allows for considerable flexibility in locating a well and defining its command
area to accommodate community preference.
12.   At this stage, maps at scale 1:2,000 with contours at 50 cm intervals would be required for
the proposed development areas. Contours showing depth from land surface to water table should
be indicated on these maps (by collecting data from existing open dug wells), if lTfW facilites are
planned for any area 1V. A preliminary definition of command areas and their associated well
locations can then be made on the basis of technical considerations alone (i.e., predicted design
discharge of the well, proposed water duty and thus command area, and topography). However,
although this delineation of command areas may be technically feasible, it must then be checked and
confirmed (or modified if necessary) at desa level to ensure that there are no serious social
constraints to the proposed area. Once this layout has been confirmed, the tentative command areas
would then be used for lhe detailed design of individual tubewell irrigation facilities V. At this
stage, it is possible to in. v the process of forming WUAs.
1/ This infonnation is not critical if DTWs are planned
2/ In negotatng the confirmed disribution of command areas within a desa, care should be taken to avoid isolating
small areas not svWd under the program and too small to serve under a 'fill-in' program.



-74 -                               A x 4
Pagg 4
13.  W&A Y2vlommn. The WUA organization would be based on the existing Presidential
Instruction No. 2 on Establishing Water User Farmer Associations of 1984 (and the 1992 update
of this Regulation) and any pertinent provincial decrees. The cotnmunity based organizations
(CBOs) in each impact area would represent the farmers, and other user groups for muldpurpose
wells. The CBO would make a written request for a tubewell system and agree to form a WUA
which would take on responsibility for O&M of the facility after comnpletion (para 8 above).
14.  This critical step would ensure that the potendal beneficiary groups fully understand their
responsibilities and commitnents. To assist in this process, special WUA development staff/units
would be established in each P2AT to provide an effective link between the conmmunity to be served
and the technical staff of P2AT and other agencies involved. These staff/units would be primarily
responsible for the community pardcipadon aspects of the development process. In particular, they
would assist the CBOs in the formiadon of WUAs by arranging informal and formal meetings
attended by community leaders, farmers and other users. At the appropriate time, they would
arrange the forum to appoint leaders to fornally establish the WUA and decide its constitution and
bylaws.
15.  During the preparation phase, orientation and participatory training would be carried out by
P2AT staff. Training would focus on: (a) identfying and explaining the roles and responsibilities
of the WUA members in the tubewell development process; (b) the organization and registration of
the WUA; and (c) the managementrole of the WUAs including, finances and O&M activities. Also
dring the preparation phase, farmers would be trained by PRAS staff in the development of their
agricultural development plas. A special challenge would be to help fanners understand what
would be involved in making the transition from rainfed to irrigated agriculture.
16.  DesigL of Tubewel Facilides. The development is conceptualized to be based on ITIWs
with design discharges of about 10 l/sec and DTWs wit design discharges of about 20 l/sec to 30
1//sec depending on the subproject area (Annex 5). A standard water duty of 1.0 1/s/ha has been
selected for design purposes for the entire development. This duty appears to be adequate to meet
peak demands of the assumed cropping patterns with a maximum of about 18 hours pumping per
day in all except very dry years (Annex 8). The design discharges and water duties imply command
areas of 10 ha for TIW facilities and of about 20-30 ha for DTWs. However teactual
area for anv well location would be decided after the well has been developed and the actual
discharge is known, A permanent pump house will be provided. The main distribution system will
be through buried PVC pipes with turnout boxes fitted with alfalfa type valves to serve areas of
about 1.0 ha. Distibution below the turnouts would be through open earth channels constructed by
the farmers. Land and fencing for pump houses would be provided by the concerned WUA.
17.     einMdfctosftMWus  s  fWtrS lFclte. In some cases,it
will be possible to adapt the groundwater facility design for multipurpose use, specifically to
provide a formal domestic water supply (DWS) and/or houseyard irrigation service, in addition to
the basic irrigaton sevice. Such opportunities would generally occur when a well is located close
to a ur settlement. The potential for such a service would be identified during the initial desa
progmming process for tubewell clusters (para 9(a) above), and the need and demand for the
servce determined when the proposed siting of tubewell locadons and their associated command
areas are discussed and confirmed with potential beneficiaries (para 12 above). Although
Government would meet the construction cost of a DWS facility, there must be a clear requirement
and demand for such a service, and beneficiaries must express willingness to meet any incremental
costs for O&M and management of the DWS facility provided.
18.  A range of DWS facility types of varying complexit can be envisaged. For example, a
simple type of facility could be a low level storage tank of suitable size (fitted with a number of
robust taps for drawing water) located near a well, which would be filled at intervals through a
valved bypass pipe taken from the irgation pump. Alternatively, domestc water supply could be



-75-                                 AnneA 4
reticulated through a buried pipeline taken from the pump to several storage tanks distributed at
strategic localities within a habitation area. The main criteria for design of the system would be
manageability by the beneficiaries and cost-effectiveness.
19.  Facilities r Houseyard igariQon. Encouragement of houseyard irrigation to improve diet
and/or income of the rural poor would be a feature of the project. As most rural houses are located
in a compound where such cultivation would be possible, houseyard irrigation could be developed
whenever groundwater irrigation facilides are located close to a settlement. Moreover, it is likely
that beneficiaries of the tubeweil conunand area will also own houses where garden irrigation could
be developed. The basic premises for the provision of an irrigation service for house gardens
would be: (a) there is an expressed desire for the facilities by potential beneficiaries; (b) facilities
would be easily manageable as part of the overall irrigation operation; and (c) the concerned
irrigation WUA would be willing to take on the additional management responsibility for providing
inrigation to house gardens and have a workable plan for collecting associated user fees.
20.   Demonstration of Designed Tubewell Facilities to Beneficiaries. Before finalizing the
design of the piped distribution system for a tubewell irrigation facility, the design principles sho.ild
be demonstrated to the intended beneficiaries. This would involve assembling a representative
group of beneficiaries to walk through the proposed command area with P2AT engineering staff.
The proposed locations of the turnout boxes and the fields to be served would be identified and
discussed with the potential beneficiaries. Any technically viable and reasonable modifications
resulting frm this demonstradon would be incorpoated in the final design.
ImWwle tation Phas
21. Normally in groundwater development, final decisions on the design of a complete irrigation
facility cannot be made until a tubewell is constructed and its actual discharge characteristics are
known. Thus, a program of production well construction is undertaken in advance of finalization
of the irrigadon facility design. In practice, P2AT offices generally complete a program of well
illing during one fiscal year and implement the associated irrigation works during the following
fiscal year. The interim period between well completion and initiation of irrigation system
construction is used for finalizing the irrigation system design, preparing and letting of works
contracts, and specification and procurement of the required pump units.
22.  The WUA Agreement. Once there is enough infornation to finalize the design of the
irrigation facility, it then possible to finalize the process of setting up the WUA since the
beneficiaries to be served can now be identified. Once composition of the WUA has been finalized
and its officers elected, they would enter into an aement under which: (a) the farmers and other
users would confirm arrangements and responsibility for O&M during the initial two years O&M
support by P2AT; and (b) agree to take full responsibility for O&M after the two year period.
Constrction of the irrigation facility would then proceed with WUA representatives encouraged to
monitor progess.
Q=zdmio and Maintenanoe Phas
23.   .Mmsigning of Tubhwell Faclities. The fint stage of this phase would involve a
ngorous procedure for handing over completed tubeweUls to WUAs. This would be managed by
P2AT which would coordinate the involvement of the other local govemment entities involved. An
essential task would be to confirm that completed tubewell facilities can function as planned and
designed before they are handed over for management to their respective WUAs. Any faults
detected during the testing process would be corrected by the contractor (or P2AT) before the hand
over to WUA is made. Once it has been confirmed that a tubewell system can perform as designed,
there would be a formal certification by P2AT that the system is fully functional. Representatves of
the local govennment (Bupati/Camat) and the WUA would observe a test of the system when the
handing over ceremony is conducted.

24.  Initial O&M Suport. After the completion of the tubewell system, the P2AT would
provide support for the O&M costs of the facility for a period of two years. This initial assistance
to the WUAs would help ensure that the tubewell O&M costs do not constrain the use of the facility
during the critical transition from rainfed to irrigated agrculture. During the initial O&M period, the
performance of the tubewell system would be carefully monitored.

transition from rainfed to irrigated agriculture. PRAS would provide support to implement the
agricultual development plan for each command area. PRAS would: provide seeds for high value
or new crops; and conduct tials and demonstrations for new crops and improved crop production
methods (including soil and water conservation, and crops diversification). PRAS would also
provide WUAs with advice on: (a) crop production credit and crop insurance; (b) post harvest
arrangements for transport, drying, storage and markedng, (c) advantages and initiatives needed for
WUA members to form a cooperative to avail of group credit; and (d) participation in various
literacy, business and income generating skills and credit programs.

Plantfg and Design crteria and Methodology fa 9rwnd-wa Deloment=
1.  This Annex oudines planning and design criteria would be used as general guidelines for
preparation of the project work programs, including the first year of project implementation
(FY 199314). The project consultants would review these criteria (and methodology to apply the
criteria) and recommend modifications, if required, so that a standardized approach to planning and
design of groundwate. development can be formulated, tested and adopted by the end of the second
yea of project implementaton.
2.  13ackgounld. The planning, design and implementation of groundwater (groundwater)
inigation development are undertaken by DGWRD through subproject offices (P2ATs) of the PAT
Subdirecorat in DOI-II, with the main focus on construction of works. The scope, scale and rate of
groundwater development in each province have been generally decided by availability of funding at
he national level, with limited involvement of the provincial and local governments. Groundwater
development has been implemented on as discrete subprojects with each consultant introducing
separate planning and design critena and procedures
3.  The main lesson leaned at date is a clear need for planning and design criteria and processes,
implementation procedures and O&M management arrangements to be standardized and applied to all
subproject areas. Such planning and design criteria should enable the technology selected for any
locaton to be hydrogeologically appropriate, the least cost solution and sustainable under farmer
anagement It should also enable ful participation of the farmers and other beneficiaries to be
served, taking into consideration physical resources and technical determinants and the possibility of
muldtpurpose use of groundwater resources.
4.  In addition, until preparation of the proposed project, groundwater development in Indonesia
has proceeded on the basis of tine consuming and expensive exploration studies to estimate reliable
groundwater resources, followed by planning and and design activities related to the use of these
resources. Ihese were caied out under separate projecs, mainly in traditional areas for groundwater
exploitation in Java. Thus, another project objective would be to adopt a planning approach and
related work programs which would reduce exploration costs. In addition, the planning process
should enable risks to be limited, and enough flexibility to be maintained in the spatial layout of a well
field, and thus individual well points, so that project can respond to inevitable unknowns in
groundwater development. To achieve this objective, perfonmance evaluation of existing and project
tubewell facilities would need to be substantially strengthened.
5.  To summarize, a consolidated and standardized methodology for the identification and planning
process still does not exist, although criteria for selection of potential groundwater development areas
have been prepared under several groundwater irrigation projects. It is therefore conciuded that for
identification and screening of potential groundwater development areas, a simple standard
methodology should be applied using as far as possible all existing physical and other resource
information. The objective would be to quickly delineate areas with groundwater development
potental and then test the various determinants to confirm that an area should be developed. The
potentially suitable areas would be evaluated through in-depth investigations following a sequential
procedure of tests. Once the impact area (cluster of tubewell) satisfies the standards of 'go-ahead'
status then it would be formally cleared for project implementadion. The following parts of this
Annex outline the methodology and sequential procedures to achieve the above planning and design
objectives.

6. a.LQp~iMIhQdQIQgy. The first step in the procedure for identification of subproject areas
would be to collect basic information and data in enough detail to establish in general terms that the
proposed development is viable. The main planning determinants are as follows:
(a)   There must be sufficient physical resources (i.e. groundwater availability and suitable
agricultural land).
(b)   There must be a general demand for irrigation service by potential beneficiaries and no
other water source except for groundwater (i.e. most farmers want and would look after
and pay for at least the O&M of ar :rrigation service).
(c)   The proposed groundwater development must be financially and economically viable
(i.e. be able to generate a economic rate of return (ERR) of at least 10 percelt)
confirmed by evaluating performnance of existing groundwater facilities and, as the
project proceeds, those constructed under the project
7.  The first stage would be to identify potential groundwater development reas through a desk
study of existing development proposals that have not yet been implemented. In this process, a
pireliminary study, using existing data and records, should confirm that the proposed well field
satsfies the general physical resource and technical criteria for the proposed project. In particular, the
proposed subproject area should not be located within the command area of an existing (or proposed)
surface irrigation scheme and has land suitable for irrigated agricultural development 11. This process
is to delineate those areas that are viable to develop and appear to satisfy the project planning and
design criteria. It should then be possible to determine the general location of proposed subprojects
(iLe., the general impact area of each cluster of tubewells) that are likely to be viable for development.
8.  Once a potential impact area has been identified with potential for groundwater development, the
area would be divided into 'go-ahead' and 'expansion' programs. The 'gQahead' part of a program
can be implemented immediately. Any '2ansion' part of a subproject area would proceed when
existing tubewells (and, when possible, those constructed under the 'go-ahead' program), have been
sufficiendy monitored and evaluated to confirm that criteria on social, financial and economic
performance can be met. This approach of dividing potential programs into 'go-ahead' and
expansion' categories would provide the flexibility needed to address the inevitable uncertainties in
groundwater development. It would also enable lessons learnt, as the project proceeds, to be
incorporated in the development process. As presently envisaged, about 15,300 ha of development
are categorized as 'go-ahead', while some 9,350 ha are initially retained in the expansion category
(Annex 1, Table 1).

before proceeding to the detailed planning and design stage, the following essential steps must be
processed for each proposed subproject area:

technical, agronomic and socio-economic information of each proposed subproject area
recorded on a desa basis. It would be essential that a sketch map of the areas is prepared
showing the location of the proposed development lands with respect to villages, roads and

areas, a primary objective would be to design an initial program which satisfied the
exploration requirements. Thus, initial 'go-ahead' wells would be spaciously located ovei
the potential impact area to give the best coverage for evaluating the groundwater resource
potential. In areas with existing groundwater development, project wells would also be
located to provide additional performance data to confirm the socio/economic impact of the
proposed well field. The work progrm would be formulated to take into account: (i) any
provincial re ional priorities, (fi) the P2AT institutional capacity to implement the work
programs; (ifi) the time frame required to develop a typical cluster of tubewells (i.e.,
procurement considerations, etc.); (iv) budgetary considerations; and (v) coordination with
other agencies involved, particularly if some wells are likely to be multipurpose. The net
outcome would be a tentadve annual work program, agreed with the provincial and local
govenmments, which would then be subject to confirmation after more detailed planning and
design of each specifc cluster of tubewells.

collected to a standard format (designed by the project consultants) for each desa and then collated for
program planning and should include, inter alia, the following determinants:
(a) area of the "tegal" and "sawah" (dry or irrigated) lands proposed for development;

tubewell area;

I1. Once the data and information has been collected for the proposed cluster of tubewells (as
outined above), this would be analyzed and evaluated against a detailed set of criteria (paras 12 and
13 below) that would be applied to fix the boundary of the cluster of tubewells and potential siting of
individual wells. This tentative siting of tubewell locations would be decided by P2AT and agreed
with local Govemnment representatives. The process would allow for some flexibility in the final
siting of wells to reflect local considerations but would, however, be always governed by technical
considerations (i.e., availability of groundwater and suitable agricultural land). Once the well field
and approximate siting of individual well has been agreed, the proposal would be submitted to the
central PAT offices for evaluation, formal clearance and approval.
12. Siting ofndividual TubW_dl Systems The final stage would be full community participation
with potential groups of farmers to confirm the tentative boundaries of individual tubewells and set up
the water users associations (WUAs). In this process, detailed physical, social and other community
factors would be taken into consideration, including whether the tubewell would be multipurpose or
not. In this process, WUAs would be told that the actual boundaries of a tubewell system would be
finalized only af= the well point has been developed and the well discharge is confirmed.
13. Criteria for Selection of Areas and Siting of Individual Tubewells. The siting of an individual
tubewell system within a cluster area would ensure that for each individual site, the physical resources
are adequate and sustainable and that technical, social and financial and economic criteria have been
satisfied. This is because there can wide variations in design parameters within relatively small
development areas. In addition, an individual tubewell siting would confirm that the command area
reaches the target population (i.e., most beneficiaries are poor families or small farm holders) and also
takes into consideration opportunities for multipurpose use of groundwater.
14. Siting for an individual tubewell would be based on the following determinants.
(a)  Selected tubewell command areas would exclude those areas where STW development is
possible. In borderline areas, where aquifer conditions are marginal for STW development,
project tubewells would be sited where existing hand drilled private wells serve less than
(say) 20 percent of the proposed command of project tubewells and project development is
unlikely to have a detrnmental effect on existing tubewells.
(b) There should be an evident demand by the majority of the farmers-beneficiaries who should
demonstrate their willingness to: (i) manage the facility at their own expense, under the
terns and conditions proposed by the project; (ii) make land available free of charge for
pump house and other facilities; (iii) provide access, if necessary for adjacent tubewells; and
(iv) acdvely participate in the construction of the system by providing access to land for
installation of the buried pipe systems and fencing for the pump house, etc.
(c) Areas where tenants farm a substantial part of the command area (say greater than 30
percent) should be given special review to confm the financial viability of the proposed
development. If necessary, specific sociological surveys should be undertaken to confirn
that there is no risk of lack of interest by the potential beneficiaries.



-                                   Annex S
Pag& S
(d)  For areas where the average farm size is large (i.e., on average greater than one hectare)
and/or the population density is low, the socio-economic viability of the tubewell location
should be examined in more detail to establish that the tubewell would achieve the poverty
alleviation objective, and to evaluate whether there might be the risk of labor shortage.
(e) Tubewells should be sited only where fanning is the dominant employment activity. Areas
where the main activity of the population is not farming (e.g., fishery, handicraft, migrant
labor, etc.) should only be considered when there are special sociological considerations
which would justify the site selection.
(f)  Areas in proximity of towns should be examined carefully as there might be shortage of
labor for agriculture intensification and/or the risk of temporary migradon.
(g)  The agricultural development potential (i.e., opportunity for high value crops, etc.) should
be given special attention in screening locations for tubewell clusters and in the design of
individual tubewells and the agricultural support requirements (particularly if an objective
would be to promote a specific high value crop).
(h)  Whenever possible, the ITrW technology (para 15) should be adopted with wells designed
to yield at least 10 I/sec.
(i) Project wells would only be sited where there would be no detrimental effect on existing
domestic water supply facilities.

15. Qral Criteria. The development is conceptualized to be based on Intermediate technology
tubewells (ITWs) with design discharges of about 10 I/sec and deep tubewells (DTWs) with
discharges of some 20 to 30 I/sec depending on the subproject area 1/. A standard duty of 1.0 I/sec/ha
has been selected for design purposes for entire development 2/ This implies a command area of
about 10 ha for ITrWs and some 20 to 30 ha for DTWs. A permanent pump house would be
provided. The main distribution system will be through buried PVC pipes with turnouts boxes fitted
with alfalfa valves to serve individual command areas of about 1.0 ha YI. The distribution within the
1.0 ha commands would be through open earth channels to be constructed by the farmers. Any
fencing for pump houses would be provided by the concerned WUA.
16. I1TW facilities would be selected for all locations where suction mode pumping technology is
feasible. In this respect, the following determinants should be taken into account:
(a)    An ITrW can be located, if necessary, at the lowest part in a command area where the
water levels may be at shallower depth, with the water raised to higher levels of the
command through the buried PVC pipe distribution system.

17. IrW Facilities. The following provides general design criteria for rTrW facilities with design
discharges of about 10 I/s serving a command area fixed by a duty of 1.0 I/sec/ha.and serving about
10 ha:
(a) Tubewell. Tubewell depths may range from about 40 m to 70 m and would be drilled at
200 mm diameter to accommodate 100 mm casing and screen. The tubular materials would
be of PVC of suitable grde. Screens would be provided for all productive aqifer layers if
unconsolidated granular materials form the aquifer. with gravel packing (if necessary).
Screened sections will cover all productive aquifer material. Wells would be designed for
open hole completion if the aquifer is consolidated rock (i.e., limestone or sandstone) with
100 mm diameter PVC casing mstalled from surface to the expected pumping water level.
(b) Eum house., The pump house would be sized at the minimum to accommodate the pump
unit and its design would provide reasonable security for the unit. The simple design
developed in East Java under ISSP I incorporates many appropriate features and has
reasonable cost. Provision should be made for a masonry walled, concrete floored sump
when this is necessary.
(c) Distribution Sye   The header pipe would be of cast iron or suitable grade PVC. The
above-ground pipe connection would be of cast iron. The buried pipe would be of PVC
material and consist of an appropriate mix of 125 mm and 100 mm diameter pipes. In this
respect, pipes rated for pressures of 5.0 kg/cm2 were commonly used under ISSP I, but
lighter duty pipe may be more appropriate in some circumstances. Buried pipes would be
set at a depth of about 1.0 m and a sand pack may be provided for pipe protection, if
necessary. Turnout boxes would be of masonry and would be fitted with alfalfa type
valves, following a standard, proven design. The layout of the pipe distribution would
make maxmum possible use of the master slope to minimize pipe length and the number of
turnout boxes (para 15 above).
(d) Pump Unit The pump unit would be a diesel engine powered centrifugal pump fitted for V-
belt drive.
18. DTW Facilities. The following provides general design criteria for DTWs with design
discharges of about 20 Vs to 30 Vs. servng command areas of 20 ha and 30 ha respcively fixed on a
design duty of 1.0 I/sec/ha.
(a) Iu1MwM1I. The tubewell depths would range from about 70 m to 110 m. Depths and design
discharges of DTWs in subproject areas are foreseen to be: about 110 Im depth for 30 I/s in
East Java; about 80 m depth for 20 I/s in West Java; and some 1 10 m for 20 I/s in Rumbia,
Lampung. However, real conditions may require some modification within these ranges.
Well tubular materials would be of PVC of suitable grade. The pump chamber casing
would be of 250 mm diameter for 20 I/s wells and 300 mm diameter for 30 Vs wells. A
production string of casing and screen of 150 mm diameter would be instaled if the aquifer
is unconsolidated granular materials and, in this case, a gravel pack may be required.
Scened sections will cover all productive aquifer material. Wells would be designed for
open hole completion if the aquifer is consolidated rock--limestone or sandstone--and, in

(b) Pum, house. The pump house would be sized at the mininum to accommodate the pump
unit and provide reasonable security for the unit.
(c) DigMbuol Syvm. The header pipe would be of cast iron or suitable grade PVC. The
above ground pipe connection would be of cast iron. The buried pipe would be PVC
material and consist of an appropriate mix of 150 mm and 125 mm diameter pipes (pipes

An inertia starting mechanism would be preferred for the engine rather than a battery
powered starter motor. V-belt drives rather than an angle gear drive would be preferred for
powering the pump.
19.  Improvements to Design Assumptions. The above design assumptions are conservative
because: (a) maximum pump running hours are assumed at about 18 hours per day; (b) inigation
requirements are conservatively calculated; and (c) average well discharges can be expected to be
higher than assumed ff the wells are more efficiently designed. However, the project consists of a
large number of separate tubewell systems. Therefore the design assumptions can be varies as the
roject proceeds and changes should enable tubewell systems to be more cost-effective and thus
inrease the pro ect economic viability. Standard design assumptions would be specifically reviewed
during the detailed planning and design of each project tubewell site.
20.  With respect to items (a) above, the project assumption of a maximum of 18 hours operation
per day is conservative and, if made less restrictive, could also convert to an increase in conmand
area served. The running hours of pump sets could be safely increased to a maximum of 22 hours a
day for short periods (in years of low rainfall). This would allow a proportional increase in the
command area served of at least 20 percent. With respect to item (b) above, there is strng evidence
that farmers, on average, appear to be using less water per crop season than the calculated irigation
requirements, without a measurable decrease in crop production. This would imply that the assumed
design water duty (which defines the command area) of 1.0 i/per/sec (and thus design pumping
hours) are too high (Annex 8). A reduction in the design irrigation duty would convert into a
prorional increase in the commd area (and thus benefits).
21.  With respect to item (c) of para 19 above, there is some evidence that well discharges are not
maximized when the bore hole is constructed because all the productive aquifer is not screened. This
shorcoming should be addressed by better quality control when the borehole is being constructed. In
additon, command areas are simply taken as a standard 10 or 30 ha. Command areas are generally
not designed to reflect the actual well point discharge, known once the well point is developed and
tested and before the buried pipe distribution system is constructed. The command area of project
tubewells should be initially designed to a duty of 1.0 1/sec/ha, but then finalized on the actual
discharge of the well point. It is recommended that the assumed design duty (1.0 i/sec/ha) be reduced
if it is confimed tt this duty is too consrative. The consultants would review the project planing
and design assumptions, and as appropriate, modify the standard planning and design criteria,
prcesses and procedures to address the issues related to possible over design of facilities.
22. Domesi Water S ly MWS) Facilitie  in some areas, multipurpo  se hof te groundwater
wvIl be possible to provide a domestic water supply and houseyard irrigation in addition to the field

irrigation service. Such opportunities will generally occur when a well is located close to a rural
settlement Potential for provision of such a service will be recognized during the initial prog ng
process for tubewell clusters. The need and demand for the service would be specifically determined
when the proposed distribution of the tubewell locations are discussed and confirmed with the

23. It is evident that different types of DWS facilities of varying complexity can be envisaged and
that the design of any facility Wll be, site specific. A simple type of DWS facility would be a low
level storage tank of suitable size (fitted with a number of robust taps for drawing water) located near
the well point. The tank would be filled at intervals through a valved bypass pipe taken from the
irrigation pump. Alternatively, DWS could be reticulated through a buried pipeline taken from the
pump to several storage tanks (fitted withi taps) distributed at strategic localities within the habitation
area. The main crteria for the design of a DWS system would be manageability by the beneficiaries
and cost-effectiveness. It seems probable that provision of a single storage tank would be the most
appropriate facilit in ny cases, particularly if clusters of lTrWs are programmd In all locations
where DWS facilties will be provided under the project, the planning and design of such facilities
would comply with accepted criteria and standards of water quality and public health, satisfactory to
the Bank, as adopted under Bank supported rural water supply and sanitation projects.
hmnQnentaLon of Wors
24. The final decisions on the design of a irrigation distribution system cannot be made until a
tubewell is constructed and its actual discharge is known. Thus, production well construction is
undertaken in advance of the design of the irrigation distribution system. In practice, the P2ATs
generally complete a program of well drilling duiing one fiscal year and phase in the implementation
the associated irrigation works into the following fiscal year. The period (six to nine months)
between well completion and initiation of irrigation system construction is use to finaLize inrigation
distribution system design, preparadon of specification and letting of works contracts, and
procurement of the required pump units. Although this process involves a time lag between
construction of a well and completion of its irrigation facility, it fits well with the Government's
annual budgeting procedures. The process may be foTeseen to continue under the project but can be
imprved.
25. Well construction in P2AT projects is most commonly done using the agencies' own drilling
rigs, through various types of drilling contracts. The tubular materials installed in the wells would be
provided under central ICB procurement. Decisions on designs of individual wells (location of
screen, etc.) and supervision of drilling, well development and pump testing would generally be the
responsibility of P2AT personnel. The procurement anrangements are summarized in Annex 14
together with a tentative schedule of contracts (by category, province and year) given in Table 1 of
Annex 14. Procurement would be based on standardized specifications and contract documentation
and follow processes and procedures aimed to improve quality control.
26. Commission of Tubewell Facilities for Handover to WUAs A rigorous procedure will be
applied by P2AT for testing the operational performance of all completed tubewell facilities against the
intended design objectives before the tubewell systems are handed over for management to their
ref.pective WUAs. Any faults detected during the testing procedure would be corrected by the
concerned entity (contactor or P2AT) before the formal handover is made.

EgLI
,RONESIA
GRUNDATER DEVUDIENI PRO1EC17
Groundwater Conditions in Subgrojet Aras
1.  Intoduction. The development objective of the project is to expand groundwater irrigation by
about 25,000 ha distributed in various subproject areas in East Java, West Java, Nusa Tenggara
Baram, Nusa Tenggara Timor, North, Central and South Sulawesi, Aceh, North and West Sumatra,
and Lampung 1/. The project would exclude development in areas where hydrogeological conditions
(depth of aquifer, pumping water level and rock type) are suitable for groundwater development by
the private sector using hand-driUed shallow tubewells (STW), or hand-dug open wells equipped
with surface mounted centrifugal pumps. In evaluating the groundwater resources, a key objective is
to delineate areas suitable for llTWs and DTWs, the two types of groundwater irrigation facility to be
installed under the project as described in Annex 4.
2.   Groundwater conditions and social attitudes towards the proposed types of development are
well enough known in many of the subproject areas to plan the development with a high degree of
certainty. However, in some subproject areas L the Outer Islands, hydrogeological conditions appear
favorable, but they are as yet unproven. Proposed subproject areas have therefore been categorized
as: (a) 'go-ahead' areas suitable for firm development programs involving limited risk of failure; and
(b) 'expansion' areas with unknowns relating to hydrogeology and/or social attitudes. In the
'expansion' areas, development programs would only be initiated after pilot schemes have confirmed
that full scale development is viable and success of the pilot programs have been proven. In this
situation, the wells drilled under the pilot schemes have an exploratory function. Annex 1, Table I
summarizes the proposed development by subproject area, type of facility and into 'go-ahead' and
'expansion' schemes. The information on groundwater conditions in the various project provinces
and their subproject areas is summarized below.
3.   EastUnx. Groundwater conditions are well known in East Java as a result of exploration and
development programs implemented to support groundwater irrigation for more than two decades.
Despite substantial groundwater irrigation development, it is considered that there is potential to serve
additional areas of at least 20,000 ha. The main aquifers are formed by either fluvio-volcanic or
limne formations. Wells in limestone formations may be completed in an open hole. Large areas
are suitable for development using ITIWs, though care will be necessary to avoid encroaching into
areas suitable for STW development. In other areas, water levels and other aquifer ccnditions prevent
the use of ITrWs, and development by DTW facilites is mandatory. Additional development in at
least part of the Brantas basin may be constrained by competing demands and the overall water
balance of the basin. Care will be necessary to avoid inducing salt water intrusion near the coast,
particularly with regard to limestone aquifers. The planned development under the project includes
4,600 ha by ITIW facilities using wells of about 60 m average depth designed for discharges of
about 10 V/s, and 3,400 ha by DTW facilities using wells of about 1 10 m average depth designed for
discharges of about 30 I/s. Some ITW facilities will require sumps in which to set the centrifugal
pumps. The entire program is categorized as 'go-ahead' status.
4.   WestJaa. Although not investigated in the detail of East Java, the groundwater conditions in
West Java are fairly well known. The most important aquifers are formed by fluvio-volcanic
materials. Three subproject areas have been selected where development can be planned on a 'go-
ahead' basis.
(a) Gabugyetan is located adjacent to the north coast and some 150 km east of Jakarta. About
1000 ha will be developed by ITrW facilities with wells of 50 m average depth designed for
discharges of about 10 Vs, and 600 ha with DTW facilities with wells of 80 m average depth
I/ Asian Development Bank is supporting a similar project with development areas in Central Java.



- 92 -                            Annex6
-922
designed for discharges of about 20 I/s. The lrTW facilities may require sumps in which to
set the centrifugal pumps.
(b) Qmpka is located adjacent to the noth coast and about 90 km east of Jakarta. About 300 ha
will be developed by 1TrW facilities with wells of about 50 m average depth designed for
discharges of about 10 I/s. and 100 ha with DTW facilities with wells of 80 m average depth
designed for discharges of about 20 Vs. It is foreseen that the JrTW facilities may require
sumps in which to set the centrifugal pumps.
(c) Karanwan is located to the east of Bandung. It is envisaged that the entire 400 ha area
will be developed by ITrW facilities with wells of averaging about 60 m in depth designed for
discharges of about '0 1/s. The centrifugal pumps will be mounted at land surface.
5.   Nusa Tenggara Barat. Development is planned at various localities on Lombok and in three
areas on Sumbawa island:
(a) Lombok. The hydrogeology is well known from studies carried out with Italian Government
assistance and from a groundwater irrigation development program cofinanced by the
Bank/Government of Italy under ISSP I (Ln. 2880-ND). The aquifers are formed by fluvio-
volcanic materials. Groundwater will be developed by DTW facilities with wells averaging
about 80 m in depth designed for discharges of 20 I/s. Though hydrogeological conditions
are known well enough to plan a program of some 900 ha, farmers' response to previous
development has been mixed. Until these problems have been resolved, the entire program is
deferred in the 'expansion' category.
(b) Siumbawa-Keli subproject area is located to the southeast of Bima in the eastern part of
Sumbawa Island. The potential aquifer is formed by alluvial material and data available
indicates that the most productive aquifer material should be encountered in the top 40-60 m of
the alluvial section. Water levels are generally at depths of 2.0 m or less below land surface.
Some farmers use groundwater taken by hand from shallow open wells to irrigate vegetables
in the dry season in a small part of the potential development area. The entire area is planted
to paddy in the wet season. It is envisaged that the area will be developed by ITrW facilities
of some 40-60 depth designed for discharges of about 10 Vs. Sumps may be required in
some localities to ensure the operation of centrifugal pumps. A subproject area of about 750
ha has been selected with 500 ha categorized as 'go-ahead' and with 250 ha deferd into the
'expansion' category until the extent of suitable aquifer is more clearly defined. Care will be
necessary to select areas with sufficient thickness of alluvium and to avoid w& ,r quality
problems.
(c) Sumbawa Sage subproject area is located in the extreme east of Sumbawa Island. Aquifer
conditions are similar to those of Keli, with most productive material concentrated in the top
40-60 m of the alluvial section and with shallow or even flowing artesian water levels. The
farmers grow dry season vegetables irrigated with hand-drawn water taken from shallow open
wells in part of the potential development area. The entire area is planted to paddy in the wet
season. It is envisaged that the area would be developed by ITrW facilities with wells of 40-
60 m depth designed for discharge of about 10 V/s. Sumps may be required to ensure
operation of centifugal pumps in some locations. The total area planned for development is
some 1,000 ha with 500 ha in the 'go-ahead' category and 500 ha deferred in the 'expansion'
category until the area of productive aquifer is properly defined.
(d) Sumbawa-&=o area is an irregular plain that rises from low elevations near the coast to
significanty higher elevations inland. It is underlain by alluvial and volcanic deposits. Water
levels are at shallow depLh in extensive areas, and shallow tubewells produce small artesian
flows near the coast. There is some uncertainty about aquifer conditions, particularly well
productivity and depths of aquifers. A small part of the plain receives irrigation in the dry



-93 -                           Annex 6
season from surface water sources. It is planned to develop about 400 ha of groundwater
irrigation by 11TW facilities with wells of 60 m average depth designed for discharges of
about 10 1/s. Some IlTIW facilities may require sumps in which to set the centrifugal pumps.
Because of uncertainty about the producdon performances of the tubewells, only 100 ha are
categorized as 'go-ahead' program with the balance deferred under the expansion' category.
6.   Nusa Teega Timor. There are several potential groundwater development areas in West
Timor which have been subject to some exploration under a previous CIDA project and under
subsequent investigations. The intermontane plain of Arold and the Besikama coastal plain have been
selected for development from a P2AT office established in Atambua 1/. The proposed development
would be by flrW facilities with wells about 60 m deep designed for about 10 U/s to serve command
areas of about 10 ha. While flowing wells are expected in parts of the Aroki plain, sumps may be
required for instalation of centifugal pumps in other areas. Although the hydrogeological conditions
are fairly well known, the response of the farmers to groundwater inigation is not known. Thus, of
300 ha development planned for both Aroli and Besikarna, only 100 ha in each area are categorized
as 'go-ahead' program, with some 200 ha in each area retained in the 'expansion' category until a
positive farmer is proven.
7.   Nori Sulawesi. A P2AT office has been established in this province over the last two years to
catry out exploratory drilling and pilot development programs. Individual potential development
areas appears to be relatively small in extent. They are located on intenrontane and coastal plains
underlain by alluvial deposits. Some 1,200 ha has been selected for development under the project in
four areas -- Ramhokan (200 ha), Tombatu (200 ha), Lolak (300 ha) and Limboto (500 ha). The
development would be by ITTW fcilities with wells around 60 m deep designed for discharges of
about 10 1/s to serve command areas of 10 ha. Reflecting uncertainties about the extents of the aquifer
systems in the proposed areas and farmer response to the provision of groundwater irrigation, the
'go-ahead' programs have been restricted to 50 ha each in Rambokan and Tombatu and 100 ha each
in Lolak and Limboto, for a total of 300 ha. The balances to the programs totalling 900 ha would be
rtained in the 'expansion' category until the uncertainties are resolved.
8.   Centml S    i. Potential groundwater development tend to be relatively small and scattered
areas, except along the lower flood plain of the Palu river, or in remote areas on the east coastal zone
of the province where the logistics of implementing development from a headquarters in Palu would
be ry difficult. The proposed subproject areas are therefore all in the fairly close vicinity of Palu.
Surface water supplies are not available and rainfall is generally low and unpredictable.
(a) Marawola is located on the right bank flood plain of the Palu river and would be an extension
of a pilot development undertaken under ISSP I which proved successful. The aquifers are
contained in an alluvial sequence and the water table is at shallow depth. It is planned to
develop about 600 ha using 1TIW facilities with wells of 50 m average depth designed for
discharges of about 10 Vls. About 50 percent of the facilities may require a sump to operate
centifugal pump units. The entire program is retained in the 'expansion' category because
certain agricultural development aspects require further investigation.
(b) BlroMaa is located on the Palu river flood plain opposite Marawola and hydrogeological
conditions are expected to be similar to those in that area. It is planned to develop 500 ha
using "ITW facilities with wells of 50 m average depth designed for discharges of about 10
I/s. About half the facilities are expected to require a sump to operate centrifugal pump sets.
Reflecting the agricultural development constraints experienced in Marawola, the 'go-ahead'
program is rticted to 100 ha and 400 ha are retained in the 'expansion' category.
