(c) Banawa is located on the west coast of the province, about 60 km by road from Palu. The
aquifer system is formed by alluvial fan deposits. It is planned to develop some 300 ha using
1TIrW facilides with wells of 60 m average depth designed for discharges of about 10 V/s.
About 50 percent of the facilities will require a sump in which to set the centrifugal pump
units. As this is a new development ara, 100 ha of the program are categorized as 'go-ahead'
and 200 ha are retained in the 'expansion' category.
(d) Dalaka is located on the east coast of the province about 60 km by road from Palu. The
aquifers are contained in alluvial fan deposits. It is planned to develop 300 ha using ITIW
facilities with wells of about 60 m average depth designed for discharges of about 10 I/s.
Sumps will be required to ensure operation of the centrifugal pump units. As this is a new
development area, 100 ha of the program are categorized as 'go-ahead' and 200 ha are
retained in the 'expansion' category.
9.   South Sulawesi Four subproject areas have been identified in this province, which was the
target of a number of pilot developments under the groundwater development component of ISSP-L
(a) Gowa Takalar. Successful pilot developments were undertaken in this subproject area under
ISSP I. The area is located about 25 km by road to the south of Ujung Pandang. The
aquifers are contained in a relatively thin alluvial section overlying a sandstone sequence with
water levels at shallow depth below the alluvial plain. It is planned to develop 1000 ha using
ITTW facilities. With single wells, depths will average about 50 m. In many cases, it may be
preferable to have multiple completion using paired wells, each of about half that depth,
tapping the alluvial aquifer. Pump discharges will be in the range of about 10-12 IUs, and
about half the facilities will require a sump in which to set the centrifugal pump. The entire
program is categorized as 'go-ahead' status.
(b) Maros is located about 20 km by road to the north of Ujung Pandang. A pilot developmnent
was undertaken in the area under ISSP I and proved groundwater conditions suitable for
development by ITTIW facilides, but indicated the need for caution regarding water quality.
The farmer response was good. The aquifer is formed by a limestone formation. The
subproject is planned to develop an additional 500 ha using ITTW facilities with wells of 50 m
average depth discharging about 10 I/s. Wells are expected to be completed 'open hole', and
sumps will not be required. The entire program of 500 ha is categorized as 'go-ahead' status.
(c) liamr is located on the west coastal plain about 80 km to the north of Ujung Pandang. The
aquifer is frmned by a limestone formation. Groundwater conditions have not been proven by
drilling, but are expected to be similar to tiose encountered around Maros, with the main
concern being the water quality. It is planned to develop 500 ha using ITrW facilities with
wells of 50 m average depth designed for discharges of about 10 Vs. Wells will probably be
completed 'open hole'. Sumps may be -'equired to ensure operation of centrifugal pump sets.
Development will be initiated with a pilot scheme of 100 ha categorized as 'go-ahead', with
400 ha retained in the 'expansion' category.
(d) Pangkajene is located on an extensive coastal plain about 40 km to the north of Ujung
Pandang and lying between Maros and Barru. Limestone formation underlies the plain.
Groundwater conditions have yet to be proved by drilling and a major constraint may be water
quality. The development will be by rlTW facilities with wells about 50 m deep designed for
discharges of about 10 /s. The wells will probably be completed in 'open hole', and sumps
may be required for the centrifugal pump units. It is planned to develop 500 ha, with a 'go-
ahead' program of 100 ha and 400 ha deferre' the 'expansion' category.
10. Ljmung. Two subproject areas have been identified in this province. Both are extensive and
could support much larger areas of development than are planned under the project:

(a) Rumbia. The poposed suboect ara is located on an extensive level plain with light sandy
soils, some 90 km to the northeast of Bandar Lampung. The aquifer system is formed by
sandy aluvium/colluvium underlain by the Upper Pelambmng sandstone formation with a
thiickness of 200 in. Existing tubew,lls. pr ly for ommumty water supply, indicate that
water levels dictate the use of DT  faciis Wells of avge depth 1 10 m for discharges of

completion in the Upper Pelambang sandstone, as thds would reduce well construcuton costs
and make for simpler well development. The major unknown is the response of the famers
to the proposed development with the population mainly transmnigrants. Therefore, an initial
't-ahead' development of 200 ha is planned which can be extended to 1000 ha (800 ha
&ferred in the 'expansion' category) if the  ults of the pilot scheme are encouraging.
(b) Ragi This is an extensive alluvial plain, located about 50 km to the southeast of
Bandar Lampung. It was reclaimed from swampland and setded in the 1980s. The soils are
heavy clays which are cropped with paddy in the wet season and some poor palawija in the
subsequent season. None of the land is inrigated. Dug wells for vilage water supplies
indicate shallow water tables, but tubewell prducti  is unproven. It is phnned to develop
the area with r7TW facilities using wells averaging Om deep with expected discharges of

'go-ahlad' program of 200 ha is planned with the wells performing an exploratory function.
ITis would be expanded by an additional 800 ha if the initial results are encouraging.
11. Agh. Two subproject areas have been selected in this province because they are close to
Banda Aceh. However, there are additional areas in the province where groundwater development
appears to be feasible.
(a) Agch2lain. This exensive alluvial plain is located near the town of Banda Aceh. Much of
the plain iS served by surface water iriatixon systems, but upwards of 1500 ha in three main
aeas are not included widtin the surface water comnd aras, though part of these will be
provided with drinage improvements Water tables are at shallow depth of less than 2.0 m
below land surface at the end of the dry season. Aquifer productivity has yet to be proved by
test production drilling, but the prospects of encounteing high yielding aquifers at relatively
shallow depth are considered to be good. It is planned to develop 600 ha of groundwater
imigadon under the project by lTIWfacilities based on wells of about 60 m depth designed
for discharges of about 10 I/s serving commnd areas of 10 ha. Reflecting uncertainties about
actual well productvity and farmers sponse to the provision of groundwater irigation, only
100 ha are included under the 'go-ahead' program with 500 ha retained in the 'expansion'
category. Close proximity of Aceh town should encourage cultivation of vegetables.
(b) Padan g Tjifain. This extensive alluvial plin is located on the east coast about 60 km by
road frm the town of Banda Aceh. Much of the plain is served by surface water irrigation,
but there are rclatively extensive areas which are not included within the surface water
commands. Moreover, the surface water availability is inadequate to provide a secure
irrigation service to the entire command area during the dry season. It is proposed to develop
600 ha of groundwater irrigation under the project using ITIW facilities with wells about 60
m deep designed for dischaes of about 10 /s to serve command areas of 10 ha. Reflecting
some uncertainty about aquifer  cty, 100 ha of the proposed prgram are categorized
as 'go-ahead', while 50 ha are retained under the 'expansion' category. Therm is a well
established system of vegetble producton (notably chillies) based on irrigation by hand
drawn water from shallow wells an part of the plain. lrduce is exported to other parts of

-97-                                Annex 7
GROUNWATER DETVPR ,OJENT
Qwundwater Monitoring    u1 Rlgulation
1.  The We  et Law of 1974 provides the basic policy statement for all
aspects of water resources development, including groundwater. Government  gulation No. 22 on
Wat ,er Management of 1982 gives the provinces the implicit right to allocate groundwater within their
areas of jurisdiction through the requirement that all groundwater use, except that taken from small
household wells for domestic use, shall be licensed by the Governors' office of the provincial
authoity. The regulation also vests technical oversight and approvai of groundwater development with
the Ministry of Mines and Energy (IME), which has delegated exercise of this power to its Directorate
of Environmental Geology (DEG). The Governors may exercise their regulatory powers by issuing
povincial decrees and regulatns with respect to groundwater development.
2.   es  Situation. Indonesia's groundwater regulation requirements are aimed at safe exploitation
of the resources to avoid over-development and pollution. Although the requirements defined by the
existing legislation are, in most respects, adequate to protect the groundwater resources if properly
applied, the funding, staffing, technical competence, and organizational arrangements presently in place
are insufficient to implement the regulatory functions. Moreover, there appears to be some lack of will
on the part of Government to fully apply the regulations and achieve their objectives.
3.  The MME has a mandate in law (para 1) to provide technical advice and approval for groundwater
development to the provincial authorities, and has delegated responsibility for these tasks to its DEG.
The work should involve groundwater data collection, resource evaluation, and technical monitoring of
groundwater development, including the response of aquifer systems to abstractions and the impact of
all activities on groundwater quality. However, the DEG is not staffed or provided with the budget to
carry out this mandate and the MME staff in the provinces are concerned only with mining related
activities. An MME Ministerial Decree for implementation of its mandate under the Government
Regulation No. 22 of 1982, requires the provincial authorities to submit information on groundwater
development to DEG for techmcal evaluation. But provinces lack technical expertise in groundwater
data collection procedures and the means to collect the information. Thus, the MME/DEG mandate
remains to all intent unfulfilled.
4.  Provincial licensing is almost entirely directed at industrial and conmnercial uses which attract a
user fee based on metered abstractions. These activities are generally located in, or in the close vicinity
of, urban centers and there is strong evidence that the licensing provisions are being circumvented by
many of these users. It is intended that irrigation and other agriculture related groundwater uses should
be licensed, but an abstracdon charge is not levied for such uses. Block licenses are provided by the
provinces for Government financed groundwater development for irrigation by P2AT. Essentially all
pnivate groundwater development for irrigation (located almo;+ entirely on Java, apart from minor hand
drawn abstractions) is unlicensed. Moreover, private development would be extremely difficult to
regulate as the only fixed asset is a hand-rilled or hand-dug hole in the ground which can be installed
at very low cost. Pump sets are mobile and are moved between wells, often under rental agreements.
5. The serious problems with regard to management of groundwater resources are now in the cities,
particularly in Jakarta, where overdraft and/or pollution of aquifers are matters of major concemn.
There is, as yet, no evidence for over-development of groundwater due to irrigation. Neither are
adverse effects on water levels in village water supply wells due to groundwater pumping for irrigadon
commonly reported. Nevertheless, there is a possibility in the future that overdraft could occur in some
areas if groundwater development for irrigation is not properly regulated.



-98 -                              Anne 2
Recent Actions in East Jav4 rovince
6.  The East Java Governor's pecree No. 700 of August 1992 and the related PRIS Decn
Technical l;Quida=n for {3rolladwatr (lnigation) Management are intended to rationalize the provincial
role in groundwater irrigation management. The Governor's Decree gives to PRIS the responsibility
for monitoring and evaluation of groundwater resources and for providing technical advice to the
Governor's office with respect to groundwater regulation and issuance of licenses. Thus, the Decree
clearly divides responsibility for groundwater irigation development between the P2AT (given
responsibility fro  the planning stage through to completion of the two years of O&M support after a
facility is comssioned) and PRIS (which will be responsible for long-term support after P2AT
completes its task).
7.  The East Java PRIS has recognized that these new responsibilities have requirements that are
outside its previous experience, particularly with regard to the groundwater monitoring and evaluation
aspects of the Governor's Decree. The PRIS has also recognized that it would: (a) require a
specialized unit with qualified staff, facilities and equipment to undertake and sustain the regulatory
assistance mandate; and (b) be necessary to draw on P2AT experience and resources during a transition
period before fully assuming responsibility for the newly mandated tasks.
Acdtons under the Proect
8.  It is beyond the scope of the proposed project to attempt improvements to the situation with
regard to groundwater regulation in the urban areas. Nevertheless, the intentions of the groundwater
regulations are correct and should be supported so far as is possible. Routine actions which could be
undertaken include:
(a) avoid obvious risks of over-development through scrutiny of the records of existing irrigation
weils in a development area as part of the site selection process; and
(b) take due account of the possibility of adverse impacts of project wells on village water supply
wells (providing water supply, when necessary, from project wells, as planned under the
project if there are any adverse effects).
9.  The project would support the initiatives taken in East Java under the Govemoes
Decree No.700 With regard to groundwater resource monitoring and evaluation, the project would:
(a)  strengthen the water level monitoring networks in groundwater irrigation areas of East Java
by provision of additional observation wells equipped with automatic water level recorders;
(b) provide equipment and materials to enable water quality monitorng;
(c) provide specialized training to selected engineers from the PRIS staff in the techniques of
groundwater monitoring and evaluation; and
(d) provide consultancy sevices to assist with the work.
10. The initiatives to be undertaken in East Java would be used as a model for actions in other
provinces with significant groundwater development. A project objective would be to develop, test and
demonstrate appropriate arrangements in East Java to enable lessons learned to be utilized in other
pjfject provinces. In anticipation of this, the project would provide for water level monitoring
networks, water quality monitonrng equipment, and training of key staff in groundwater resource
monitoring and evaluation for each of the project provinces, if requested. The actual program in each
project provinces would be confmed during the project annual review process.

GROUNDWATER D 
Brigion Water Ruwki
Intmduction
1.      The subproject areas are distributed in several islands of Indonesia. Individually, they
experience a wide variety of climatic conditions, and the soils range frm light texture with high
drainage characteristics to heavy texture with impeded drainage. However medium to heavy
textured soils predominate in the totality of the project area. Reflecting the above variability of
climatic and soil conditions, there is a large variety of crop options which can be grown with
irrigation, though the options may be reduced if markets are not readily available, pariicularly for
some vegetable crops. The preferred wet season crop is rice where soil conditions re at all
suitable. Notable exctions to this rice-dominated wet season cropping occur in parts of Lombok,
some areas in the vicinihy of Palu in Central Sulawesi, and around Rumbia in Lampung, all of
which have light, highly drained soils coupled with relatively deep water tables.
2.      Though cultivation of rice during the dry season is possible with irrigation in most
subproject areas, excepting those with adverse soil conditons, the iiaion water demands of rice
in the dry season are high. For this reason, water supplies provided by the groundwater facilities
ave not been sized t,o support dry season rice cropping over their total command areas. Moreover,
examination of exisung tubewell rgatdon operaions (for example in East Java) shows that many
farmers do not grow dry season rice, preferring to have more intensive cover of palawija crops
because of their lowe water demands. Therefore, for some subproject areas, the inigation water
demands have been estimated on the basis of cropping pattems with 100 pernt cover of paddy in
the wet season and 75-95 percent cover of palawija crops during the first and second dry crop
seasons. in some parts of Java and Sumatra where the rainfall is sufficient to attempt a second nce
crop, a partial cover of paddy is assumed during the first dry season. For exceptional axeas, such
as Lombok, a cropping sequence dominated by palawija crops has been used.
onai of Udid Requireent
3.      The crop irrigation water requirements have been derived or extrapolated for the various
subproject locaions from existing studies (prepared by consultants). All studies use essentially the
sae methodology for their water requirement calculations. For defining  water rwaIremnts
for facility design purposes, the effective rainfall from the 80 percent pbable rainfal in each half-
monthly period has been estimated. The evapotranspiration (El) of the crop has then been
calculated using the Modified Penman method for the esfimation of potential evapotranspiration
(ETo) with an appropriate crop coefficient (Kc) to reflect such crop charactenstics as stage of
ganth etc. This provides an estimate of a crop water requirement at the plant. This requirement is
then adjusted to take account of the cropping pattern and intensity, and fialy of the efficiency of
the irigaton water delivery and application system. The result provides an esdmate of the water
demand in each half-monthly period at the well head and the peak half-monthly water demand.
Similar calculations have been made using the S0 percent probable rainfall for each half-monthly
period to derive an ektimate of wat requirements n the average rainfill year for use in economic
analyses (para 6).
4.      The above exercise is carried out to define the water requirements at the well head for the
subproject areas and does so with ronable accuracy. However, as individual calculations have
not been made for all subproject areas, or for the plethora of cropping patterns which are pcssible
and could emerge, the water rquirements are to ths degree indicadve.



-100-                               Annex 
Pago 2
5.      C    m    ad Field Appication EM*=y. The planned irigation facilities will have
buried PVC pipe conveyance systems with each turnout serving about 1.0 ha. The piped sections
would have no losses attributable to leakage if the distribution systems are properly constructed,
although most of the alfalfa valves have at least slight leaks when closed. Thus, most of the losses
will occur in the earth channel conveyances from the outlet valves to the fields and from on-field
applications. A conveyance efficiency of 90 percent has been selected for rice cultivaton in the wet
season with an appropriate allowance for deep percolation losses to account for field application
efficiency. The combined conveyance and field efficiency with palawija crops has be ,n taken as 60
percent.
6.      Ewrg&p Re uixen . Effective rainfall conditions of the 80 percent probable rainfall
year are used to calculate irrigation requirements for sizing the tubewell facilities. However, the
water demand based on this situation cannot be used to estimate the pumping requirements of the
facilities for use in the project economic analyses. For such analyses, puirrping requirements to
meet crop water deficits under average rainfall conditions are more appropriate. Table 1 shows
monthly pumping requirements to meet the irrigation demands of the selected cropping patterns for
typical subproject areas in the project provinces under the average and 80 percent probable rainfall
conditions. The pumping requirements are expressed as mean daily pumping hours in each month
with an assumed water duty of 1.0 sec/a at the well head. The table shows that average daily
pumping requirements under average rainfall conditions only rarely exceed 18 hours/day in any
month in any project province. Moreover, the average daily pumping requirement does not exceed
22 hours/day in any month in any pkovince under more extreme conditions of 80 percent probable
rainfalls
7.     It should be noted that calculations of crop water requirements based on the Modified
Penman formula appear to produce high estimates in Indonesian conditions. There is no evidence
from existing tubewells that farmers actually apply water in the quantities indicated by Penman
calculations. Many existing tubewells appear to operate for substantially less hours than assumed in
the design and the crop yields and cropping intensities obtained by many farmers are not indicative
Of serious irrigation water deficits. It may, therefore, be assumed that the design water duty of 1.0
I/sec/ha is conservative except in for occasional conditions of extreme drought. Indeed, experience
may show that the tubewell facilities could be designed with a lower water duty, implying that
command areas could be expanded.
8.     It is recommended that the performance of existing tubewell systems be monitored and
evaluated to establish if fte project design assumpdons, in particular the design water duty, can be
modified to make the investments more economical and sociaUy beneficial. Such a study should be
given priority by the project consultants so that any lessons learned can be incorporated in future
work programs. The study would critically examine what farming activity, or cropping pattern
choice, contributes most to the water demand (and thus pumping hour peaks). Once the key
determinants have been identified, the inrigation practices of existing tubewell farmers should be
monitored and evaluated. It will then be possible to identify what changes in the design criteria are
acceptable and would have the most impact on reduction of costs per unit area served and the related
increase in benefits. Any potential increase in benefits would be achieved by expanding the
command area per unit volume of water delivered at the tubewelL

Indigated AgicultuLr Develnt
BackgmuAd
1  At present, groundwater irrigation development has been mainly resticted to providing an
irrigation service and assisting farmers in the setting up of water user associadons (WUAs) to manage
completed works. However, achievement of project benefits depends on all farners makdng the
transition frm rainfed to intensive irrigated agriculture. This requires intensive and specialized
extension support which is substantially different from that provided by existin agrcultural extension
services. The transition period presents a risk for fanners, since it requires adoption of new cultural
practices and the larger investments in production inputs for irrigated agriculture.
2.  Ag& ul om   Obieyes. Government has proposed an agricultural development
(AD) activity to support development of groundwater irrigation . The activity would be implemented
under the technical guidance of Ministry of Agriculture (MOA) at national level, with provincial
agcultural services (PRAS) providing direct support to farme;s and farmer groups. The main
objective of the AD component would be to assist the farmers in the transition from rainfed to irrigated
agriculture. Specific project objectives would include,: (a) strengthening of provincial agricultural
agencies responsible for project impact areas, particularly, the extension services; (b) training of
farmers in iridgation pracces and irgated agriculture; (c) encouragement of crop diversification; and
(d) assistance in provision of production inputs and development of specialized markets. Additional
obcives would be to develop secondary income opportunities (i.e., irrigation of house gardens) and
to enhance of the role of women in agricultural development.
Ag fultuial D=l0flL  Activities
3.  To achieve the above objectives, the project would support the following AD activities: (a)
MOA/PRAS participation in the project planning process; (b) formulation of a "location specific"
agricultural development plan for individual tubewells, or clusters of wells; (c) provision of intensive
assistance. and extension services to the farmers during the first period of tubewell operation; (f)
support for irrigated home gardening, (g) support of other activities to enhance the role of women in
irrigated agricultume; and (f) monitoring and evaluation of agricultural impact These activities would
be applied to new groundwater areas under the project, but may also benefit existing tubewells where
agricultural production has been less than expected. In addition, MOA/PRAS would undertake a pilot
promotion of STWs, where technically feasible, as a possible alternative to larger tubewell systems
implemented by DGWRD/PAT. These AD activities are described below.
4.  I    _=ion   Ariw1A uumlPln.  For each tubewell system, or cluster of wells, a location
sPecfc agriculturd plan would be drawn-up as integral part of the general development process. The
activity would be caried out intensively by the PRAS agricultural team in coordination with the P2AT
technical staff. The tasks would include: (a) assessment of the specific site agricultural potential; (b)
definition of the possible cropping patterns; (c) planning and scheduling of the extension activities and
involvement of other PRAS agencies; and (d) planning for farmers' trAning (including arrangements
for visits to existing tubewells). For each area, the design of the agriculturl plan would be carried out
duing the preparion and the implementation phases of the tubewell development process (Annex 4).
5.  S5al1xnsign S e. This activity represents the core of the agricultural development
component and would be carried out during the first two years after commissioning of a tubewell
system, or clusters of wells. Specialized PRAS staff would assist farmers by demonstrations of
improved crop varieties and high-value crops. There would also be demonstrations of on-field water
management techniques and other agricultural practices, including ferdlizers, pest control, post-harvest
pocessing and storage. In addition, PRAS staff would advise on crop diversification, specialized
mas4r.tc . sett.nn of onneadtives and monitoring and evaluation of agricultural production

8. Women-in-Development CWIIY. A basic objective would be to encourage and enhance the role
of women in the project process (Annex 12). Thus, the agricultural development plans for eachi
tubewell development would be decided with full participation of the women's groups to be served.
To achieve this objective, the organization and training of women's groups would be facilitated using
field level staff provided to develop WUAs, in conjunction with other agencies involved in organizing
WID activities at village level. Project staff would coordinate with groups promoting non-formal
education to assess whether and how an income generating activity could be used as course material in
their programs. At village level, existing PKK c ganizations would be involved in any WID activity.
9.  Monitoring andEvaluation CU&EM. The M&E of agricultural performance of the project would
be undertaken by PRAS. In most provinces, this would mean strengthening the existing M&E
process within the PRAS to enable agricultural impact of groundwater development to be separated out
from general provincial performance data. Thus, agricultural performance data for groundwater
irrigation facilities would be collected and analyzed foUowing standard formats so that inigation and
agricultural data are compatible. This would enable irrigated agricultural impact to be evaluated in a
systematic manner. To achieve this objective, the M&E units within PRAS would need to be
strengtheied and given sufficient resources and budgetary provisions to undertake their new mandated
task.

including training of PRAS staff; (e) monitoring and evaluation of agricultural activities; (f) WID
activities; (g) a pilot STW program; and (h) special studies and surveys.
11. ExnSi  S11ppZO. The project would finance about 50 irrigated agricultural development
workers (TADWs), together with related   ation and equipment. The IADWs would be full time
field wotkers to assist farmers in the transition from rainfed to ingated agriculture. The additional
IADWs would differ from existing PPLs because they would be specially trained in the agricultural

12. Crop Demonstration. Project demonstration plots are widely used by the MOAJPRAS in
Indonesia to show fanners new practices associated with inigated agriculture (water management, land
development, input application, new crops, etc.). The project demonstrations would follow the general
methodology and process used by MOA/PRAS with about 1,000 demonstration plots (one per 40 ha
for Java and one per 20 ha for the Outer Islands). Selected farmers would be given technical
guidance, inputs and support for land development to cover demonstrations on about 0.5 ha for two
years ( 'ur to six crop seasons). Labor would be provided by farmers who would own the crop
production. In addition, seine 1,000 demonstration plots for house garden agriculture would be also
implemented following the same methodology with one or two demonstations per village.
13. Institutional Strengtieing. Fun - swouldbe made available by theprject to strengthen existing
PRAS structure, namely the TGA and PPS at kabupaten level, the Mantri-tarm and the existing PPL of
areas developed with groundwater resources. The support received by the existing PRAS staff is
important to help ensure project success and sustainability since after the intensive assistance, each
tubewell would continue to receive the routine PRAS support. The existing staff would be trained in
irigated agriculture using groundwater through specific programs and by on-the-job training by the
consultants employed under the project.
14. Taining of Contact Fanms. Farmers training would be implemented by applying current MOA
methodology and programs, which is based on contact fanners. Some 1,000 contact farmers (five per
tubewell at the average) would be trained by the MOA staff and consultants during the initial stages of
groundwater development and in the first and third year after facilities are commissioned.
15. Technical Assistance. The project would finance consultant services to generally assist the
MOAYPRAS and to train key senior project staff. The services would be joint venture of foreign and
national consultants as detailed in Annex 13. The foreign consultants would include a senior
agronomist, supported by short-term specialists in other disciplines (agro-economics, institutions,
credit and marketing, etc.) which may be needed during the project period. National consultants
would provide irrigation agronomists, agro-economists and insdtutional specalists with short-term
specialist support. In addition, national consultants would provide a specialist for support of WID
activities.
16. M&F. Acdvties. The project would support M&E activities by financing the employment of
about 18 data processors to work with the DLRD within PRAS at provincial level. They would
process field data on the agricultural performance of the groundwater irrigation facilities, collected by
the field staff of PRAS and P2AT. Data to be processed would include pumping hours, cropping
patterns, cropping intensities, crop yields, and input quantities and prices. The data processing staff
would be temporarily employed by the PRAS for the project period.
17. WLD ActAtivids. The project provides lump sum funding for WID activities for a range of project
related activities (Annex 12). Specific programs to be decided once activities have been tested and
demonstrated on a pilot basis. Project support related to the role of women would be administrated
and coordinated through a small unit within the Directorate of Extension (MOA) at national level, with
PRAS undertaking similar responsibilities at the provincial level.

and socio-economic issues related to groundwater development. An initial study required would be a
baseline survey to establish the 'presen( situation and pioritize the AD acdvities to be tackled under
the proposed project. Another study would be a pilot program of shallow tubewell development
through prlovision of about 230 pump sets and shallow boreholes in areas with suitable groundwater
conditions and where the beneficiaries cannot afford these installations thiemselves.

Instiutonal Awect of (roundwater DM&kd=pm
1.   This Annex desctibes some of the more important project institutional aspects whicii should
be given specal attention ding project implementation. In this respect, the institutional setting
draws primarily on the existing stuaion in project provinces, but also includes experience from
other provinces (imarily Central Java) where extensive Government promoted and/or purely
private sector developmnt of£groundwater has occurd
Rolesof thie Ed= ndlPublic S
2.   Developwent of groundwater for irrigation using tubewells equipped with motor powered
pump units was first initiated on a large scale in East Java by the public sector in the 1970s. The
development was based on deep tubewels designed for medium to high discharges when equipped
with vertical shaft turbine pumps powered by diesel engines. As first conceived, the groundwater
supplies were intended to supplement surface water in irrigation commands where there were
surface water deficits in the dry season. The surface and groundwater supplies were delivered
through common channels, but the management difficulties of these supplementary irrigation
facilities soon became apparent. It was decided therefore that public sector groundwater
development would be based on one source of irrigation supply to defined command areas and not
conjunctive use. With increasing experience, it was also decided that public sector constructed
groundwater facilities would be handed over to water users associations (WUAs) for management.
3.   This mix of public and private sect involvement in groundwater development persists to
the present. Basicaly, planning, design and constructon of the facilities are the responsibility of
the public setor agenc (P2A1), and after facilites are commissioned, they are handed over to their
WUAs for manaeent, albeit with goverment suppor of initial O&M (fuel supply, payment of
pUM  Opeo  salar and some maintenance assistance for the first two years). This mode of
development has been applied in Java and, to a lesser extent, in some provinces of the Outer
Islands. The proposed project would strengthen this broad approach by ensuring full community
pardcipation by early establishment of WUAs so that they can be involved in planning, and design
of facilities (Annex 4).
4.   The private sector has a long history of groundwater development for irrigation using
shallow hand-dug wells with the water being manually abstracted. This mode persists to this day
for serving smal areas, often with vegetable cropping, as for example in the Sape and Keli areas of
Sumbawa. By the late 1970s, the private sector on Java had devised a technology for constructing
shallow tubewells (STW) for use with low dischare centrifugal pumps powered by light oil or
diesel engines. However, the technology is only suitable for areas where shallow aquifers can be
reached by the well construction method and where pumping water levels are within suction depth.
Nevertheless, the mode of development has expanded rapidly on Java. The development occurs
with several variations. Individuals own a well and pump for their own use and often sell water to
farmers on adjacent lands. Other farmers own only the well and hire the pump unit. Pumps are
purchased directly from private dealers, routine servicing is by the owners, and repairs are carried
out in local machine shops. Public involvement has been minimal, and institutional credit has
rrely been used for purchase of facilities1'.

5.      veral technical agencies are directly involved in public groundwater irrigation
develc -ti nent The subdirectorate office of Pengembangan Air Tanah (PA1) within the Directorate
of brigtion II (DOI-11) of the Drectorate Generl of Water Resources Development (DOWRD) in
the Ministry of PubLic Works (MPW) helps plan, coordinate and provide technical backstopping to
provincial groundwater irrigaton project offices known as P2AT. The centrally funded project
offices are being integrated into the Provincial Water Resources Development Services (PRIS) of
the Provincial Public Works offices in several provinces. While all but one of the project area
provinces have established P2AT offices, their capabilities vary widely. East Java has the best
established and most extensive organization with a provincial office in Surabaya and four subproject
offices. The Lombok office is also well established The P2AT office for North Sumatra has yet to
be established, and the offices in the remaining provinces are quite small at present.
6.   In theory, the provincial level P2AT offices are coordinated for local operations by the
Kantor Wiliyah Propinsi (provincial wings of the central public works agency), but in practice,
P2AT offices operate primarily under direct guidance of the PAT. P2AT takes responsibility for
implementing public groundwater irrigation projects, and presently assumes responsibility for
supervising scheme operations after construction, a role similar to that exercised by PRIS for
surface water irrigation systems.
7 .   JIrri dga  cultura elment. Agricultural extension is supposed to be provided
through the extension services within PRAS. The PRAS extension service involves a tiered
hiarchy of personnel extending down to extension agents operating out of subdistrict (kecamatan)
offices, each servicing some 300-1000 ha of agricultural land. In prctice, the role of the extension
services in groundwater irrigation development is limited for a variety of reasons, including budget
constraints, lack of interagency coordination, competing extension schedules, and personnel
limitations. The project makes provision to address this shortcoming (Annex 9).
8.   The project would be assisted and advised by a National Project Coordination Committee
(NPCC) led by BAPPENAS and including representatives from the Ministry of Public Works
(MPW), the Ministry of Agriculture (MOA), and the Ministry of Home Affairs (MOHA). The
coordination objectives would address issues related to: (a) policy review; (b) interagency
coordination; (c) overall physical, financial and impact of project implementafion; and (d) advice and
guidance on any matters pertining to the project. The NPCC would also liaise with other line
agencies that have relevance to the project to inform and coordinate, as required, on the activities of
the project. The lead implementing agency (PAT) would provide the secretariat to the NPCC. At
provincial level, the project would be assisted by a Provincial Project Coordination Committee
(PPCC) led by each BAPPEDA and include representatives from the PRIS and PRAS and the other
concerned agencies of provincial government.
9.        1.   Local govemments at provincial, kabupaten (district) and kecamatan
levels are responsible for coordinating concerned agencies involved in groundwater irrigation
development and scheme operations. The primary mechanisms are Interagency Irrigation
Committe  (TIC) at each government level, of which the most strategic is the district committee
under the aegis of the Provincial Officer (Bupati). The llCs vary widely in their degree of activity
and prformance, and their structue, at present, is almost endrely oriented to planning operations of
surface water irrigadon schemes. This deficiency would be addressed under the project.
10.  At the provincial level, regular coordination meetings would be held to identify roles and
responibilities, prpare yearly budget allocations for each area of operational support, and make
provision of staff time, during the annual planning and programming process. The project

CommnJtv Parlidgtion
11. The basic policies required to obtain genuine community participation have already been
incorporated in the developmental framework of the Gcvernment and are expressed in various
Government Decrees, in particular, those related to WUAs (para 2, Annex 11). The main principles
include: (a) sustaining efective performance of services through genuine community participation
in the project planning, design, construction, operation and maintenance processes; (b) integration
of the needs and perceptions of women and other disadvantaged groups into the development
process; (c) strengthening program flexibility and responsiveness to obtain the necessary degree of
community participation; (d) efficient training and support for beneficiary community groups; and
(e) collaboration with other Government agencies and non government organizations working in
rural programs.
12.  The project would thus adopt a participatory development approach (Annex 4), primarily to
facilitate the process by which the farmers and other users of groundwater assume their
responsibilities in the development process. The implementing agencies will therefore need to
orient and train their staff in participatory development techniques and procedures. Project staff and
field workers well versed in such techniques would be one of the keys to the project's success.
1-3.  PEDjg&t Staffing at Field Level for Conmnuitv dQa    The project would finance the

1.    Basic project objectives are to minimize public sector support and encourage and enable
effective community participation in all phases of the groundwater development process. Thus,
Government financed and constructed groundwater facilities would be handed over to established
fanmers groups for management immediately after completion of construction, albeit with some initial
O&M support from Government. An essential activity to achieve this turn over the tubewell facilities
will be to set up, train and support water user associations (WUAs) which are able to manage and
sustain the tubewel} systems.
2.   Legal provisions regarding WUAs reflect both national and provincial legislation. The 1982
Presidential Instruction No. 2 on Establishing Water User Farmer Associations prescribes
responsibilities for establishing WUAs to the Mnistries of Public Work, Home Affairs and
Agriculture. The instruction serves as a model for provincial WUA decrees, and all project
provinces have established decrees which define the legal framework "or WUA organization,
development and operation.
3.   These provincial decrees grant WUAs the right and responsibility to privately develop and
manage locally based water sources (springs, wells, seasonal surface waters) or to manage state
owned irrigation systems, in part or entirely. Although legal ownership of the facilities remains with
Government, WUAs are given the rights to: elect their own officials; establish locally adapt bylaws
as adjuncts to model WUA constitutions; own property; and to raise their own revenues, mcluding
collectig of irrigation fees. In 1992, the M(5HA issued a Regulation No. 12 which, inter alia,
establishes the WUAs as legal entities with contractual rights under national law. This new
Reguladon should greatly strengthen the status of WUAs since it will enable WUAs to open bank
accounts in the association's name and thus enable WUAs to have access to credit facilities.
Opsradiof FtaciHties
4.    Actual rules of operation are set by individual WUAs to suit the specific needs and
perceptions of their members. In some cases, P2AT offices have attempted to establish day/block
systems of rotational water supply. These were originally devised for groundwater irrigation
facilities with large comand areas and commensurably large numbers of beneficiaries in East Java.
Such systems are required during peak demand periods if all beneficiaries are to be served in an
equitable and timely manner. However, the system is often violated (and may not be str.iuTy
necessary) in off-peak periods when facilities tend to be operated essentially on-demand. Operations
on Madura, where tobacco is mono-cpped in season, present a special case whereby the entire
command is served daily into small storage reservoirs from where the tobacco plants are irrigated by
hand. It is evident that operating procedures for the irrigation facilities must remain a decision of the
concemed WUAs, though advice is often necessary from either P2AT or the agricultural extension
service.
5.     1 1MiQfl R1sKcPig. If WUAs are to be fully accountable for facility operations, it
is essential that proper records ae maintained of pump operation hours, fuel deliveries and
consumption, deliveries of water to individual beneficiaries, routine and special maintenance
expenditures, and facity serviceability. Generally, the task of keeping such records rests with the
tubewell operator, though a system of counter-checking by a WUA official is necessary. The quality



- 110-                            Annex II
Page, 2
of operational record keeping are extremely variable. To address this shortcoming, standard
procedures and books would be devised by the project to provide full, accurate and easily checked
operational records for groundwater irrigation facilities established under the project. An essential
requirement of this record keeping effort would be that tubewell operators employed by WUAs are
literate and reasonably numerate.
6.    Water ChaTs and Cost Rhoev The level and form of water charges to be made to the
beneficiaries for the irrigation service are decided by the WUAs of individual facilities, as are the
conditions of payment of the charges. Advice on setting charges is typically provided by concerned
P2AT officials when the WUAs are established. In most cases, water charges are volumetrically
based, relating to time of pump operation to srvice an individual beneficiary. Water charges are
normally set to cover routine O&M costs (fuel, operator etc.), and repairs to equipment. They
should also cover eventual replacement cost of at least the pump set. During the inidal two year
period of O&M support from Government, water charges are made so that WUA q accumulate funds
to ensure their futwe financial viability. Repayment arrangements for water charges are the decision
of the concerned WUA and are variable. Some impose only a short grace period for payment of
dues and failure to pay is penalized by termination of the irrigation services. Other WUAs allow a
considerable grace period, often to the end of the crop season, which essendally constitutes an
internally operated credit system A/.
7.    Finance Accounting and Bank Accounts. In the past, different groundwater irrigation
development projects have proposed various systems of financial bookkeeping for WUAs. While
the required bookkeeping system should be simple, it should also be complete, transparent and easily
checked by association officials and members, and by Government officials responsible for
monitoring the project. An appropriate and standardized system of financial bookkeepmg would be
devised and extended, together with standard books, to WUAs established under the project.
8.    As WUAs accumulate funds that are, in effect, the common property of their members, it
may be necessary to establish bank accounts for the safe keeping and use of such funds. In some
cases, bank accounts have been opened in the name of the WUA. In other cases, accounts are held
in the joint names of WUA officials and require two or more signatures to carry out a transaction
involving a withdrawal of funds. Either of these arrangements would be appropriate for the banking
requirements of the WUAs to be established under the project.
Guidance forWUAs
9.    Muli-agency support for groundwater initiatives should be targeted at WUAs, since these are
the basic groupings of beneficiaries served by the project. This support can best be channeled
tdrough WUA guidance teams, as has occurred in Central Java. The guidance teams may have
differing membership and differing roles, depending on the hierarchical level.
10.   P oincial Committees Provincial groundwater irrigation WUA committees should include
multi-agency membership, comprising selected departments from the provincial Governors office,
BAPPEDA, PRIS-P2AT, Department of Agriculture (Extension; Water Management), Cipta Karya
(Domestic Water Supply), etc. Leadership could be vested in the Production Division of the
Govemors office, with the deputy leadership position going to BAPPEDA. Such comnittees could
meet twice a year, or as needed. The functions of the provincial committees should be directed to
general planmng and policy concerns, including supportive legislation/decrees, agricultural support
services, envirnmental issues, special provisions and dispensations for drilling permits, financial
policies, etc.

WUA Formation and OeainlSW"a Strategie
13.  Efforts to form viable WUAs in groundwater irrigation systems must be based on carefully
programmed strategies, sequenced with technical development activities, in which farmer
beneficiaries are consulted from the start. Such "participatory" strategies, if carefully scheduled and
adhered to, should not cause any delays in project implementaton and should improve the chances
hat newly formed WUAs become effective (Annex 4).
14.  Groundwater irrigadon WUAs have special technical, financial, and administrative needs that
must be addressed. Because groundwater systems are totally dependent upon the operation of their
pumping equipment, the availability of repair facilities are critical elements for developing farmer
confidence and foroptimizing performance. Likewise, the process of evolving sound financial and
management proceres, as well rganiozational growth, can benefit from external backstopping and
support
15.  The primary responsibility for promoting WUAs for groundwater facilities should be in the
hands of P2AT, backstopped by the agricultural extension agents. Special sub-professional "field
organizers" attached to the executing agency can assist this process, but participation by professional
staff will be necessary, particularly during early phases. WUA promotion/formation campaigns
would conlsist of the following sequence of actiities:
(a)  he Ca=ign1. Pre-camnaign meetings (prior to setting up a WUA) should be
undertaken in all sites identified for development, prior to the commencement of drilling.
Potential beneficiaries, farmer leaders and village officials, are invited to attend. Where
several well-sites are scheduled for individual villages, all such sites should be represented.
The purpose of the pre-campaigns is to explain project strategies and future farmer
obligations, and to secure commitment to operate and maintain schemes. Such pre-
campaigns play strategic roles in securing farmer commitment and to instill a sense of
participation, ownerip, and responsibility from the start.
(b) POnnal WUA  ntducio and Sys= Design Review   nce a tubewell drilling program
is successfully completed and potential sites are identified as technically viable, all farmers
in a proposed command area would be invited to attend a formal meeting to review project
strategies and schedules, and WUA organizational and management aspects. At this
meeting proposed system facility designs would be presented and reviewed.
(c)                                               . Following a period of informal
contacts to establish a consensus on potential leaders, formal meetings would be staged,
attended by all WUA farmer members, to appoint leaders, to formally establish the WUA,



-112 -                             AnnexI
and to establish its constitution. The timing of such fornation meetings should precede
commencement of WUA operations by some 1-3 months.
(d) Establishment of WUA Bylaw  Meetings to setup WUA by-laws usually occur just prior
to commencement of scheme operations, and again follow informal contacts with WUA
and village officials and key farmers. This meeting should follow completion of
distribution system construction and would be open to all the WUA membership.
(e)  WUA Officer Training WgIkshua Just prior to conmencement of WUA operations,
short (2-3 day) workshops on WUA organizational, administrative, and financial
management procedures are staged for all WUA officers. These workshops would
generally be conducted for several WUAs jointly to save staff resources. Training for
pump operators would also be conducted in six-day workshops.
16.  Post-Formaton WUA Operadnal Sport. Systematic programs of post-formation WUA
operational support are needed to cover a variety of aspects. These include plot level water
management, planning and management of cropping patterns and their water demands, irrigation
management including rotational schedules, improvement of skills of WUA officers in management
of finances and record keeping, establishment of WUA bank accounts, administration of operational
subsidies, and other forms of operational monitoring.
WUA Perfonn    Monitorng
17.   While operational monitoring covers a variety of facets, the WUA responsibilities include
recording, analysis and reporting of data on irrigation scheme performance. Such data would be
collected initially by pump operators and other WUA officials and recorded to standard format in
record books, primarily for the purpose of intemal WUA management and control. It would
therefore include records of cost recovery and finances. The data should be processed so that it can
be easily used and collated by field staff for use in their reports prepared for the sub-district oversight
committees and for P2AT. Operational data collected from groundwater irrigation schemes should
be designed to specifically meet performance monitoring objectives. In this respect, irrigation
performance data would need to be supplemented by agro-economic data on production costs and
crop returns collected in seasonal surveys. These data sets, should be checked on a sample basis by
government staff and processed and analyzed by the project M&E units within the P2ATs and
PRAS. Performance data should be made available to the respective PRIS for long term monitoring
records. The consultants would help to design and implement monitoring methodologies and tasks.
Servicing and R      of EQuipWnt
18.  Mechanisms need to be evolved to provide backstopping repair facilities for engine and pump
equipment. Pump operators would be trained to undertake minor servicing and repair of mechanical
equipment. By taking advantage of fuel subsidies provided by P2AT during the first two years of
operation, WUAs should be able to build up a capital reserve fund from irrigation fee collections for
use in funding equipment repair. The WUAs would be encouraged to keep any capital reserves in a
WUA bank account
19.  Firm arrangements need to be made to handle major repairs and breakdowns in a timely
manner, particularly if there are standing crops in the field. One option would be to adopt the
arrangements recently introduced in East Java with the PRIS to provide long term technical support
Provisions of post-procurement maintenance and servicing contract packages with equipment
dealers, as is planned in Central Java, is another option which should be further evaluated.

INOWNESIA
GEOMNWATER DOQPMENM PROJEC
Il Role of Women in 2Grounft;X UCyIoMAt
BackgQnd
1.  Indonesia is one of the most culturally diverse countries in East Asia with almost 300 distinct
cultures. Different kinship systems (patrilineal, matrilineal and bilineal) determine land ownership.
The society is pluralistic and a strongly influenced by "ADAT (customary law), various religions
(Islam, Buddhism and Hinduism) and indigenous beliefs. The Government formally recognizes that
ownership, property rights, marriage and family and kinship relationships are regulated by ADAT.
In this respect, women's participation in most parts of Indonesia is not inhibited by high levels of
gender segregation which characteize many other parts of Asia
2.  Women have a right to inherit and own property, including agricultural land Javanese rural
women can inherit property, including sawah (paddy) land both under the Islamic law and customary
inheritance practices. The 'gono-gini' (customary Javanese marriage law) states that all property
acquired by the couple is considered joint property. Two recent surveys in East Java have reported
that some 25 percent and 17 percent of the land owners were women respectively. Traditionally
Indonesian women have a strong decision making role within the household and are involved in
water use and management. They handle money from sale of agricultural produce as well as off-
farm employment.
Women in Agigulme
3. Women in Indonesia play an important role in agriculture. They are involved production as
weJl as in production, processing, storage and marketing of secondary food crops and small animal
husbandry. However, their contribution is largely as unpaid family labor. Consequently, women
are not generally recognized as farmers for technical support and extension and insufficient attention
is paid to enhancing their role in agricultural productivity. The proportion of female labor in the
agricultural sector has increased as men move to non-agricultural employment opportunities in the
economy. In 1961, some 73 percent of the male labor force was employed in agriculture compared
to 69 percent of the female work force. By 1989, agricultural labor participation by males had
reduced to 56 percent compared to 57 percent for women.
4.  Since land holdings are small, most farmers rely on family labor for farming In some areas,
such as Java and Sumatra, small farmers rely heavily on exchange labor. Agriculture is practiced as
a community activity with groups of women undertaking tasks such as rice transplanting. While
land preparation and application of pesticide and fertilizer for paddy is done by men, transplanting
and weeding are typically done by females. However, recent surveys by the Directorate of
Extension (MOA) indicate that more women are becoming involved in handling and use of
pesticides.
5. There is an increasing demand on women's time and energy in areas with groundwater
development due to multiple cropping and intensive cultivation. On the other hand there are regions
like Palu, Mataram and Lombok where theree is a potential for involving women in economic
activities, preferably operating out of or near the homestead by udlizing GW faciliies.
6. The role of women becomes more important in areas where temporary male migration takes
place for off-farm employment. For example, in West Madura, extensive male migration reduces
available farm labor and strongly influences agricultural activities. Men temporarily migrate to
Surabaya, Jakarta and even Thlailand for work and arrange their travel to be available during land
preparation and harvesting of wet season paddy. In these situations, women left behind find it
difficult to meet the labor demands for increased cropping intensity made possible through irrigation.



-114-                             Anmex 12
7.  wg=  aTd he Ac           x3nsim Sevcs Te Directorate of Extension uses a training
and visit methodology to promote technology packages, which include seed, pesticide, ferilizer and,
in some cases, credit. These packages are designed centrally and thus may often not be fully relevant
to local situations. The key person in the extension system is the PPL (field extension worker) of
which about 10 percent (some 3,600) are women. Women are not formally excluded from access to
extension services, but the program has inherent gender biases. For example, a recent sample
survey (carried out under an IFAD financed project for training of farmers on surface irTigation
schemes) revealed that about 65 percent of women had never met the PPL and 50 percent of them did
not know his duties. One task of the PPL is to organize farmers into groups called 'lompok Tani',
many of which have very few women participants. Nevertheless, there are about 320,000
'Klompok Tani Wanita' (women's groups) in Indonesia, each with some 20 to 50 members. Their
activities and inpact are dependent on local initiative.
Leassos Leaed related to WID AM=ct of Agricutural DOMelg=met
8. A recent Bank discussion paper on WID in Indonesia I/concludes that, despite Government's
sound and well-focused strategy towards the involvement of women in development, the
Government, donors, and society will have to continue to work on a range of improvements if
women are to truly become equal partners. Improvements required include: social services and
infrastructure; employment and productivity; the institutional framework; and social and cultural
change. Several country reviews undertaken by the Bank have also reported that, in spite of the key
role that women play in irrigated agriculture, projects tend to overlook their interests. In recent
years, a number of irrigation projects have made attempts to address the issue of female farmers.
Assessments of these projects has indicated that the WID components have been an 'add on', almost
as an afterthought. A recent assessment of the FAO supported On-Farm Water Use and Water
Management Training and Development Project indicated that project documents had made no
reference to the role of women in agriculture, and in indirect reference, they were quoted negatively
as 'possible socio-econonic constraints to be evaluated'. The main lesson learned is that the role of
women should be integral element of the project design from the preparation state.
EmStrategy forWID
9. Aspects and activities related to the role of women would be an integral part of the project
planning and design and the participatory development process involving the local community. In
this respect, the proposed project would provide resources and be formulated to ensure women are
specifically reached and fully involved in all activities where they have a legidmate role. The project
strategy would address the following objectives:
(a) WID interventions would take into account location specific features related to socio-
cultural and agro-economic determinants. Planning would be based as far as possible on
available existing data to avoid duplicating the cost of carrying out detailed surveys.
Selected site data for monitoring and unpact assessment would, however, be collected.
(b) The WID focus would be directed on social benefits and increase agricultural productivity
of female fanners.
(c) Income generation activities would be designed to benefit from economy of scale by
developing a village as whole rather than a few households. Action programs would be
designed to have a demonstradon impact and catalyze further spread.
(d) WID related trining would include non-formal education and have sufficient flexibility to
reflect lessons learned as the project proceeds.
/ Ionesia Wome in Development - A Str  for Continued Progress, Es Asia and Pacific Regional
Studies, Country Dqm  , World Bank, April 1992.



-115-                           PAn=x12
(e) Care would be taken to include all female participants in the program and not limit
participation to land owners, since in Indonesia, the issue of how poor households get
access to project benefits is more significant than status issues related to women.
(f) Progress would be continuously monitored and evaluated to document lessons learned.
10. Special attention would be given to ensure fthat potential benefits to women are achieved.
Thus, the project would include: (a) reflection of gender issues in baseline surveys and proposals
for individual components; (b) inclusion of women issues in orientation and training activides of
local government and community leaders, project management and implementation staff, and
community based organization (CBO) members; (c) integrl involvement of women in CBO/WUA
arrangements for the planning, design, construction, O&M and monitoring of project schemes; and
(d) development of relevant socio-economic, skill and management training programs.
Acthon Pl
11. The project would focus on five key areas to enhance and encourage the role of women
including: (a) participation in groundwater use for domestic water supply; (b) irrigation of house
gardens; (c) participation in management of groundwater development as full members of WUAs; (d)
improved access to agncultural extension services; and (e) separately identified documentation and
dissemination of WID lessons learned.
12. Domestic Water Suppl. Low income women typically spend a significant amount of time
transporting water for a variety of household consumption and productive needs. Conveniently
located public taps offering a reliable source of water represent a time saving which can be applied to
ongoing or new productive and commercial activities. The domestic water supply component of the
project has been conceptualized and designed to meet these needs.
13. Houseard Irrigad2n. House gardens are found all over Indonesia though they are more
charcteristic of Java where the ecology has been supportive. Herbs, medicinal plants and fruits are
commonly grown. It is estimated that some 130,000 hectres are cultivated as house gardens in East
Java. The house garden area in Central Java was reported to average about 250 sq. meters. Banana
and coconut can be found in virtually all gardens with cassava is the most popular seasonal crop. In
addition, a minority of households grow vegetables in the house gardens and keep fowl, goats, and
pigs. Home garden activities are characterized by minimal inputs and by a low productivity. In
many areas, provision of irrigation supply would alleviate a substantial risk and greatly expand the
opportunities for productive enterprises. With irrigation assured, appropriate packages of seeds
would be distributed to project beneficiaries to sponsor improvements in house garden cultivation.
Thus, about 1,000 small scale demonstrations designed for house gardens would be organized and
advice made available on cultivation technology and marketing produce.
14. Women's Role in WUAs. The project would promote women's involvement in water user
associations (WUAs) for resource management and related activities. In order to vocalize women's
special needs in irrigation systems, WUAs would be encouraged to have a female member on the
WUA management committee. Moreover, if the land is owned by a female, she should be the
registered member and encouraged to participate in the WUA. In this respect, the customary pmctice
has been that female landowners have been represented by their husband or father. Recent agrarian
reforms by Government are encouraging provincial agencies to streamline registration and issue tides
for land in the name of both the husband and wife. The influence cf these reforms need to be taken
into consideration when forming WUAs.
15. Acest A uturalExSe . Recognizing the need to address issues related to
wOmen's poductivity in agriculture, the Replita IV formulated a policy for training women in
agrcultural sklls for fanning and use of appropriate technology for production, storge, processing
and marketing of agricultural produce. Progress in extending this training initiative has been slow,



- 116-                                .&flil§An 12
Page, 
primarily due to a general weakness in the agriculture extension services. The lower literacy of
women has also been pointed out as a problem in extension. There is therefore a need for a modified
approach to reach women by using non-fornal education techniques.
16. To address these shortcomings, training of PRAS extension staff would emphasize the need
and ways to encourage women's participation in groundwater development. Training material for
WIID project activities would be produced by the Directorate of Extension after a review of existing
material. Instructions issued to the involved subdivisions of PRAS would contain explicit reference
to women as being among key farners to be contacted and motivated. The WID focus group within
PRAS would be responsible for designing teaching material for use in the training programs. The
extension field staff would organize: (a) demonstration plots managed by women for training in crop
diversification; and (b) introduction of a simple labor saving devices for threshing and similar
faming activities.
17. Mhi1g ng and Evaluation. The WI) aspects of the project would be monitored at two levels.
The Directorate of Extension (WID Focal Group) would be .esponsible for monitoring, reporting,
documenting and disseminating lessons learnt on WID aspects of the project. The World Bank
supervision missions would specifically report on the progress made on WID activities during annual
reviews of the project.
Institudgnal ngeM= Rfor Lmaion of a WID Action PIaBt
18. W    F. In principle, aU WI) activities are an integral part of the project and would
be implemented as a part of agriculture development component. However, to ensure that WID
activities have the appropriate priority in the annual work programs, a WID Focal Group would be
set up within the Directorate of Extension (MOA). The group would take the lead role under the
project in coordinating WID activities. The WFG would be represented on the Project Coordination
Committee at the National level, together with a representative of the Ministry for Role of Women.
The Directorate of Extension in PRAS would be responsible for developing special strategies to
enhance role of women in each of the project provinces. Where necessary PRAS would coordinate
and collaborate with other Government institutions with an interest in WI] involvement, including
the Women's Study Centers and cadres such as PKK 1/ at the provincial and desa levels.
19. An important objective of the WID Focal Group will be to involve the Women's Study
Centers. These centers have been established by the Ministry of Education in most of the project
provinces and cary out basic research on socio-economic aspects of women's status to assist the
Government in planning and implemeiiting development program The center teams usually consist
of a multi-disciplinary faculty and staff for field research. A competent consultant would be
employed under the agricultural development consultancy (Annex 13, Attachment 2) to assist and
advise the WID Focal Group.
20. Since WIID activities under the project are new to groundwater development, special attention
will be given to progress at the project annual reviews. Lessons learned would be evaluated and the
WID strategy modified as required to best achieve the project WI] objectives.
I/  The PKK (Pembln  Kesejahterean Kelurga) is a nationwide network of women cadres which grew out of a
women's movement in Central Java in 1967. By the end of 1971 almost all villages in Central Java had
esabished PKK Groups. Recognizing the potential of the movement to involve women in development
progam (parcarly Family Planing), the movement received strng Govaenment support. By the end of
1983, PKK was incpated in state legislation and PKK cadres have now been established in most villages in
the vroject provinces.

RNDONSIA
Pement Arangmn
1. Gneral. The procurement arrangements under the project are designed to achieved the
following objectives: (a) quality control through standardization of specifications and terms and
conditions of the contracl conditions, (b) competitive bidding by maintaining the stqndeized
contract which enable comparative analysis; (c) encouragement of local contractors for activities
where local contracting capability are available; (d) ease of management and supervision by
Government and the Bank; and (f) appropriate and simple technology which can be replicated
through the project provinces. Thus, contracts for works and goods would be based on model bid
documents approved by the Government and Bank. The procurement plan is given in Table 4.3 of
the main text.
2.   Review of            Although no civil works contracts are expected to cost more than
US $ 1.0 million, any works contracts exceeding that amount and equipment and materials
contracts estimated to cost IJS $ 200,000 equivalent or over, would be subject to the Bank's prior
nwiew. It is expected that about 16 contracts and about 95 percent of the goods by value, would be
subject to prior review. As works would be procured following LCB procedures and their size
would be small (less than US $ 1.0 million), these contracts would be subject to random post-
award review. Contracts of lesser value for works and goods would be based on model bid
documents approved by the Bank and Govenment. Invitation documents, proposed awards and
contracts for consultant services would be reviewed by the Bank.
3.   Works. Project procurement of works would be undertaken by the implementing agencies
at national and provincial levels. The civil works (US $ 27.6 million), including construction of
irrigation and domestic water supply facilities and observation wells, would be widely dispersed at
many subproect locations in eleven provinces. There would be two types of contracts: (a) some
45 contracts forconstruction of the boreholes and completion of the tubewell facility; and (b) about
45 contracts for construction of the buried type distribution systems and other minor civil works
(pump house, etc.). All contracts will be packaged as much as possible, however, none are not
expected to exceed US $ 1.0 million with the equipment and construction materials supplied by the
P2AT (para S below). These works contracts would be small to encourage local provincial
contrs and would therefore be implemented under LCB contts let by the concerned P2AT
provincal offices as tentatively indicated in Table 1 attached. This procurement schedule is based
on the implementation schedule shown on Annex 1, Table 6 and reflect the cost esimates for the
various project activities given in Annex 3.
4.   Construction of pump houses and instlatdon of buried pipe distribution systems would be
undertaken by local contractors administered by the P2AT office procured using standard bid
documents approved by the Bank. Under these contracts, all special pipe materials and alfalfa
valves installed would be provided from P2AT stocks procured nationaly (through the PAT ofrtce)
under ICB or LCB procedures, as appropriate (pam 7 below). PVC pipes, pumps and diesel
engines would be procured under ICB procedures with installation undertaken departmentally by
P2AT staff.
5.   Te procurement arrangements would be carefuly designed to: (a) enable use of standard
bid documents; (b) encourge focal contuactors, particuarly for the drilling of tubewell bore holes;
and (c) standadize equipment and materials through centrally administ   contra  The number
and timing of dtese conaws would reflect the rate of project implementation (Annex 1, Table 6).
The poject pcurment schedule would be revised annually to take into account the updated
implementation schedule for each provincal work pm    Tus, pcment scheduling would
