- 124.                                     Page, 2
be an important detenninant for the preparation of annual budgetary requests, which in tunm will
decide the project administration requirements and staffing requirements of each P2AT office.
6.   Goods Procurement of goods would consist of large packages of materials and equipment
(US $ 27.6 million) required for construcdon of the tubewell facilities (well casings and screens,
drilling equipment spare parts, drilling bits, drilling mud, PVC pipes and fittings for distribution
system, diesel motor powered pump sets) and would attract foreign competition. Procurement of
these goods would therefore be undertaken centrally through the PAT office in Jakarta under ICB
procedures using model bid documentation. The equipment specifications would be standardized
to facilitate the O&M of the items procured. Tenders issued by PAT would be in appropriately
sized packages to reflect the implementaion progress and to be attractive to responsible foreign
manufacturers. The packaging of contracts would be decided by PAT within DGWRD after
consultation (on progress and requirements) with concerned project provincial offices (P2ATs).
For bid comparisons, local manufacturers would be allowed a 15 percent margin of preference of
the CI.F. pnce or the applicable customs duty, whichever is lower.
7.   In addition to the main goods contracts, small amounts of materials (US $ 0.9 million)
which can be fabricated locally (outlet valves, PVC storage tanks for DWS works, etc.) would be
procured by LCB tenders let by provincial project offices, following standard specifications issued
by the PAT office. Most other equipment (US $ 0.7 million), including office and survey
equipment and computers, would be required by scattered project offices in the provinces.
However, to encourage quality control, procurement of these goods would be centralized using
lists and specifications drawn up by the project coordinadon unit after consultation with provincial
project offices. The procurement would be undertakne following LCB procedures acceptable to
the Bank (US $ 0.4 million), or through internadonal and local shopping (US $ 0.3 million).
Foreign firms would not be interested in the goods to be procured under LCB procedures, however,
they would not be excluded from bidding.
8.   Government regulations prohibit the importation of vehicles. Therefore, vehicles estimated
at about US $ 0.7 million, would be classified as reserved procurement and would not be financed
under the Loan.
9.    Sice   Services related to survey, investigation and design (US $ 0.9 million),
agricultural support (US $ 1.0 million), institudonal support (US $ 5.9 mfillion), initial O&M
(US $ 3.2 million), and training activities (US $ 1.9 million) would not be suitable for international
compedtion since these activides are widely scattered, small in size, and scheme specific, both in
locadon and scheduling. In addition, they would take place over the six year project period. They
would thus be underten, either departmentally following selected procedures acceptable to the
Bank (US $ 7.6 million), or by local consultant services (US $ 5.3 million) employed in
accordance with the Bank's Guidelines for the Use of Consultants.
10.    nultanservices. (US $ 11.4 million). The two consultancy contracts would be for
groundwater irrigation development employed by DGWRD and agricultural development
employed under DGCFA within MOA as described in Annex 13. Both consultancies would be
procured under Bank Guidelines for the Use of Consultants.
11.  Scial studies (US $ 0.6 million) would be decided as the project proceeds and address
such subjects as, inter alia: appropriateness of technology; groundwater resource monitoring and
evaluation; and agricultural support of community and WID activities. Studies would be
undertaken by consultant services employed in accordance with the Bank's Guidelines for the Use
of Consultants.

GROUNDWATE,R   EVE           SPRJC
Projc Inm t on the Phy.dcal Environment
1.    The project is not expected to have a negative impact on the physical
environment.since it does not involve disturbance of land forms. The agriculturl development
would take place on lands which are, at present, used for cultivation of field craps. Thus, the
project would not involve clearance of forest or any other natural vegetation and, therefore,
would not have any negative impacts on the habitat of wildlife. The development is not
expected to create dminage problems, or cause soil deterioration such as salinization. In fact,
groundwater development for irrigation would generally improve the drainage conditions of
agricultura lands when they have a natural tendency to water logging.
2.     Groundwater development for irrigation can be planned and implemented as discrete
module units (i.e., each individual tubewell is a separate unit), and thus the development impact
on the aquifer system can be monitored from the outset. This process would provides timely
warning of any adverse effects so that mitigating actions can be taken before irreversible
damage occurs. Furthermore, the M&E of groundwater resources is an essential step in the
agreed procedures for the planning and design of the project (Annex 5). Water level monitonng
networks would also be established in the groundwater development areas to provide warning
of change, in paricula any progressive water table decline resulting from over-exploitation. In
addition, groundwater quality in the development areas would be continuously monitored,
particularly in areas adjacent to the coast where there may be the risk of water quality
ioration due to salt water intrusion of the aquifer system.
3.     With respect to water over-exploitation, even in East Java where groundwater has
been fairly highly developed for irrigation, there have been no indications of overdraft, or
reports of irngation withdrawals having adverse effects on village water supply wells.
Moreover the available environmental impact reports for irtigon projects indicate that leached
fertilizers and pesticides have not, as yet, caused any signifitcant deterioration in the quality of
shallow groundwater (or of surface water runoff).
QaUmPaQLDfthe lprje
4.     The project would increase agricultural production and increase incomes in rural areas
which are now almost endrely dependent on rainfall for crop cultivation and where poverty is
prevalent. Alleviation of hard core poverty is an important project objective and groundwater
irrigation development can be pardcularly effectively in this respect. The tubewell facilities
serve relatvely small, discrete areas and, therefore, can be located to serve targeted groups of
beneficiaries. A special project focus will be to enable full participation of the beneficiaries and
communities to be served in all phases of the development process (Annex 4).
5 .    The project would also provide formal arrangement for domestic water supplies to be
taken from the groundwater irrigation facilities: (a) when the wells are located in the close
vicinity of rural settlements; and (b) where there is a clearly expressed desire from potential
beneficiaries for such facilities. Water abstracted by tubewells is normally of good biological
quality for drinking purposes and provisions would be made for treatment by chlorination, as
required, to maintaJn the quality for the consumers



-128-                             Annex 15
Page, 2
6.     Provision of domestic water supplies from project wells would be promoted in all
situations where the groundwater abstraction for irrigation could impact adversely on existing -
viUage water supplies. Thus, the domestic water supply component of the project would have
both environmental and social benefits through provision of easily accessible drinking water
supplies that are: (a) safer to use than many of the existing supplies taken fiom open wells; and
(b) secure and sustainable with appropriate beneficiary management.
7.     The project has been formulated and provides resources to ensure that the needs of
women are properly addressed within the development process and that women are fully
involved in aU activities where they have a legitimate or beneficial role (Annex 12).
Envh=I Statu.s of th.e Priojc
8.     The proposed project is designated Category B under Bank environmental standards
because the environmental impacts would be localized and positive. Furthermore, any negative
impacts which may result from necessary planning and design decisions would be reversible
and easily manageable with the institutional resources to be made available through the project.
Preliminary identification of environmental issues has been undertaken as part of project
preparation. Potential environmental impact has been reviewed in detail with Government
dunng project preparation and appraisal process.
9.     Agreed action programs to safeguard environmental impact will be taken into account,
e,ither throu-gh a project component (i.e., provisions for water quantity and quality monitoring),
r thirough specific criteria and procedures under the project for environmental related activities.
These actions would apply to all subproject areas. As an additional safeguard, under the quality
control assurance obtained from Government, environmental assessments would be undertaken
in project areas, satisfactory to the Bank, to ensure that social equity and water quality
considerons are taken into account in the siting and planning and design of tubewell facilities.

Projec Slumsion Requiremets
1.   Proect S     jdsion The Bank staff requirements to supervise the project would be greater
than the resources (about 12 staff weeks per year) for a typical specific investment opemtion. The
supervision arrangements for the proposed project would be complex for the Bank because of the
geographical spread and the range of new initiatives being addressed. Thus, Govemment would
need to collect, analyze and provide to the Bank, all roudne implementation and performance data as
an input to the Bank's supervision process. In this respect, an important function of the consultant
services (Annex 13) would be to guide and assist Government in project supervision and to
strengthen the capability of individual agencies to monitor and evaluate project implementation.
However, even with the consultant's input, the project would benefit from a continuous Bank
supervision input and it may be more effective to supervise the project through RSI (Jakarta
Resident Mission). Recommended supervision requirements are summarized below.
2.   Su     sion Arrangements. The recommended arrangement would be for the Bane's
resident mission staff to supervise the project two times each year. The main mision would be
May/June each year and constitute the annual implementation review. During this mission,
proprate acdion would be taken to review and amend the annual work program for the following
issal year (to takce into account the budgetary timetable). The ond mission would take place
about mn November/December each year, and one important task would be to review the budgetary
request based on the work program agreed during the previous mission. Any needed day-to-day
follow-up actions would be undertaken by RSI staff. The first supervision mission (July 1993)
would require sufficient staff resources to cover the project start-up and to pay special attention to
the agricltural development and community pardcipation activities. It is thus recommended that the
irst supervision mission should, scheduling permitted, include headquarters staff who have been
involved in the projectpreparation and/or the appraisal missions. The Bank supervision resources
-would be as follows:
Staffing                   Staff Weeks
Year I    Years 2-6
(a)  Annual Implementation Review   Econonist/Agricultunist  3         3
(May/June each year)        Engineer                  3            2
Consultant                3           2
(b) Second Supervision Mission  Economist or Engineer     3            3
(Nov/Dec each year)         Consultant                2            2
(c)  General Supervision Activities   Task Manager        2            3
Total Staff Rqient(pe yea)                    16          15
3.   It is also recommended that thematic subjects, such as WID, groundwater n.- %itoring, etc. be
supervised on a multi-poect basis with additional resources, as required. This would enable generic
lessons learned to be applied on a subsector or country basis.
